Particular Conditionals with Possibility (Qad Yakun)
Enumeration of particular conditionals with possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of particular conditional propositions modalized with 'qad yakun' and distinguish them from unconditional disjunctions.

Core Idea
'Qad yakun' modalizes the entire disjunction, indicating possibility rather than necessity.

In this passage, the author systematically lists all possible particular conditional forms using the modal 'qad yakun' (possibly). These forms are foundational for constructing valid syllogisms with modalized disjunctive premises in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the modal prefix
Each line begins with 'qad yakun' (قد يكون), which modalizes the entire conditional as possible.
This is not part of the disjunction itself but a modal operator.
2
Identify the disjunction structure
After 'qad yakun' comes 'imma an yakun ... aw yakun ...' (either ... or ...). The two parts are disjuncts.
The disjunction is exclusive or inclusive depending on context; here it is treated as a compound proposition.
3
Examine the quantity terms
Each disjunct contains a quantified categorical proposition using terms like 'kull' (every), 'ba'd' (some), 'la shay'' (nothing), or 'la kull' (not every). The first part uses subject A and predicate B, the second uses subject J and predicate D.
These quantify over individuals or instances.
4
Understand the modal effect
'Qad yakun' means the disjunction holds possibly, but not necessarily. It contrasts with conditionals that are necessary or perpetual.
The author later mentions other modalities like 'dawam' (perpetuity) and 'itlaq 'amm' (absolute).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجزئيات فعلى الترتيب الذي مر ذكره كذلك:
قد يكون إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو يكون بعض ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون بعض أ ب أو يكون بعض ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون لا شيء من أ ب أو يكون لا كل ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون لا كل أ ب أو يكون لا شيء من ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون لا كل أ ب أو يكون لا كل ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو يكون لا شيء من ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو يكون لا كل ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون بعض أ ب أو يكون لا شيء من ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون بعض أ ب أو يكون لا كل ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون لا شيء من أ ب أو يكون كل ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون لا شيء من أ ب أو يكون بعض ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون لا كل أ ب أو يكون كل ج د
قد يكون إما أن يكون لا كل أ ب أو يكون بعض ج د
وأما السوالب فعلى هذا الترتيب أيضا:
ليس دائما إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو كل ج د
ليس دائما إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو بعض ج د
ليس دائما إما أن يكون بعض أ ب أو كل ج د
ليس دائما إما أن يكون بعض أ ب أو بعض ج د
ليس دائما إما أن يكون لا شيء من أ ب أو لا شيء من ج د
وعلى هذا إلى آخر ما مر من الجزئيات؛ هذا هو الكلام في الشرطيات بحسب الضرورة من الجهات. وقد يعتبر غيرها أيضا من الدوام والإطلاق العام.
As for the particulars, likewise in the arrangement that has been mentioned: Possibly, either every A is B or some J is D. Possibly, either some A is B or some J is D. Possibly, either nothing from A is B or not every J is D. Possibly, either not every A is B or nothing from J is D. Possibly, either not every A is B or not every J is D. Possibly, either every A is B or nothing from J is D. Possibly, either every A is B or not every J is D. Possibly, either some A is B or nothing from J is D. Possibly, either some A is B or not every J is D. Possibly, either nothing from A is B or every J is D. Possibly, either nothing from A is B or some J is D. Possibly, either not every A is B or every J is D. Possibly, either not every A is B or some J is D. As for the negatives, also in this arrangement: Not always, either every A is B or every J is D. Not always, either every A is B or some J is D. Not always, either some A is B or every J is D. Not always, either some A is B or some J is D. Not always, either nothing from A is B or nothing from J is D. And so on up to the end of what has passed of the particulars; this is the discussion on conditionals with respect to necessity among modalities. And others are also considered, like perpetuity and absolute general.

Commentary
This passage extends the enumeration to particular conditional propositions (جزئيات) using the modal 'قد يكون' (possibly). A dozen combinations are listed, covering all possible distributions of the quantities (كل, بعض, لا شيء, لا كل) in both parts. Then the negatives for these particular conditionals are given with 'ليس دائما' (not always), following a similar pattern. The author concludes that this covers conditionals with necessity (الضرورة) as one modality, and notes that other modalities like perpetuity (الدوام) and absolute general (الإطلاق العام) can also be considered, implying the same enumerative structure applies.

Negatives of Particular Conditionals (Laysa Daaim)
Enumeration of negative particular conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the negative forms of particular conditionals using 'laysa daaim' and contrast them with the affirmative possibility forms.

Core Idea
'Laysa daaim' negates the perpetual truth of the conditional, indicating it is not always the case.

After the affirmative forms, the author lists the negatives using 'laysa daaim' (not always). These are not the direct negations of possibility but rather negations of perpetual truth, which is a weaker negation.

Guided Path
1
Notice the negative prefix
Each negative begins with 'laysa daaim' (ليس دائما), meaning 'not always'.
This is a modal negation of perpetuity, not of possibility.
2
Compare with affirmative forms
The structure inside 'imma...aw' mirrors the affirmative forms (e.g., 'kull A B aw kull J D'). The difference is the modal prefix.
The quantity combinations are a subset of those in the affirmative list; not all forms are repeated.
3
Understand the modal semantics
'Laysa daaim' means the disjunction is not true at all times. It could be false sometimes, but not necessarily always false. This contrasts with 'qad yakun', which asserts possibility.
In modal logic, 'not always' is the negation of 'always' (daaim).
4
Recognize the enumeration pattern
The author lists five examples and then says 'and so on up to the end of what has passed of the particulars', implying that the negative forms follow the same exhaustive pattern as the affirmatives.
The full set would include all 13 forms with 'laysa daaim'.

Enumeration of Possible Conditional Propositions Under Necessity
Author enumerates all possible combinations of conditionals with necessity modality, then discusses other modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate all possible forms of conditional propositions under the modality of necessity using Arabic logical notation, and distinguish between implicative and accidental conditionals with their appropriate modalities.

Core Idea
The exhaustive list of conditional forms under necessity is the foundation for conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic.

In this passage, the author presents a complete enumeration of every possible combination of antecedent and consequent in a conditional proposition, under the modality of necessity. This is not just a list to memorize—it is a systematic tool that lets you see at a glance which forms are possible and which are not. Understanding this enumeration prepares you for the conditional syllogisms that build on these propositions, which are widely used in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the pattern of enumeration
The author lists all combinations of affirmation/negation and universality/particularity for the antecedent and consequent. Notice the use of 'أب' for 'every A is B' and 'ج د' for 'C is D'. The list follows a systematic order: first all affirmative conditionals (10 forms), then negative conditionals (5 forms).
The variables أ،ب،ج،د are placeholders; any terms can substitute.
2
Distinguish affirmative from negative conditionals
Affirmative conditionals begin with 'قد يكون إما أن يكون...' (It is possible that either...). Negative conditionals begin with 'ليس دائما إما أن يكون...' (It is not always that either...). Note that the negation applies to the whole conditional, not just one part.
The negative conditionals correspond to the affirmatives but are fewer because some combinations are impossible under necessity.
3
Understand the role of necessity
All listed forms are under the modality of necessity (ضرورة). The author then mentions other modalities: permanence (دوام), absolute generality (إطلاق عام), and possibility (إمكان). Necessity applies to both implicative (لزومية) and accidental (اتفاقية) conditionals, but absoluteness only to implicative, and possibility only to accidental.
This distinction is crucial: not every conditional is necessary; some are accidental.
4
Apply the notation to an Islamic example
Take a conditional from kalam: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a cause.' Assign 'أ' for 'the world' and 'ب' for 'temporally originated' in the antecedent; 'ج' for 'the world' and 'د' for 'has a cause' in the consequent. Match this to one of the listed forms.
This helps you see the abstract form behind real theological statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يكون إما أن يكون لا كل «أب» أو يكون لا شيء من «ج د» قد يكون إما أن يكون لا كل «أب» أو يكون لا كل «ج د» قد يكون إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو يكون لا شيء من «ج د» قد يكون إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو يكون لا كل «ج د» قد يكون إما أن يكون بعض «أب» أو يكون لا شيء من «ج د» قد يكون إما أن يكون بعض «أب» أو يكون لا كل «ج د» قد يكون إما أن يكون لا شيء من «أب» أو يكون كل «ج د» قد يكون إما أن يكون لا شيء من «أب» أو يكون بعض «ج د» قد يكون إما أن يكون لا كل «أب» أو يكون كل «ج د» قد يكون إما أن يكون لا كل «أب» أو يكون بعض «ج د». وأما السوالب فعلى هذا الترتيب أيضًا : ليس دائما إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «ج د» ليس دائما إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو بعض «ج د» ليس دائما إما أن يكون بعض «أب» أو كل «ج د» ليس دائما إما أن يكون بعض «أب» أو بعض «ج د» ليس دائما إما أن يكون لا شيء من «أب» أو لا شيء من «ج د» وعلى هذا إلى آخر ما مرَّ من الجزئيات؛ هذا هو الكلام في الشرطيات بحسب الضرورة من الجهات. وقد يعتبر غيرها أيضًا من الدوام والإطلاق العام. وغير ذلك. غير أنها وهي الجهات من جملة ما يكون اعتباره في المتصلات أولى، وذلك يعرف من بعد. وأما في الاتفاقيات وإن كان الأمر فيها كذلك فربَّما لم يوجد بل يمكن أن يوجد ؛ فإذن جهة الضرورة في اللزومية والاتفاقية، وجهة الإطلاق في اللزومية، وجهة الإمكان في الاتفاقية. فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
It is possible that it is either not every A is B or none of C is D; it is possible that it is either not every A is B or not every C is D; it is possible that it is either every A is B or none of C is D; it is possible that it is either every A is B or not every C is D; it is possible that it is either some A is B or none of C is D; it is possible that it is either some A is B or not every C is D; it is possible that it is either none of A is B or every C is D; it is possible that it is either none of A is B or some C is D; it is possible that it is either not every A is B or every C is D; it is possible that it is either not every A is B or some C is D. And as for the negatives, they are also in this order: It is not always the case that either every A is B or every C is D; it is not always the case that either every A is B or some C is D; it is not always the case that either some A is B or every C is D; it is not always the case that either some A is B or some C is D; it is not always the case that either none of A is B or none of C is D. And so on up to the end of the particulars that have passed. This is the discussion of conditionals according to necessity from the modalities. And other (modalities) are also considered, such as permanence and absolute generality. And other than that. However, they—the modalities—are among the things whose consideration in conjunctives is more appropriate, and that is known later. And as for accidental conditionals, even if the matter is similar, perhaps it does not exist but can exist. So the modality of necessity is in implicative and accidental (conditionals), the modality of absoluteness is in implicative (conditionals), and the modality of possibility is in accidental (conditionals). So consider what you have known, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of all possible forms of conditional propositions under the modality of necessity, followed by a discussion of other modalities. The author lists every combination of affirmation/negation and universality/particularity in the antecedent and consequent for both affirmative and negative conditionals. The list is systematic: first the affirmatives (ten possibilities), then the negatives (five possibilities, following the same pattern). This enumeration serves as a memorization anchor for the student. The author then notes that other modalities—such as permanence (dawam) and absolute generality (itlaq 'amm)—can also be considered, but the discussion of necessity is primary. He distinguishes between implicative (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals: necessity applies to both, absoluteness applies only to implicative, and possibility applies only to accidental. The student must grasp that the list given is exhaustive only for necessity; other modalities require separate treatment.

جهات
ضرورة
لزومية
اتفاقية
دوام
إطلاق عام
إمكان

Checkpoint

A) Only implicative (لزومية) conditionals
B) Only accidental (اتفاقية) conditionals
C) Both implicative and accidental conditionals
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
The exhaustive enumeration of conditional forms under necessity provides the raw material for conditional syllogisms, and the modality distinctions (necessity, absoluteness, possibility) must be correctly assigned to implicative and accidental conditionals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Understanding Deviant Conditionals (Al-Munharifat)
Author defines deviant conditionals and shows reduction to standard forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an ambiguous conditional expression and reduce it to its standard muttasil or munfasil form.

Core Idea
Deviant conditionals are ambiguous phrases reducible to clear conjunctive or disjunctive forms.

In this passage, the author introduces deviant conditionals—phrases whose logical form is unclear. By analyzing their wording, we can resolve the ambiguity into either a conditional (muttasil) or disjunctive (munfasil) expression. This skill is essential for correctly interpreting propositions in Islamic sciences where conditional statements carry legal or theological weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
Look for expressions like 'لا يكون أب فيكون ج د' that do not clearly signal a conditional or disjunctive. The formulation is 'sigha muwhima'—ambiguous.
Such phrases typically use negation and connecting words like 'ف' or 'أو' but in a way that leaves the logical relation unclear.
2
Determine possible interpretations
Check if the statement can be read as a conditional (muttasil) meaning 'If A then not C', and as a disjunctive (munfasil) meaning 'Either not A or not C'. Both interpretations are logically possible.
The author shows that the same ambiguous phrase can be reduced to two different standard forms.
3
Apply the reduction rule
Convert the ambiguous phrase into the standard forms given by the author. For the first example, one reduction yields 'إن كان أب فلا يكون ج د' (muttasil) and another yields 'إما أن لا يكون أب وإما أن لا يكون ج د' (munfasil).
The author notes that the disjunctive reduction is 'more appropriate for disjunctives' because it attaches without specification.
4
Recognize other indicators
Words like 'إلا' indicate universal restriction (hasr kulli). 'إنما' specifies that the consequent follows the antecedent. 'ما' indicates acceptance of the consequent given the antecedent, while 'لو' indicates acceptance of the non-existence of the consequent given the non-existence of the antecedent.
These indicators help determine the logical form without ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنحرفة من الشرطيات ما يكون مذكورًا بصيغة موهمة لا يفهم من تلك الصيغة أنه منها أو من غيرها كما في قولنا «لا يكون «أب» فيكون «ج د»» فإنه من المتصلات في قوة قولنا «إن كان «أب» فلا يكون «ج د»». ومن المنفصلات في قوة قولنا «إما أن لا يكون «أب» وإما أن لا يكون «ج د»». وكذلك قولنا «لا يكون «ج د» أو يكون «أب»» فإنه من المتصلات في قوة قولنا «كلما كان «ج د» فـ «أب»». ومن المنفصلات في قوة قولنا «إما أن لا يكون «ج د» وإما أن يكون «أب»»، وإنها هي الأولى بالمنفصلات لالتحاقها بها من غير تعيين. وقولنا «ليس يكون «ج د» إلا وأن يكون «أب»» فإنه الحصر الكلي، وكذلك قولنا «إنَّما يكون «أب» إذا كان «ج د»»، فإن كلمة «إنما» تدل على تخصيص التالي باتباعه للمقدم. وأما كلمة «الما» فقد مرَّ ذكرها من قبل أن دلالتها لا تكون مقصورة على اللزوم فقط بل تدل على تسلم التالي ووضعه لازما من تسلم المقدم ووضعه. وعلى عكسها كلمة «لو» فإنها تدل على تسلم عدم التالي ووضعه لازمًا من تسلم عدم المقدم. وعلى هذا في الغير من الألفاظ المستعملة لتعريف المعاني بحسب اللغات؛ فإن ما لا يكون البحث فيه معنويا كان التعليل فيه أولى على الخصوص فيما نحن فيه.
And the deviant among conditionals is that which is mentioned in an ambiguous formulation, so that it is not understood from that formulation whether it is from them (i.e., conjunctives) or from others. For example, our statement 'It is not that A is B, so it is that C is D' — it is from the conjunctives in the strength of our statement 'If A is B, then it is not that C is D.' And from the disjunctives in the strength of our statement 'Either it is not that A is B, or it is not that C is D.' Likewise our statement 'It is not that C is D, or it is that A is B' — it is from the conjunctives in the strength of our statement 'Whenever C is D, then A is B.' And from the disjunctives in the strength of our statement 'Either it is not that C is D, or it is that A is B,' and they (the disjunctives) are more appropriate for disjunctives due to their being attached to them without specification. And our statement 'It is not that C is D except that A is B' is universal restriction. Similarly our statement 'Only if C is D then A is B,' for the word 'innama' indicates specification of the consequent as following the antecedent. As for the word 'al-ma' (ما), its mention has passed before, that its indication is not limited to implication only, but it indicates the acceptance of the consequent and its placement as necessary from the acceptance of the antecedent and its placement. And its opposite is the word 'law' (لو), for it indicates acceptance of the non-existence of the consequent and its placement as necessary from the acceptance of the non-existence of the antecedent. And so on for other words used for defining meanings according to languages; for that which is not investigated meaningfully, the explanation is more appropriate, especially in what we are discussing.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage defines deviant conditionals (al-munharifat) as those expressed in an ambiguous form such that it is unclear whether they are conjunctive (muttasil) or disjunctive (munfasil). The author gives two examples. The first: 'لا يكون أب فيكون جد' can be interpreted as a conjunctive: 'If A is B, then it is not that C is D', or as a disjunctive: 'Either it is not that A is B, or it is not that C is D'. The second: 'لا يكون جد أو يكون أب' can be interpreted as a conjunctive: 'Whenever C is D, then A is B', or as a disjunctive: 'Either it is not that C is D, or A is B'. The author notes that the disjunctive interpretation is more appropriate for disjunctives because it attaches without specification. Then he discusses other expressions: 'ليس يكون جد إلا وأن يكون أب' indicates universal restriction (hasr kulli). 'إنما يكون أب إذا كان جد' uses 'innama' to specify that the consequent follows the antecedent. The word 'ما' (al-ma) indicates that the acceptance of the consequent is necessary given the acceptance of the antecedent, but not limited to implication. The word 'لو' (law) is opposite: acceptance of the nonexistence of the consequent follows from acceptance of the nonexistence of the antecedent. The student must understand how to reduce ambiguous conditionals to standard logical forms.

Key Terms
منحرفات
صيغة موهمة
حصر كلي

Deviant conditionals are not a special type but ambiguous expressions that must be reduced to clear conditional or disjunctive forms for logical analysis.

Identifying and Resolving Deviant Conditionals
Defines deviant conditionals and demonstrates equivalence transformations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a deviant conditional and convert it into its equivalent conjunctive and disjunctive forms.

Core Idea
A deviant conditional disguises its logical type; you must resolve its ambiguity.

Deviant conditionals (المنحرفات) are conditional propositions phrased in a way that obscures whether they are conjunctive (if-then) or disjunctive (either-or). This lesson will teach you to recognize such ambiguous forms and apply equivalence transformations to clarify their logical structure. Mastering this is essential for avoiding misinterpretation in logical arguments across Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous form
Look for conditionals expressed with phrases like 'لا يكون... فيكون' (it is not that... so that...) or 'لا يكون... أو يكون' (it is not that... or it is...). These are deviant because they do not clearly indicate a conjunctive or disjunctive relationship.
2
Recall the two possible equivalences
Every deviant conditional can be reinterpreted as both a conjunctive conditional (متصل) and a disjunctive conditional (منفصل). For example, 'لا يكون أ فيكون ب' is equivalent to 'إذا كان أ فلا يكون ب' (If A then not B) and 'إما لا يكون أ وإما لا يكون ب' (Either not A or not B).
3
Apply the transformation
Take the deviant form and rewrite it explicitly as a conjunctive conditional by identifying the antecedent and consequent. Then rewrite it as a disjunctive conditional by identifying the two alternatives.
4
Verify consistency
Check that the two equivalent forms are logically equivalent (e.g., by truth tables or known equivalences). This confirms the deviant form is correctly resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في المنحرفات
والمنحرفة من الشرطيات ما يكون مذكورًا بصيغة موهمة لا يفهم من تلك الصيغة أنه منها أو من غيرها كما في قولنا لا يكون «أب» فيكون «ج د»؛ فإنه من المتّصلات في قوة قولنا إن كان أب» فلا يكون جدا. ومن المنفصلات في قوة قولنا إما أن لا يكون «أب» وإما أن لا يكون «ج د». وكذلك قولنا لا يكون ج د» أو يكون «أب»؛ فإنه من المتّصلات في قوة قولنا كلما كان ج د» فـ «أب». ومن المنفصلات في قوة قولنا إما أن لا يكون «ج د» وإما أن يكون «أب»، وإنها هي الأولى بالمنفصلات لالتحاقها بها من غير تعيين. وقولنا ليس يكون (ج) (د) إلا وأن يكون (أ ب) فإنه الحصر الكلي، وكذلك قولنا إنَّما يكون «أب» إذا كان (ج) د»، فإن كلمة إنما تدل على تخصيص التالي باتباعه للمقدم. وأما كلمة «الما» فقد مرَّ ذكرها من قبل أن دلالتها لا تكون مقصورة على اللزوم فقط بل تدل على تسلم التالي ووضعه لازما من تسلم المقدم ووضعه. وعلى عكسها كلمة «لو» فإنها تدل على تسلم عدم التالي ووضعه لازمًا من تسلم عدم المقدم. وعلى هذا في الغير من الألفاظ المستعملة لتعريف المعاني بحسب اللغات؛ فإن ما لا يكون البحث فيه معنويا كان التعليل فيه أولى على الخصوص فيما نحن فيه.
Section on Deviant Conditionals. The deviant among conditionals is that which is mentioned in a misleading form, so that it is not understood from that form whether it is from them or from others, as in our saying 'It is not that 'ab' so that 'jd' becomes'; for it is from conjunctive conditionals in the force of our saying 'If 'ab' then it is not 'jd'. And it is from disjunctive conditionals in the force of our saying 'Either it is not 'ab' or it is not 'jd'. And similarly our saying 'It is not 'jd' or it is 'ab'; for it is from conjunctive conditionals in the force of our saying 'Whenever it is 'jd' then 'ab'. And it is from disjunctive conditionals in the force of our saying 'Either it is not 'jd' or it is 'ab', and they are more proper to disjunctives because they attach to them without determination. And our saying 'It is not that (j)(d) except that (a b) is' is total restriction, and similarly our saying 'It is only that 'ab' when (j) is d', for the word 'innama' indicates the specification of the consequent by following the antecedent. As for the word 'alma', its mention has passed before that its indication is not restricted to implication only, but rather indicates the acceptance of the consequent and its positing as necessary from the acceptance and positing of the antecedent. And opposite to it is the word 'law', for it indicates the acceptance of the negation of the consequent and its positing as necessary from the acceptance of the negation of the antecedent. And upon this in other words used to define meanings according to languages; for that whose investigation is not conceptual, explanation is more appropriate, especially in what concerns us.

Commentary
This passage defines deviant conditionals (المنحرفات) among conditional propositions. A deviant conditional is one expressed in a form that is misleading, such that one cannot immediately tell whether it is a conjunctive conditional (متصل) or a disjunctive conditional (منفصل). The author provides examples using the letters 'ab' and 'jd' as placeholders for propositions. For instance, the statement 'لا يكون «أب» فيكون «ج د»' (It is not that 'ab' so that 'jd' becomes) is shown to be equivalent to both a conjunctive conditional ('If 'ab' then it is not 'jd') and a disjunctive conditional ('Either it is not 'ab' or it is not 'jd'). Similarly, other forms are analyzed. The author then discusses the words 'إنما' (innama) and 'لو' (law), explaining their logical force: 'innama' specifies the consequent as following the antecedent, while 'law' indicates that the negation of the consequent follows from the negation of the antecedent. This discussion is crucial for correctly parsing conditionals in logical argumentation, especially when dealing with ambiguous phrasing in natural language.

The Logical Force of إنما and لو in Conditionals
Explains the logical force of 'innama' and 'law' in conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the logical implications of 'innama' and 'law' in conditional propositions and use them correctly in arguments.

Core Idea
'Innama' affirms consequent from antecedent; 'law' affirms negation of consequent from negation of antecedent.

The words إنما and لو are common in Arabic conditionals, but their logical force differs. This lesson explains how they function in conjunctive conditionals, building on the concept of deviant conditionals. Understanding these particles is crucial for analyzing Qur'anic and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'innama' in conditionals
'إنما' restricts the truth of the consequent to the occurrence of the antecedent. For example, 'إنما يكون كذا إذا كان كذا' means 'It is only the case that C when A occurs', which is equivalent to 'If A then C'.
2
Understand 'law' in conditionals
'لو' indicates that the absence of the consequent is necessitated by the absence of the antecedent. The passage says: 'لو تدل على تسلم عدم التالي ووضعه لازما من تسلم عدم المقدم,' i.e., it indicates acceptance of the negation of the consequent as necessary from acceptance of the negation of the antecedent. So it is like 'if not A then not C'.
3
Distinguish from other particles
Compare with 'إذا' which is a straightforward conditional, and 'لما' which was mentioned earlier. The passage notes that 'لما' indicates acceptance of the consequent from acceptance of the antecedent, opposite to 'لو'.

Source Text

Definition and Divisions of Contradiction
Definition and initial divisions of contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus, differentia, and subdivisions of logical contradiction (tanaqud) as defined in this passage.

Core Idea
Contradiction is difference by affirmation/negation that necessarily yields one true and one false.

The author begins by recalling that contradiction is a type of opposition. He then defines it precisely: it is the difference between two propositions by affirmation and negation in such a way that one must be true and the other false. This definition gives us a proximate genus (difference by affirmation/negation) and a differentia (the truth requirement). The passage goes on to break down types of difference and mutual exclusion, clarifying what counts as genuine contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence that defines contradiction. Identify the proximate genus (difference by negation and affirmation) and the differentia (the remainder: requiring one true and one false).
The proximate genus is explicitly stated; the differentia is implied in the rest of the definition.
2
Identify the remote genus
The author mentions 'absolute difference' as a remote genus. See how it branches into difference by negation/affirmation and difference not by negation/affirmation.
This helps situate contradiction within a broader classification.
3
Distinguish mutually exclusive vs non-mutually exclusive
Within difference by negation/affirmation, note the division: some pairs are mutually exclusive (e.g., moving/not moving) and some are not (e.g., moving/not stationary). Understand why the latter are not contradictions.
Mutual exclusion is a necessary but not sufficient condition for contradiction.
4
Distinguish essential vs accidental mutual exclusion
Mutual exclusion itself divides into essential (e.g., human/not human) and accidental (e.g., human/not animal). Only essential mutual exclusion yields genuine contradiction.
Accidental mutual exclusion depends on external relations; human necessarily implies animal, so 'not animal' is implicitly contradictory to human.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع: في التناقض والعكس وما يتعلّق بهما في الحمليات والشرطيات وفي هذا النوع من المباحث: أما الأول منها فذلك في التناقض؛ قد مرَّ من قبل أن التناقض نوع من التقابل. وإنه أي التناقض عبارة عن اختلاف قضيتين بالسلب والإيجاب على وجه يقتضى لذاته أن تكون إحداهما صادقة والأخرى كاذبة. فالاختلاف بالسلب والإيجاب هو جنسه القريب، والباقي هو فصله. والاختلاف مطلقًا هو الجنس البعيد ينقسم إلى [أ] الاختلاف بينهما بالسلب والإيجاب؛ [ب] وإلى الاختلاف بينهما لا بالسلب والإيجاب. والاختلاف بينهما بالسلب والإيجاب ينقسم [أ] إلى ما يكون بين المتنافيين كما في قولنا هذا الشيء متحرّك، هذا الشيء ليس بمتحرك ; [ب] وإلى ما لا يكون بين المتنافيين كما في هذا متحرّك، هذا ليس بساكن والتنافي أيضًا ينقسم [1] إلى ما يكون بالذات كما في قولنا هذا إنسان هذا ليس بإنسان; [ب] وإلى ما لا يكون بالذات كما في قولنا هذا إنسان، هذا ليس بحيوان. فإن التنافي بينهما باعتبار أن يكون الإنسان حيوانا بالضرورة.
ثم الصادقة من القضيتين المتناقضتين قد تكون معينة وقد لا تكون؛ وذلك لأن الحكم بالشيء على الشيء لا يخلو من أن يكون في مادة الوجوب أو مادة الامتناع أو في مادة الإمكان على ما عرف فلو كان الحكم بينهما في مادة الوجوب لكانت الصادقة منهما هي الموجبة كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان. وإن كان في مادة الامتناع كانت الصادقة هي السالبة كما في قولنا كل إنسان صهَّال، بعض الإنسان ليس بصهال، وأما الكاذبة فيهما فعلى عكس الموجبة. وإن كان في مادة الإمكان فالصادقة منهما لا تكون متعينة، والكاذبة كذلك إلا في الممكن الماضي والحاضر. فأما في المستقبل كما يقال زيد يمشي غدًا، زيد لا يمشي غدًا، فلا يتعين أحدهما البتة؛ إذ لو تعين أحد الطرفين منهما للوقوع لخرج عن الإمكان ولبطل الاختيار لكنه بالنظر إلى ذاته. فأما بالنظر إلى السبب فالتعين ليس إلا، وأما الكلام في الاختيار فذلك من المباحث الكلامية تعرف من الكتب المنسوبة إلى علم الكلام.
ثم التناقض على الحد الذي مرَّ ذكره لا يتحقق بين القضيتين إلا باتحادهما في المحكوم عليه وبه والشرط والجزء والكل والمكان والزمان والإضافة والقوة والفعل. فإنهما إذا اختلفتا في أمر من هذه الأمور لم يقيما الصدق والكذب أصلا كما إذا اختلفتا في المحكوم عليه فيقال مثلا الجمل يذبح ويراد به الجمل في الأرض، ويقال الجمل لا يذبح ويراد به الجمل في السماء وهو يذبح الجمل : فإنهما يصدقان معًا. وكذلك في المحكوم به كما يقال زيد عدل ویراد به العادل وزيد ليس بعدل ويراد به العدالة، فإنهما يصدقان أيضًا. وكذلك في الشرط كما يقال الكاتب متحرك ويراد به ما دام كاتبًا، الكاتب ليس بمتحرك ويراد به الشخص الذي يوصف بوصف كونه كاتبًا؛ إذ يمكن اجتماعهما في الصدق وكذلك في الجزء كما يقال جزء حبشي أسود ويراد به الوجه، جزء حبشي ليس بأسود ويراد به العين مثلا. وكذلك في الكل كما يقال كل حبشي أسود ویراد به الظاهر من البدن، كل حبشي ليس بأسود؛ ويراد به الظاهر والباطن من البدن . وكذلك في المكان كما يقال زيد متحرك ويراد به على الأرض زيد ليس بمتحرك ; ويراد به على السماء. وكذلك في الزمان كما يقال زید متدین ویراد به بعد التوبة ,وتقائها زيد ليس بمتدین ویراد به بعد التوبة دون تقائها. وكذلك في الإضافة كما يقال زيد مطيع ويراد به أنه مطيع للخالق، زيد ليس بمطيع ويراد به أنه ليس بمطيع للخلق. وكذلك في القوة كما يقال زيد عالم ويراد به أنه عالم بالقوة، زيد ليس بعالم ويراد به أنه ليس بعالم بالفعل. وعلى هذا في الفعل، فإنهما من جملة ما لا تناقض بينهما حينئذ.
The fourth type: On contradiction, conversion, and what relates to them in categorical and conditional [propositions]. As for the first of these, it is concerning contradiction. It has passed before that contradiction is a type of opposition. And it, meaning contradiction, is an expression of the difference of two propositions by negation and affirmation in a way that requires by itself that one of them be true and the other false. So difference by negation and affirmation is its proximate genus, and the remainder is its differentia. And absolute difference is the remote genus; it divides into (a) difference between them by negation and affirmation, and (b) difference between them not by negation and affirmation. And difference between them by negation and affirmation divides into (a) what is between two mutually exclusive [propositions] as in our saying 'this thing is moving, this thing is not moving', and (b) what is not between two mutually exclusive [propositions] as in 'this is moving, this is not stationary'. And mutual exclusion also divides into (1) what is essential as in our saying 'this is a human, this is not a human', and (b) what is not essential as in our saying 'this is a human, this is not an animal'. For the mutual exclusion between them is by virtue of the fact that a human is necessarily an animal. Then the true [proposition] of the two contradictory propositions may be determined and may not be. That is because the judgement of a thing about a thing is not free from being in the matter of necessity, or the matter of impossibility, or the matter of possibility, as was known. So if the judgement between them is in the matter of necessity, then the true of the two is the affirmative, as in our saying 'every human is an animal, some human is not an animal'. And if it is in the matter of impossibility, then the true is the negative, as in our saying 'every human is a neighing animal, some human is not a neighing animal'. And as for the false in them, it is the opposite of the true. And if it is in the matter of possibility, then the true of them is not determined, and likewise the false, except in the possible past and present. As for the future, as is said 'Zaid will walk tomorrow, Zaid will not walk tomorrow', then neither of them is determined at all; for if one of the two sides were determined to occur, it would exit from possibility and free choice would be nullified, but it is with respect to its essence. As for with respect to the cause, determination is inevitable. And as for speech concerning free choice, that is among the theological inquiries known from books attributed to the science of theology. Then, contradiction, according to the definition that has passed, is not realized between two propositions except by their unity in the subject, the predicate, the condition, the part, the whole, the place, the time, the relation, the potentiality, and the actuality. For if they differ in any of these matters, they do not establish truth and falsity at all, as when they differ in the subject, so for example one says 'the camel is slaughtered' meaning the camel on earth, and one says 'the camel is not slaughtered' meaning the camel in the sky, and he is slaughtering the camel: then they are both true together. Similarly in the predicate, as is said 'Zaid is just' meaning the just person, and 'Zaid is not just' meaning justice, they are both true also. Similarly in the condition, as is said 'the writer is moving' meaning as long as he is writing, 'the writer is not moving' meaning the person described as being a writer; for they can both be true. Similarly in the part, as is said 'part of an Abyssinian is black' meaning the face, 'part of an Abyssinian is not black' meaning the eye for example. Similarly in the whole, as is said 'every Abyssinian is black' meaning the apparent part of the body, 'every Abyssinian is not black' meaning the apparent and inner part of the body. Similarly in the place, as is said 'Zaid is moving' meaning on the earth, 'Zaid is not moving' meaning in the sky. Similarly in the time, as is said 'Zaid is pious' meaning after repentance and mindfulness, 'Zaid is not pious' meaning after repentance without mindfulness. Similarly in the relation, as is said 'Zaid is obedient' meaning he is obedient to the Creator, 'Zaid is not obedient' meaning he is not obedient to creatures. Similarly in potentiality, as is said 'Zaid is knowledgeable' meaning he is knowledgeable potentially, 'Zaid is not knowledgeable' meaning he is not knowledgeable actually. And likewise in actuality, for they are among what has no contradiction between them at that point.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage defines logical contradiction (tanaqud) and elaborates its conditions. It begins by recalling that contradiction is a type of opposition (taqabul). The definition: contradiction is the difference between two propositions by affirmation and negation in a manner that necessarily entails that one is true and the other false. The author then analyzes this definition: the proximate genus is 'difference by affirmation and negation', and the differentia is the remainder of the definition (the requirement of truth/falsity). The remote genus is 'absolute difference', which branches into difference by affirmation/negation and difference otherwise. Difference by affirmation/negation itself splits into difference between mutually exclusive propositions (e.g., 'this is moving' vs 'this is not moving') and difference not between mutually exclusive ones (e.g., 'this is moving' vs 'this is not stationary' – these are not direct contradictories because 'not stationary' is not equivalent to 'not moving' in the sense of negation). Further, mutual exclusion (tanafi) divides into essential (e.g., 'this is a human' vs 'this is not a human') and accidental (e.g., 'this is a human' vs 'this is not an animal') – the latter are not pure contradictories because 'human' necessarily implies 'animal'.
Next, the author discusses when the truth of one of the two contradictories is determined. The matter (madda) of the judgement – whether necessary, impossible, or possible – affects this. In necessary matter (e.g., 'every human is an animal'), the affirmative is true and the negative false. In impossible matter (e.g., 'every human is a neighing animal'), the negative is true and affirmative false. In possible matter, truth is not determined except for past and present possibles (which have occurred or not). For future contingents (e.g., 'Zaid will walk tomorrow'), neither is determinately true or false in itself, because if one side were fixed, possibility would be lost, and free choice would be negated. However, the author notes that with respect to the cause, determination does exist, but discussion of free will belongs to theology.
Finally, the author lists ten conditions for genuine contradiction: the two propositions must agree in subject (mahkum 'alayh), predicate (mahkum bihi), condition (shart), part (juz'), whole (kull), place (makan), time (zaman), relation (idafa), potentiality (quwwa), and actuality (fi'l). If they differ in any of these, they can both be true simultaneously. Numerous examples illustrate each condition, such as: subject (camel on earth vs camel in sky), predicate (Zaid as just person vs Zaid as justice), condition (writer moving as long as writing vs writer as a person), part (face of Abyssinian black vs eye not black), whole (apparent body black vs apparent and inner body not black), place (Zaid moving on earth vs on sky), time (Zaid pious after repentance and mindfulness vs after repentance without mindfulness), relation (obedient to Creator vs not obedient to creatures), potentiality (knowledgeable potentially vs actually), and actuality (analogous to potentiality). The key lesson: contradiction only occurs when the two propositions are completely identical in all these respects; otherwise, they are not truly contradictory.

The Role of Matter (Madda) in Contradiction
Matter and truth determination in contradictories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which of two contradictory propositions is true based on the matter (madda) of the judgement, including the special case of future contingents.

Core Idea
In necessary matter the affirmative is true; in impossible matter the negative is true; in possible matter truth is undetermined for the future.

The author addresses an important point: given a pair of contradictories, is the truth always determined? He explains that it depends on the 'matter' (modal status) of the judgement. For necessary and impossible matters, truth is fixed; for possible matters, truth may not be determined, especially for future events, preserving free will. This lesson explores that discussion and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three modal matters
The author refers to necessity (wujub), impossibility (imtina'), and possibility (imkan). Understand each as a modal property of the relation between subject and predicate.
These were defined earlier in the text.
2
Apply to necessary matter
In necessary matter (e.g., 'every human is an animal'), the affirmative is true and the negative false. Verify why: the predicate is necessarily true of the subject.
Example: 'Every human is an animal' vs 'Some human is not an animal'.
3
Apply to impossible matter
In impossible matter (e.g., 'every human is a neighing animal'), the negative is true and the affirmative false. The predicate cannot be true of the subject.
Example: 'Every human is a neighing animal' vs 'Some human is not a neighing animal'.
4
Apply to possible matter
For possible matter (e.g., 'Zaid will walk tomorrow'), truth is not determined in itself because if one side were fixed, possibility would be lost and free will negated. Note the exception for past and present possibles, which are effectively determined.
The author also notes that with respect to the cause, determination exists, but this is a theological matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع: في التناقض والعكس وما يتعلّق بهما في الحمليات والشرطيات وفي هذا النوع من المباحث: أما الأول منها فذلك في التناقض؛ قد مرَّ من قبل أن التناقض نوع من التقابل. وإنه أي التناقض عبارة عن اختلاف قضيتين بالسلب والإيجاب على وجه يقتضى لذاته أن تكون إحداهما صادقة والأخرى كاذبة. فالاختلاف بالسلب والإيجاب هو جنسه القريب، والباقي هو فصله. والاختلاف مطلقًا هو الجنس البعيد ينقسم إلى [أ] الاختلاف بينهما بالسلب والإيجاب؛ [ب] وإلى الاختلاف بينهما لا بالسلب والإيجاب. والاختلاف بينهما بالسلب والإيجاب ينقسم [أ] إلى ما يكون بين المتنافيين كما في قولنا هذا الشيء متحرّك، هذا الشيء ليس بمتحرك ; [ب] وإلى ما لا يكون بين المتنافيين كما في هذا متحرّك، هذا ليس بساكن والتنافي أيضًا ينقسم [1] إلى ما يكون بالذات كما في قولنا هذا إنسان هذا ليس بإنسان; [ب] وإلى ما لا يكون بالذات كما في قولنا هذا إنسان، هذا ليس بحيوان. فإن التنافي بينهما باعتبار أن يكون الإنسان حيوانا بالضرورة.
ثم الصادقة من القضيتين المتناقضتين قد تكون معينة وقد لا تكون؛ وذلك لأن الحكم بالشيء على الشيء لا يخلو من أن يكون في مادة الوجوب أو مادة الامتناع أو في مادة الإمكان على ما عرف فلو كان الحكم بينهما في مادة الوجوب لكانت الصادقة منهما هي الموجبة كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان. وإن كان في مادة الامتناع كانت الصادقة هي السالبة كما في قولنا كل إنسان صهَّال، بعض الإنسان ليس بصهال، وأما الكاذبة فيهما فعلى عكس الموجبة. وإن كان في مادة الإمكان فالصادقة منهما لا تكون متعينة، والكاذبة كذلك إلا في الممكن الماضي والحاضر. فأما في المستقبل كما يقال زيد يمشي غدًا، زيد لا يمشي غدًا، فلا يتعين أحدهما البتة؛ إذ لو تعين أحد الطرفين منهما للوقوع لخرج عن الإمكان ولبطل الاختيار لكنه بالنظر إلى ذاته. فأما بالنظر إلى السبب فالتعين ليس إلا، وأما الكلام في الاختيار فذلك من المباحث الكلامية تعرف من الكتب المنسوبة إلى علم الكلام.
ثم التناقض على الحد الذي مرَّ ذكره لا يتحقق بين القضيتين إلا باتحادهما في المحكوم عليه وبه والشرط والجزء والكل والمكان والزمان والإضافة والقوة والفعل. فإنهما إذا اختلفتا في أمر من هذه الأمور لم يقيما الصدق والكذب أصلا كما إذا اختلفتا في المحكوم عليه فيقال مثلا الجمل يذبح ويراد به الجمل في الأرض، ويقال الجمل لا يذبح ويراد به الجمل في السماء وهو يذبح الجمل : فإنهما يصدقان معًا. وكذلك في المحكوم به كما يقال زيد عدل ویراد به العادل وزيد ليس بعدل ويراد به العدالة، فإنهما يصدقان أيضًا. وكذلك في الشرط كما يقال الكاتب متحرك ويراد به ما دام كاتبًا، الكاتب ليس بمتحرك ويراد به الشخص الذي يوصف بوصف كونه كاتبًا؛ إذ يمكن اجتماعهما في الصدق وكذلك في الجزء كما يقال جزء حبشي أسود ويراد به الوجه، جزء حبشي ليس بأسود ويراد به العين مثلا. وكذلك في الكل كما يقال كل حبشي أسود ویراد به الظاهر من البدن، كل حبشي ليس بأسود؛ ويراد به الظاهر والباطن من البدن . وكذلك في المكان كما يقال زيد متحرك ويراد به على الأرض زيد ليس بمتحرك ; ويراد به على السماء. وكذلك في الزمان كما يقال زید متدین ویراد به بعد التوبة ,وتقائها زيد ليس بمتدین ویراد به بعد التوبة دون تقائها. وكذلك في الإضافة كما يقال زيد مطيع ويراد به أنه مطيع للخالق، زيد ليس بمطيع ويراد به أنه ليس بمطيع للخلق. وكذلك في القوة كما يقال زيد عالم ويراد به أنه عالم بالقوة، زيد ليس بعالم ويراد به أنه ليس بعالم بالفعل. وعلى هذا في الفعل، فإنهما من جملة ما لا تناقض بينهما حينئذ.
The fourth type: On contradiction, conversion, and what relates to them in categorical and conditional [propositions]. As for the first of these, it is concerning contradiction. Commentary
This passage defines logical contradiction (tanaqud) and elaborates its conditions. It begins by recalling that contradiction is a type of opposition (taqabul). The definition: contradiction is the difference between two propositions by affirmation and negation in a manner that necessarily entails that one is true and the other false. The author then analyzes this definition: the proximate genus is 'difference by affirmation and negation', and the differentia is the remainder of the definition (the requirement of truth/falsity). The remote genus is 'absolute difference', which branches into difference by affirmation/negation and difference otherwise. Difference by affirmation/negation itself splits into difference between mutually exclusive propositions (e.g., 'this is moving' vs 'this is not moving') and difference not between mutually exclusive ones (e.g., 'this is moving' vs 'this is not stationary' – these are not direct contradictories because 'not stationary' is not equivalent to 'not moving' in the sense of negation). Further, mutual exclusion (tanafi) divides into essential (e.g., 'this is a human' vs 'this is not a human') and accidental (e.g., 'this is a human' vs 'this is not an animal') – the latter are not pure contradictories because 'human' necessarily implies 'animal'.
Next, the author discusses when the truth of one of the two contradictories is determined. The matter (madda) of the judgement – whether necessary, impossible, or possible – affects this. In necessary matter (e.g., 'every human is an animal'), the affirmative is true and the negative false. In impossible matter (e.g., 'every human is a neighing animal'), the negative is true and affirmative false. In possible matter, truth is not determined except for past and present possibles (which have occurred or not). For future contingents (e.g., 'Zaid will walk tomorrow'), neither is determinately true or false in itself, because if one side were fixed, possibility would be lost, and free choice would be negated. However, the author notes that with respect to the cause, determination does exist, but discussion of free will belongs to theology.
Finally, the author lists ten conditions for genuine contradiction: the two propositions must agree in subject (mahkum 'alayh), predicate (mahkum bihi), condition (shart), part (juz'), whole (kull), place (makan), time (zaman), relation (idafa), potentiality (quwwa), and actuality (fi'l). If they differ in any of these, they can both be true simultaneously. Numerous examples illustrate each condition, such as: subject (camel on earth vs camel in sky), predicate (Zaid as just person vs Zaid as justice), condition (writer moving as long as writing vs writer as a person), part (face of Abyssinian black vs eye not black), whole (apparent body black vs apparent and inner body not black), place (Zaid moving on earth vs on sky), time (Zaid pious after repentance and mindfulness vs after repentance without mindfulness), relation (obedient to Creator vs not obedient to creatures), potentiality (knowledgeable potentially vs actually), and actuality (analogous to potentiality). The key lesson: contradiction only occurs when the two propositions are completely identical in all these respects; otherwise, they are not truly contradictory.

The Ten Conditions for Genuine Contradiction
Ten conditions for genuine contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten conditions (subject, predicate, condition, part, whole, place, time, relation, potentiality, actuality) and test whether any pair of propositions is truly contradictory by checking these conditions.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires full identity in ten respects; otherwise, both propositions can be true.

The author concludes by listing the requirements for two propositions to be genuinely contradictory. If they differ in subject, predicate, condition, part, whole, place, time, relation, potentiality, or actuality, they are not truly contradictory. He provides vivid examples for each, showing how apparent contradictions dissolve when the differences are recognized. This lesson will help you diagnose false contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Unity of subject (mahkum 'alayh)
The subject must be the same entity in both propositions. The author gives the example of 'camel on earth' vs 'camel in sky' – they differ in location and thus are not contradictory.
2
Unity of predicate (mahkum bihi)
The predicate must be the same attribute. Example: 'Zaid is just' (meaning the just person) vs 'Zaid is not just' (meaning justice). These can both be true if Zaid is just but not justice itself.
3
Unity of condition (shart)
Both propositions must refer to the same condition. Example: 'the writer is moving' (as long as he writes) vs 'the writer is not moving' (as a person). They can be simultaneously true.
4
Unity of part and whole (juz' and kull)
If one refers to a part and the other to the whole, they are not contradictory. Example: 'part of an Abyssinian is black' (face) vs 'part of an Abyssinian is not black' (eye). Similarly for whole vs part.
5
Remaining six: place, time, relation, potentiality, actuality
Each specifies a different dimension: place (earth vs sky), time (after repentance with or without mindfulness), relation (obedient to Creator vs not to creatures), potentiality (knowledgeable potentially vs actually), and actuality (similar to potentiality). Ensure both propositions align in each dimension.
These conditions prevent false contradictions due to implicit differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع: في التناقض والعكس وما يتعلّق بهما في الحمليات والشرطيات وفي هذا النوع من المباحث: أما الأول منها فذلك في التناقض؛ قد مرَّ من قبل أن التناقض نوع من التقابل. وإنه أي التناقض عبارة عن اختلاف قضيتين بالسلب والإيجاب على وجه يقتضى لذاته أن تكون إحداهما صادقة والأخرى كاذبة. فالاختلاف بالسلب والإيجاب هو جنسه القريب، والباقي هو فصله. والاختلاف مطلقًا هو الجنس البعيد ينقسم إلى [أ] الاختلاف بينهما بالسلب والإيجاب؛ [ب] وإلى الاختلاف بينهما لا بالسلب والإيجاب. والاختلاف بينهما بالسلب والإيجاب ينقسم [أ] إلى ما يكون بين المتنافيين كما في قولنا هذا الشيء متحرّك، هذا الشيء ليس بمتحرك ; [ب] وإلى ما لا يكون بين المتنافيين كما في هذا متحرّك، هذا ليس بساكن والتنافي أيضًا ينقسم [1] إلى ما يكون بالذات كما في قولنا هذا إنسان هذا ليس بإنسان; [ب] وإلى ما لا يكون بالذات كما في قولنا هذا إنسان، هذا ليس بحيوان. فإن التنافي بينهما باعتبار أن يكون الإنسان حيوانا بالضرورة.
ثم الصادقة من القضيتين المتناقضتين قد تكون معينة وقد لا تكون؛ وذلك لأن الحكم بالشيء على الشيء لا يخلو من أن يكون في مادة الوجوب أو مادة الامتناع أو في مادة الإمكان على ما عرف فلو كان الحكم بينهما في مادة الوجوب لكانت الصادقة منهما هي الموجبة كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان. وإن كان في مادة الامتناع كانت الصادقة هي السالبة كما في قولنا كل إنسان صهَّال، بعض الإنسان ليس بصهال، وأما الكاذبة فيهما فعلى عكس الموجبة. وإن كان في مادة الإمكان فالصادقة منهما لا تكون متعينة، والكاذبة كذلك إلا في الممكن الماضي والحاضر. فأما في المستقبل كما يقال زيد يمشي غدًا، زيد لا يمشي غدًا، فلا يتعين أحدهما البتة؛ إذ لو تعين أحد الطرفين منهما للوقوع لخرج عن الإمكان ولبطل الاختيار لكنه بالنظر إلى ذاته. فأما بالنظر إلى السبب فالتعين ليس إلا، وأما الكلام في الاختيار فذلك من المباحث الكلامية تعرف من الكتب المنسوبة إلى علم الكلام.
ثم التناقض على الحد الذي مرَّ ذكره لا يتحقق بين القضيتين إلا باتحادهما في المحكوم عليه وبه والشرط والجزء والكل والمكان والزمان والإضافة والقوة والفعل. فإنهما إذا اختلفتا في أمر من هذه الأمور لم يقيما الصدق والكذب أصلا كما إذا اختلفتا في المحكوم عليه فيقال مثلا الجمل يذبح ويراد به الجمل في الأرض، ويقال الجمل لا يذبح ويراد به الجمل في السماء وهو يذبح الجمل : فإنهما يصدقان معًا. وكذلك في المحكوم به كما يقال زيد عدل ویراد به العادل وزيد ليس بعدل ويراد به العدالة، فإنهما يصدقان أيضًا. وكذلك في الشرط كما يقال الكاتب متحرك ويراد به ما دام كاتبًا، الكاتب ليس بمتحرك ويراد به الشخص الذي يوصف بوصف كونه كاتبًا؛ إذ يمكن اجتماعهما في الصدق وكذلك في الجزء كما يقال جزء حبشي أسود ويراد به الوجه، جزء حبشي ليس بأسود ويراد به العين مثلا. وكذلك في الكل كما يقال كل حبشي أسود ویراد به الظاهر من البدن، كل حبشي ليس بأسود؛ ويراد به الظاهر والباطن من البدن . وكذلك في المكان كما يقال زيد متحرك ويراد به على الأرض زيد ليس بمتحرك ; ويراد به على السماء. وكذلك في الزمان كما يقال زید متدین ویراد به بعد التوبة ,وتقائها زيد ليس بمتدین ویراد به بعد التوبة دون تقائها. وكذلك في الإضافة كما يقال زيد مطيع ويراد به أنه مطيع للخالق، زيد ليس بمطيع ويراد به أنه ليس بمطيع للخلق. وكذلك في القوة كما يقال زيد عالم ويراد به أنه عالم بالقوة، زيد ليس بعالم ويراد به أنه ليس بعالم بالفعل. وعلى هذا في الفعل، فإنهما من جملة ما لا تناقض بينهما حينئذ.
The fourth type: On contradiction, conversion, and what relates to them in categorical and conditional [propositions]. As for the first of these, it is concerning contradiction. It has passed before that contradiction is a type of opposition. And it, meaning contradiction, is an expression of the difference of two propositions by negation and affirmation in a way that requires by itself that one of them be true and the other false. So difference by negation and affirmation is its proximate genus, and the remainder is its differentia. And absolute difference is the remote genus; it divides into (a) difference between them by negation and affirmation, and (b) difference between them not by negation and affirmation. And difference between them by negation and affirmation divides into (a) what is between two mutually exclusive [propositions] as in our saying 'this thing is moving, this thing is not moving', and (b) what is not between two mutually exclusive [propositions] as in 'this is moving, this is not stationary'. And mutual exclusion also divides into (1) what is essential as in our saying 'this is a human, this is not a human', and (b) what is not essential as in our saying 'this is a human, this is not an animal'. For the mutual exclusion between them is by virtue of the fact that a human is necessarily an animal. Then the true [proposition] of the two contradictory propositions may be determined and may not be. That is because the judgement of a thing about a thing is not free from being in the matter of necessity, or the matter of impossibility, or the matter of possibility, as was known. So if the judgement between them is in the matter of necessity, then the true of the two is the affirmative, as in our saying 'every human is an animal, some human is not an animal'. And if it is in the matter of impossibility, then the true is the negative, as in our saying 'every human is a neighing animal, some human is not a neighing animal'. And as for the false in them, it is the opposite of the true. And if it is in the matter of possibility, then the true of them is not determined, and likewise the false, except in the possible past and present. As for the future, as is said 'Zaid will walk tomorrow, Zaid will not walk tomorrow', then neither of them is determined at all; for if one of the two sides were determined to occur, it would exit from possibility and free choice would be nullified, but it is with respect to its essence. As for with respect to the cause, determination is inevitable. And as for speech concerning free choice, that is among the theological inquiries known from books attributed to the science of theology. Then, contradiction, according to the definition that has passed, is not realized between two propositions except by their unity in the subject, the predicate, the condition, the part, the whole, the place, the time, the relation, the potentiality, and the actuality. For if they differ in any of these matters, they do not establish truth and falsity at all, as when they differ in the subject, so for example one says 'the camel is slaughtered' meaning the camel on earth, and one says 'the camel is not slaughtered' meaning the camel in the sky, and he is slaughtering the camel: then they are both true together. Similarly in the predicate, as is said 'Zaid is just' meaning the just person, and 'Zaid is not just' meaning justice, they are both true also. Similarly in the condition, as is said 'the writer is moving' meaning as long as he is writing, 'the writer is not moving' meaning the person described as being a writer; for they can both be true. Similarly in the part, as is said 'part of an Abyssinian is black' meaning the face, 'part of an Abyssinian is not black' meaning the eye for example. Similarly in the whole, as is said 'every Abyssinian is black' meaning the apparent part of the body, 'every Abyssinian is not black' meaning the apparent and inner part of the body. Similarly in the place, as is said 'Zaid is moving' meaning on the earth, 'Zaid is not moving' meaning in the sky. Similarly in the time, as is said 'Zaid is pious' meaning after repentance and mindfulness, 'Zaid is not pious' meaning after repentance without mindfulness. Similarly in the relation, as is said 'Zaid is obedient' meaning he is obedient to the Creator, 'Zaid is not obedient' meaning he is not obedient to creatures. Similarly in potentiality, as is said 'Zaid is knowledgeable' meaning he is knowledgeable potentially, 'Zaid is not knowledgeable' meaning he is not knowledgeable actually. And likewise in actuality, for they are among what has no contradiction between them at that point.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage defines logical contradiction (tanaqud) and elaborates its conditions. It begins by recalling that contradiction is a type of opposition (taqabul). The definition: contradiction is the difference between two propositions by affirmation and negation in a manner that necessarily entails that one is true and the other false. The author then analyzes this definition: the proximate genus is 'difference by affirmation and negation', and the differentia is the remainder of the definition (the requirement of truth/falsity). The remote genus is 'absolute difference', which branches into difference by affirmation/negation and difference otherwise. Difference by affirmation/negation itself splits into difference between mutually exclusive propositions (e.g., 'this is moving' vs 'this is not moving') and difference not between mutually exclusive ones (e.g., 'this is moving' vs 'this is not stationary' – these are not direct contradictories because 'not stationary' is not equivalent to 'not moving' in the sense of negation). Further, mutual exclusion (tanafi) divides into essential (e.g., 'this is a human' vs 'this is not a human') and accidental (e.g., 'this is a human' vs 'this is not an animal') – the latter are not pure contradictories because 'human' necessarily implies 'animal'.
Next, the author discusses when the truth of one of the two contradictories is determined. The matter (madda) of the judgement – whether necessary, impossible, or possible – affects this. In necessary matter (e.g., 'every human is an animal'), the affirmative is true and the negative false. In impossible matter (e.g., 'every human is a neighing animal'), the negative is true and affirmative false. In possible matter, truth is not determined except for past and present possibles (which have occurred or not). For future contingents (e.g., 'Zaid will walk tomorrow'), neither is determinately true or false in itself, because if one side were fixed, possibility would be lost, and free choice would be negated. However, the author notes that with respect to the cause, determination does exist, but discussion of free will belongs to theology.
Finally, the author lists ten conditions for genuine contradiction: the two propositions must agree in subject (mahkum 'alayh), predicate (mahkum bihi), condition (shart), part (juz'), whole (kull), place (makan), time (zaman), relation (idafa), potentiality (quwwa), and actuality (fi'l). If they differ in any of these, they can both be true simultaneously. Numerous examples illustrate each condition, such as: subject (camel on earth vs camel in sky), predicate (Zaid as just person vs Zaid as justice), condition (writer moving as long as writing vs writer as a person), part (face of Abyssinian black vs eye not black), whole (apparent body black vs apparent and inner body not black), place (Zaid moving on earth vs on sky), time (Zaid pious after repentance and mindfulness vs after repentance without mindfulness), relation (obedient to Creator vs not obedient to creatures), potentiality (knowledgeable potentially vs actually), and actuality (analogous to potentiality). The key lesson: contradiction only occurs when the two propositions are completely identical in all these respects; otherwise, they are not truly contradictory.

Key Takeaway
For genuine contradiction, the two propositions must be identical in subject, predicate, condition, part, whole, place, time, relation, potentiality, and actuality.

The Eight Conditions for Contradiction
Author enumerates eight conditions of unity required for contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the eight conditions of unity required for two propositions to be genuinely contradictory.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires unity in eight respects; a single difference saves both propositions from being contradictory.

In this passage, the author lists the conditions that must be identical for two propositions to be truly contradictory. Without unity in each, both can be true. We will examine each condition with examples from the text to master this essential tool.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of contradiction
Recall that two propositions are contradictory if they cannot both be true and cannot both be false. This requires unity in all relevant respects.
The author refers to a previous definition of contradiction.
2
List the ten items mentioned
The passage mentions ten items: subject (محكوم عليه), predicate (محكوم به), condition (شرط), part (جزء), whole (كل), place (مكان), time (زمان), relation (إضافة), potentiality (قوة), and actuality (فعل).
These are traditionally grouped into eight conditions, with subject and predicate being primary. Potentiality and actuality are sometimes considered under modality.
3
Examine examples for each condition
For each condition, the author gives an example showing that differing in that condition allows both propositions to be true. For instance, differing in subject: 'The camel is slaughtered' (earth) and 'The camel is not slaughtered' (sky) are both true.
Go through each example in the Arabic and translation.
4
Identify the pattern
Notice that in each example, the apparent contradiction is resolved by clarifying the intended meaning. The conditions ensure that the two propositions are about exactly the same thing in the same respect.
This prevents false contradictions in debate.
5
Apply the test to a new pair
Take a pair of propositions that seem contradictory and check each condition. If any condition differs, then both propositions can be true and they are not contradictory.
Use Islamic science examples such as 'The Quran is eternal' and 'The Quran is not eternal' – check unity of time, place, etc.

ثم التناقض على الحد الذي مرَّ ذكره لا يتحقق بين القضيتين إلا باتحادهما في المحكوم عليه وبه والشرط والجزء والكل والمكان والزمان والإضافة والقوة والفعل. فإنهما إذا اختلفتا في أمر من هذه الأمور لم يقيما الصدق والكذب أصلا كما إذا اختلفتا في المحكوم عليه فيقال مثلا الجمل يذبح ويراد به الجمل في الأرض، ويقال الجمل لا يذبح ويراد به الجمل في السماء وهو يذبح الجمل : فإنهما يصدقان معًا. وكذلك في المحكوم به كما يقال زيد عدل ویراد به العادل وزيد ليس بعدل ويراد به العدالة، فإنهما يصدقان أيضًا. وكذلك في الشرط كما يقال الكاتب متحرك ويراد به ما دام كاتبًا، الكاتب ليس بمتحرك ويراد به الشخص الذي يوصف بوصف كونه كاتبًا؛ إذ يمكن اجتماعهما في الصدق وكذلك في الجزء كما يقال جزء حبشي أسود ويراد به الوجه، جزء حبشي ليس بأسود ويراد به العين مثلا. وكذلك في الكل كما يقال كل حبشي أسود ویراد به الظاهر من البدن، كل حبشي ليس بأسود؛ ويراد به الظاهر والباطن من البدن . وكذلك في المكان كما يقال زيد متحرك ويراد به على الأرض زيد ليس بمتحرك ؛ ويراد به على السماء. وكذلك في الزمان كما يقال زید متدین ویراد به بعد التوبة ،وتقائها زيد ليس بمتدین ویراد به بعد [۱۲] التوبة دون تقائها. / وكذلك في الإضافة كما يقال زيد مطيع ويراد به أنه مطيع للخالق، زيد ليس بمطيع ويراد به أنه ليس بمطيع للخلق. وكذلك في القوة كما يقال زيد عالم ويراد به أنه عالم بالقوة، زيد ليس بعالم ويراد به أنه ليس بعالم بالفعل. وعلى هذا في الفعل، فإنهما من جملة ما لا تناقض بينهما حينئذ.
Then contradiction, according to the definition mentioned earlier, does not occur between two propositions except by their unity in the subject, predicate, condition, part, whole, place, time, relation, potentiality, and actuality. For if they differ in any of these matters, they do not establish truth and falsehood at all, as when they differ in subject: e.g. 'The camel is slaughtered' meaning the camel on earth, and 'The camel is not slaughtered' meaning the camel in the sky; both are true together. Similarly in the predicate: e.g. 'Zayd is just' meaning the just person, and 'Zayd is not just' meaning justice; both are also true. Similarly in the condition: e.g. 'The writer is moving' as long as he is writing, 'The writer is not moving' meaning the person described by the attribute of being a writer; they can be true together. Similarly in the part: e.g. 'A part of an Abyssinian is black' meaning the face, 'A part of an Abyssinian is not black' meaning for example the eye. Similarly in the whole: e.g. 'Every Abyssinian is black' meaning the exterior of the body, 'Every Abyssinian is not black' meaning the exterior and interior of the body. Similarly in place: e.g. 'Zayd is moving' meaning on the ground, 'Zayd is not moving' meaning in the sky. Similarly in time: e.g. 'Zayd is religious' meaning after repentance and piety, 'Zayd is not religious' meaning after repentance without piety. Similarly in relation: e.g. 'Zayd is obedient' meaning to the Creator, 'Zayd is not obedient' meaning to the creatures. Similarly in potentiality: e.g. 'Zayd is knowledgeable' meaning potentially, 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' meaning actually. And so on for actuality; these are among what have no contradiction between them then.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage defines the necessary conditions for two propositions to be truly contradictory. The author lists ten items (subject, predicate, condition, part, whole, place, time, relation, potentiality, actuality) but they are traditionally grouped into eight because subject and predicate are primary, and potentiality/actuality often fall under modality. The key point is that contradiction requires unity in all these respects; any difference allows both propositions to be true simultaneously. The author gives concrete examples for each, drawn from everyday language: the camel example for subject, Zayd's justice for predicate, the writer for condition, the Abyssinian for part and whole, place and time distinctions, relation to Creator vs. creatures, and potential vs. actual knowledge. These examples illustrate that apparent contradictions can be resolved by clarifying the intended meaning. The passage serves as a rigorous test for identifying genuine contradictions in dialectical and demonstrative arguments.

Key Takeaway
Two propositions are truly contradictory only if they are unified in subject, predicate, condition, part, whole, place, time, relation, potentiality, and actuality; any difference allows both to be true.

Conditions for Contradiction in Quantified Propositions
Conditions for contradiction and quantified proposition relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a pair of quantified propositions are contradictories, contraries, or subcontraries, based on the conditions of subject, predicate, time, quantity, and quality.

Core Idea
A genuine contradiction requires opposing truth values in every mode; universals that can both be false are merely contraries.

In classical Islamic logic, understanding contradiction (tanāquḍ) is essential for checking the validity of arguments. This passage from the logic textbook explains the minimal conditions according to the majority of philosophers—unity of subject, predicate, and time—and then clarifies how quantity and quality interact. You will learn to distinguish between contradictory pairs (like 'every S is P' vs 'some S is not P') from contrary pairs (two universals) and subcontrary pairs (two particulars).

Guided Path
1
Extract the three minimal conditions
The author states that philosophers require unity of subject (maḥkūm ʿalayh), predicate (maḥkūm bih), and time (zamān). Identify these in the Arabic passage.
These are the core conditions; other factors like place and relation are subsumed under predicate.
2
Map conditions to subcategories
Note how the author subsumes condition, part, and whole under subject, and place, relation, potentiality, and actuality under predicate. This simplifies the list of requirements.
This shows the philosophers' approach of reducing conditions to the most fundamental.
3
Test contradiction for quantified propositions
The author gives examples: 'Every existent is possible' and 'No existent is possible' can both be false (so they are not contradictories but contraries). 'Some existent is possible' and 'Some existent is not possible' can both be true (so they are subcontraries). Recognize these patterns.
Use the matter of possibility (imkān) to see how both can be false/true.
4
Identify the correct contradictory forms
The passage states that contradictories are 'every' vs 'not some' (universal affirmative vs particular negative) and 'some' vs 'no one' (particular affirmative vs universal negative). Memorize these two pairs.
In logical terms: A vs O, and I vs E.
5
Refute the objection about unity of subject
The author addresses a critic who thinks if the subject is one, both contradictory propositions cannot be true. The response distinguishes between numerical oneness and definitional oneness; opposites can apply to the same subject in definition (e.g., evenness and oddness on a number).
This reinforces that unity of subject alone does not prevent contradiction.

وبالجملة فاللازم عند الجمهور من الحكماء وحدة المحكوم عليه وبه والزمان لما أن الشيء المعين للشيء المعين في الزمان المعين لا يمكن أن يثبت ولا يثبت أصلا لكن وحدة الشرط والجزء والكل مندرجة تحت وحدة المحكوم عليه، ووحدة المكان والزمان والإضافة والقوة والفعل مندرجة تحت وحدة المحكوم به وكما أن الاتحاد بينهما من اللوازم في هذه الأمور فكذلك الاختلاف في الغير نحو الكمية والكيفية والجهة إذا كانتا محصورتين. فإنهما إذا كانتا مخصوصتين كفى في التناقض بينهما الاختلاف في الكيفية والجهة بعد الاتحاد في الأمور التي مر ذكرها على خلاف المحصورتين. فإن الكليتين قد تكذبان كما يقال كل موجود ممكن، لا شيء من الموجود بممكن، والجزئيتين قد تصدقـان كما يقال بعض الموجود ممكن بعض الموجود ليس بممكن. وبهذا يظهر أن التناقض لا يمكن بين المهملتين لما أنهما في قوة الجزئيتين. ومنهم من قال كيف يمكن الصدق فيهما والموضوع فيهما شيء واحد لكنه خطأ، فإنه إذا عني بالواحد واحدًا بالعدد فقد أخطأ؛ وإنه ليس بواحد معين. وإن عني بالواحد واحدًا بالحدّ فقد أخطأ أيضًا فإنه لا يمتنع أن تحمل الأضداد أو ما هو في حكمها على الواحد بالحد نحو الزوجية والفردية على العدد الذي هو واحد في الحد فالمتناقضان إذن هما مثل ذي سوري «كل» و «ليس بعض أو بعض» و «لا واحد»، كما يقال كل موجود ممكن، بعض الموجود ليس بممكن، أو يقال بعض الموجود ممكن لا شيء من الموجود بممكن. فالكليتان نحو المتضادتين من حيث إنهما لا يجتمعان ولكن يرتفعان في مادة الإمكان على خلاف المتناقضتين؛ فإنهما لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان البتة لا في مادة الإمكان ولا في غير مادة الإمكان. فالجزئيتان إذن هما الداخلتان تحت
In sum, what is necessary according to the majority of philosophers is the unity of subject, predicate, and time, because a specific thing for a specific thing in a specific time cannot both be established and not established at all. But the unity of condition, part, and whole is subsumed under the unity of the subject, and the unity of place, time, relation, potentiality, and actuality is subsumed under the unity of the predicate. And just as unity between them is necessary in these matters, so also difference in other aspects like quantity, quality, and modality if they are restricted. For if they are specific, difference in quality and modality suffices for contradiction between them after unity in the matters mentioned, contrary to the unrestricted ones. For two universals may both be false, as is said: 'Every existent is possible, No existent is possible', and two particulars may both be true, as is said: 'Some existent is possible, Some existent is not possible'. And by this it becomes clear that contradiction is not possible between the indeterminate (muhmala) because they are in the force of particulars. And some said: how can both be true when the subject is one? But it is wrong, for if by 'one' he means numerically one, he is mistaken; for it is not one determined. And if by 'one' he means one in definition, he is also mistaken, because there is no impossibility in opposites or what is in their ruling being predicated of what is one in definition, like evenness and oddness on the number which is one in definition. So contradictories are like those with a quantifier of 'every' and 'not some', or 'some' and 'no one', as is said: 'Every existent is possible, Some existent is not possible', or 'Some existent is possible, No existent is possible'. For the two universals are like opposites in that they do not both exist together but can both be absent in the matter of possibility, unlike contradictories; for they neither exist together nor are absent at all, neither in the matter of possibility nor in other matter. So the two particulars are the ones that fall under...
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage refines the earlier discussion by presenting the position of the majority of philosophers (al-ḥukamā'), who reduce the necessary conditions for contradiction to just three: unity of subject, predicate, and time. The other conditions (condition, part, whole) are subsumed under the subject, and (place, time, relation, potentiality, actuality) under the predicate. Then the author turns to the role of quantity, quality, and modality in contradiction. He explains that for 'restricted' (maḥṣūra) propositions—universal and particular—difference in quality and modality is sufficient for contradiction after subject, predicate, and time are unified. He illustrates that two universal propositions (e.g., 'Every existent is possible' and 'No existent is possible') can both be false, and two particular propositions (e.g., 'Some existent is possible' and 'Some existent is not possible') can both be true, so they are not contradictories. True contradictories are 'every A is B' vs. 'some A is not B', or 'some A is B' vs. 'no A is B'. The author then refutes an objection that if the subject is one, both cannot be true; he argues that 'one' can be understood numerically or by definition, and it is possible for opposites like even and odd to apply to the same number in definition. Finally, he distinguishes universal propositions from contraries (mutadāddatān), which can both be false, unlike contradictories. The passage ends mid-sentence comparing particulars.

Conditions for Contradiction: Unity and Difference
Author explains the necessary conditions for contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary conditions of unity and difference for two propositions to be contradictory in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires unity in subject, predicate, and time, with difference in quality or modality.

In this passage, the author lays out the conditions that must be met for two propositions to be genuine contradictories. These include unity in subject, predicate, and time, and several other elements, as well as difference in quality and modality when these are restricted. Mastering these conditions is crucial for correctly analyzing arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the elements that must be unified
Look for the author's list: subject (maḥkūm ʻalayh), predicate (maḥkūm bihi), and time are explicitly required. Additional elements like condition, part, whole, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality are subsumed under subject or predicate unity.
Unity does not mean exact identity in every respect; it means the specific aspects must be the same.
2
Identify the elements that must differ
The author mentions quantity, quality, and modality when restricted. For unrestricted modalities, difference in quality and modality suffices after unity is established.
The distinction between restricted and unrestricted modalities affects the required difference.
3
Apply the conditions to a pair of contradictory propositions
Take an Islamic example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Some intoxicant is not forbidden'. Check if subject, predicate, and time are the same. Then check difference in quantity (universal vs. particular) and quality (affirmative vs. negative).
The propositions must also be about the same time and place implicitly.
