وبالجملة فاللازم عند الجمهور من الحكماء وحدة المحكوم عليه وبه والزمان لما أن الشيء المعين للشيء المعين في الزمان المعين لا يمكن أن يثبت ولا يثبت أصلا لكن وحدة الشرط والجزء والكل مندرجة تحت وحدة محصورتين. فإنهما إذا كانتا مخصوصتين كفى في التناقض بينهما الاختلاف في الكيفية والجهة بعد الاتحاد في الأمور التي مر ذكرها على خلاف المحصورتين. فإن الكليتين قد تكذبان كما يقال كل موجود ممكن، لا شيء من الموجود بممكن، والجزئيتين قد تصدقان كما يقال بعض الموجود ممكن بعض الموجود ليس بممكن. وبهذا يظهر أن التناقض لا يمكن بين المهملتين لما أنهما في قوة الجزئيتين. ومنهم من قال كيف يمكن الصدق فيهما والموضوع فيهما شيء واحد لكنه خطأ، فإنه إذا عني بالواحد واحدًا بالعدد فقد أخطأ؛ وإنه ليس بواحد معين. وإن عني بالواحد واحدًا بالحدّ فقد أخطأ أيضًا فإنه لا يمتنع أن تحمل الأضداد أو ما هو في حكمها على الواحد بالحد نحو الزوجية والفردية على العدد الذي هو واحد في الحد فالمتناقضان إذن هما مثل ذي سوري «كل» و «ليس بعض أو بعض» و «لا واحد»، كما يقال كل موجود ممكن، بعض الموجود ليس بممكن، أو يقال بعض الموجود ممكن لا شيء من الموجود بممكن. فالكليتان نحو المتضادتين من حيث إنهما لا يجتمعان ولكن يرتفعان في مادة الإمكان على خلاف المتناقضتين؛ فإنهما لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان البتة لا في مادة الإمكان ولا في غير مادة الإمكان. فالجزئيتان إذن هما الداخلتان تحت التضاد من حيث إنهما داخلتان تحت الكليتين لا من حيث إنهما يجتمعان، أو لا يجتمعان؛ إذ الاجتماع ممتنع في الكليتين دون الارتفاع، فالارتفاع ممتنع في الجزئيتين دون الاجتماع كما مر . ثم المحمول إذا يدل تحصيله بالعدول كما يقال كل موجود ممكن، لا شيء من الموجود بلا ممكن، فهما السالبتان من حيث إن كل واحد منهما يتلو تلو صاحبه ، والجزئيتان منهما فهما الداخلتان تحت التالي كما يقال بعض الموجود ممكن بعض الموجود ليس بلا ممكن. ثم لقائل أن يقول : لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان التناقض بين القضيتين مفتقرا في التحقق إلى الشرائط المذكورة نحو الاتحاد في البعض من الأمور، والاختلاف في البعض كذلك كما مر ؛ وإنه لا يمكن لما كان التناقض هو المنافاة الذاتية وجودًا وعدمًا. والافتقار إلى الشرائط مما ينافي في المنافاة الذاتية بالضرورة ضرورة افتقار كل واحدة منهما إلى وجود تلك الشرائط في كونها منافية؛ ولا يقال تلك الشرائط شرائط التناقض بينهما فالتناقض مما يفتقر إلى تلك الشرائط لا المنافاة الذاتية بينهما من حيث إنهما متناقضتان؛ فالمتناقضة إذن لذاتها منافية، وإن كان التناقض مفتقرًا إلى تلك الشرائط. فإنا نقول: هب أنه كذلك لكن القضية لا تكون متناقضة إلا وأن تكون تلك الشرائط متحققة، والمتناقضة لا تكون منافية لذاتها إلا وأن تكون متناقضة؛ فالمتناقضة إذن لا تكون منافية لذاتها إلا وأن تكون تلك الشرائط متحققة. والحق في هذا البحث أن يقال : المنافاة الذاتية إذا كانت عبارةً عما لا يفتقر وجودها إلى شيء ما أصلا فلا مجال لوجودها بين المتناقضتين، وقد كانت المنافاة الذاتية بينهما مفتقرة إلى نفس المنافاة لا محالة. وإذا كانت عبارة عما يجب بوجودها أن تكون هذه القضية منافية لتلك القضية من غير أن يكون لغيرها مدخل في كونها منافية . فقد كان من اللوازم أن تكون متحققة بينهما
In summary, what is necessary according to the majority of philosophers is the unity of the subject, predicate, and time, since a specific thing for a specific thing at a specific time cannot be affirmed nor denied at all. But the unity of condition, part, and whole is subsumed under the unity of the subject, and the unity of place, time, relation, potentiality, and actuality is subsumed under the unity of the predicate. Just as union between them is necessary in these matters, so too difference in other aspects such as quantity, quality, and modality, when they are restricted. For when they are specific, difference in quality and modality suffices for contradiction between them after union in the matters previously mentioned, contrary to the unrestricted ones. For the two universals may both be false, as when one says 'Every existent is possible, no existent is impossible,' and the two particulars may both be true, as when one says 'Some existent is possible, some existent is not possible.' And by this it appears that contradiction is not possible between two indefinite propositions because they are equivalent to two particulars. And among them there is one who said: How can [both] be true when the subject in them is one? But that is a mistake. For if he means by 'one' numerically one, he has erred; for it is not a specific one. And if he means by 'one' one by definition, he has also erred, for it is not impossible that opposites or what is in their ruling be predicated of what is one by definition, such as evenness and oddness upon the number which is one by definition. So the two contradictories are things like having a quantifier 'every' and 'not some' or 'some' and 'not one,' as when one says 'Every existent is possible, some existent is not possible,' or one says 'Some existent is possible, no existent is possible.' So the two universals are like the two contraries insofar as they do not meet but both can be absent in the matter of possibility, contrary to the two contradictories; for they neither meet nor are both absent at all, neither in the matter of possibility nor outside the matter of possibility. So the two particulars are the ones subsumed under contrariety insofar as they are subsumed under the two universals, not insofar as they meet or do not meet; for meeting is impossible in the two universals without their absence, so absence is impossible in the two particulars without meeting, as has passed. Then the predicate, if its affirmation is indicated by adversal [negation], as when one says 'Every existent is possible, nothing of the existent is without possibility,' then they are the two negatives insofar as each of them follows the other, and the two particulars among them are the ones subsumed under the follower, as when one says 'Some existent is possible, some existent is not without possibility.' Then it may be said: If it were as mentioned, then contradiction between two propositions would depend in actualization on the mentioned conditions, such as union in some matters and difference in others as has passed; and that is not possible because contradiction is intrinsic opposition in existence and non-existence. And dependence on conditions opposes intrinsic opposition necessarily, because each of them necessarily depends on the existence of those conditions in being opposed; and it is not said that those conditions are conditions for the contradiction between them, so that contradiction depends on those conditions, not the intrinsic opposition between them insofar as they are contradictories. For the contradictory is thus by itself opposed, even if contradiction depends on those conditions. For we say: Grant that it is so, but the proposition is not contradictory unless those conditions are realized, and the contradictory is not opposed by itself unless it is contradictory; so the contradictory is thus not opposed by itself unless those conditions are realized. And the truth in this investigation is to say: If intrinsic opposition is an expression for that whose existence does not depend on anything at all, then there is no room for its existence between contradictories, and indeed intrinsic opposition between them depends on the opposition itself inevitably. And if it is an expression for that which by its existence necessitates that this proposition is opposed to that proposition without anything else having a role in its being opposed, then it would be necessary that it be realized between them.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the conditions necessary for logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) and a defense against the objection that these conditions undermine the intrinsic nature of contradiction. The author begins by stating that according to most philosophers, contradiction requires unity (ittiḥād) in the subject (maḥkūm ʻalayh), predicate (maḥkūm bihi), and time. Other forms of unity—such as condition, part, whole—fall under subject unity, while place, time, relation, potentiality, and actuality fall under predicate unity. Then the author explains that after establishing unity, difference (ikhtilāf) in quantity, quality, or modality suffices for contradiction, but only when these modalities are restricted (maḥṣūratān). For unrestricted modalities, difference in quality and modality is enough. The author illustrates with examples from possibility: universal propositions like 'Every existent is possible' and 'No existent is impossible' can both be false (i.e., contraries), while particular propositions like 'Some existent is possible' and 'Some existent is not possible' can both be true. This shows that indefinite propositions cannot contradict because they reduce to particulars. An objection arises: how can both particulars be true if their subject is one? The author replies that 'one' may mean numerically one (which would be impossible) or one by definition (which allows opposites like even/odd on the same number). The correct definition of contradictories involves quantifiers: 'Every S is P' vs. 'Some S is not P' or 'Some S is P' vs. 'No S is P'. The relation of universals is like contrariety (they cannot both be true but can both be false in some matter), while contradictories can neither both be true nor both be false. Particulars are subsumed under contrariety only because they are subsumed under universals. Next, the author discusses adverse (bi-l-ʻudūl) propositions, where the predicate is negated, giving formulas like 'Every S is P' and 'No S is non-P' as negatives. A further objection claims that if contradiction requires these conditions, it cannot be intrinsic opposition (munāfāt dhātīyya) because dependence on conditions is contrary to self-sufficiency. The author responds by distinguishing: the conditions are necessary for the propositions to be contradictory; the contradictory itself is intrinsically opposed only when those conditions hold. The truth (al-ḥaqq) is that if 'intrinsic opposition' means requiring no external condition, then it cannot exist between contradictories because they require at least themselves. If it means that given the opposition, nothing else has a role, then such opposition must have its conditions. Thus, the conditions for contradiction are not separate from the intrinsic opposition but are part of its realization.

Key Takeaway
Contradiction requires precise conditions of unity in subject, predicate, and time, and difference in quality or modality; imprecise conditions lead to invalid contradictions.

Defending Intrinsic Opposition: The Dialectical Exchange
Author defends intrinsic opposition against objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct an objection against the intrinsic nature of contradiction and evaluate the author's response within the framework of Islamic dialectical method.

Core Idea
The conditions for contradiction are not external but constitute the very definition of intrinsic opposition.

The author anticipates an objection: if contradiction requires all these conditions, how can it be intrinsic? The objection claims that dependence on conditions negates intrinsic opposition. The author responds by distinguishing between the conditions being necessary for the contradictory propositions to be realized and the intrinsic opposition being an intrinsic property of those propositions when the conditions hold. This dialectical exchange is typical of the Islamic logical tradition, where objections are systematically addressed.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Identify the mu'tarid's claim: contradiction (tanāquḍ) requires conditions like unity of subject, predicate, etc., therefore it cannot be intrinsic opposition (munāfāt dhātīyya) because intrinsic opposition should not depend on anything external.
The objection is based on a strict definition of 'intrinsic'.
2
Analyze the author's first response
The author grants the premise but argues that the conditions are conditions for the contradiction between the propositions, not for the intrinsic opposition between them. The contradictory propositions are intrinsically opposed by themselves, but they only become contradictory when those conditions are met.
This distinguishes between the opposition itself and the actualization of contradiction.
3
Examine the refined argument
The author then presents a stronger counter: the proposition is not contradictory unless the conditions hold, and the contradictory is not intrinsically opposed unless it is contradictory. Therefore, the intrinsic opposition itself depends on those conditions.
This seems to favor the objector, but the author then offers a resolution.
4
Understand the final resolution (al-ḥaqq)
The author distinguishes two senses of 'intrinsic opposition': (1) that which requires absolutely no external condition – then it cannot exist between contradictories because they require at least themselves; (2) that which, given its existence, has no other role in causing the opposition – then it must have conditions, and the conditions are part of the definition.
The second sense preserves both the intrinsic nature and the necessity of conditions.

محصورتين. فإنهما إذا كانتا مخصوصتين كفى في التناقض بينهما الاختلاف في الكيفية والجهة بعد الاتحاد في الأمور التي مر ذكرها على خلاف المحصورتين. فإن الكليتين قد تكذبان كما يقال كل موجود ممكن، لا شيء من فيهما شيء واحد لكنه خطأ، فإنه إذا عني بالواحد واحدًا بالعدد فقد أخطأ؛ وإنه ليس بواحد معين. وإن عني بالواحد واحدًا بالحدّ فقد أخطأ أيضًا فإنه لا يمتنع أن تحمل الأضداد أو ما هو في حكمها على الواحد بالحد نحو الزوجية والفردية على العدد الذي هو واحد في الحد فالمتناقضان إذن هما مثل ذي سوري «كل» و «ليس بعض أو بعض» و «لا واحد»، كما يقال كل موجود ممكن، بعض الموجود ليس بممكن، أو يقال بعض الموجود ممكن لا شيء من الموجود بممكن. فالكليتان نحو المتضادتين من حيث إنهما لا يجتمعان ولكن يرتفعان في مادة الإمكان على خلاف المتناقضتين؛ فإنهما لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان البتة لا في مادة الإمكان ولا في غير مادة الإمكان. فالجزئيتان إذن هما الداخلتان تحت التضاد من حيث إنهما داخلتان تحت الكليتين لا من حيث إنهما يجتمعان، أو لا يجتمعان؛ إذ الاجتماع ممتنع في الكليتين دون الارتفاع، فالارتفاع ممتنع في الجزئيتين دون الاجتماع كما مر . ثم المحمول إذا يدل تحصيله بالعدول كما يقال كل موجود ممكن، لا شيء من الموجود بلا ممكن، فهما السالبتان من حيث إن كل واحد منهما يتلو تلو صاحبه ، والجزئيتان منهما فهما الداخلتان تحت التالي كما يقال بعض الموجود ممكن بعض الموجود ليس بلا ممكن. ثم لقائل أن يقول : لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان التناقض بين القضيتين مفتقرا في التحقق إلى الشرائط المذكورة نحو الاتحاد في البعض من الأمور، والاختلاف في البعض كذلك كما مر ؛ وإنه لا يمكن لما كان التناقض هو المنافاة الذاتية وجودًا وعدمًا. والافتقار إلى الشرائط مما ينافي في المنافاة الذاتية بالضرورة ضرورة افتقار كل واحدة منهما إلى وجود تلك الشرائط في كونها منافية؛ ولا يقال تلك الشرائط شرائط التناقض بينهما فالتناقض مما يفتقر إلى تلك الشرائط لا المنافاة الذاتية بينهما من حيث إنهما متناقضتان؛ فالمتناقضة إذن لذاتها منافية، وإن كان التناقض مفتقرًا إلى تلك الشرائط. فإنا نقول: هب أنه كذلك لكن القضية لا تكون متناقضة إلا وأن تكون تلك الشرائط متحققة، والمتناقضة لا تكون منافية لذاتها إلا وأن تكون متناقضة؛ فالمتناقضة إذن لا تكون منافية لذاتها إلا وأن تكون تلك الشرائط متحققة. والحق في هذا البحث أن يقال : المنافاة الذاتية إذا كانت عبارةً عما لا يفتقر وجودها إلى شيء ما أصلا فلا مجال لوجودها بين المتناقضتين، وقد كانت المنافاة الذاتية بينهما مفتقرة إلى نفس المنافاة لا محالة. وإذا كانت عبارة عما يجب بوجودها أن تكون هذه القضية منافية لتلك القضية من غير أن يكون لغيرها مدخل في كونها منافية . فقد كان من اللوازم أن تكون متحققة بينهما
In summary, what is necessary according to the majority of philosophers is the unity of the subject, predicate, and time, since a specific thing for a specific thing at a specific time cannot be affirmed nor denied at all. But the unity of condition, part, and whole is subsumed under the unity of the subject, and the unity of place, time, relation, potentiality, and actuality is subsumed under the unity of the predicate. Just as union between them is necessary in these matters, so too difference in other aspects such as quantity, quality, and modality, when they are restricted. For when they are specific, difference in quality and modality suffices for contradiction between them after union in the matters previously mentioned, contrary to the unrestricted ones. For the two universals may both be false, as when one says 'Every existent is possible, no existent is impossible,' and the two particulars may both be true, as when one says 'Some existent is possible, some existent is not possible.' And by this it appears that contradiction is not possible between two indefinite propositions because they are equivalent to two particulars. And among them there is one who said: How can [both] be true when the subject in them is one? But that is a mistake. For if he means by 'one' numerically one, he has erred; for it is not a specific one. And if he means by 'one' one by definition, he has also erred, for it is not impossible that opposites or what is in their ruling be predicated of what is one by definition, such as evenness and oddness upon the number which is one by definition. So the two contradictories are things like having a quantifier 'every' and 'not some' or 'some' and 'not one,' as when one says 'Every existent is possible, some existent is not possible,' or one says 'Some existent is possible, no existent is possible.' So the two universals are like the two contraries insofar as they do not meet but both can be absent in the matter of possibility, contrary to the two contradictories; for they neither meet nor are both absent at all, neither in the matter of possibility nor outside the matter of possibility. So the two particulars are the ones subsumed under contrariety insofar as they are subsumed under the two universals, not insofar as they meet or do not meet; for meeting is impossible in the two universals without their absence, so absence is impossible in the two particulars without meeting, as has passed. Then the predicate, if its affirmation is indicated by adversal [negation], as when one says 'Every existent is possible, nothing of the existent is without possibility,' then they are the two negatives insofar as each of them follows the other, and the two particulars among them are the ones subsumed under the follower, as when one says 'Some existent is possible, some existent is not without possibility.' Then it may be said: If it were as mentioned, then contradiction between two propositions would depend in actualization on the mentioned conditions, such as union in some matters and difference in others as has passed; and that is not possible because contradiction is intrinsic opposition in existence and non-existence. And dependence on conditions opposes intrinsic opposition necessarily, because each of them necessarily depends on the existence of those conditions in being opposed; and it is not said that those conditions are conditions for the contradiction between them, so that contradiction depends on those conditions, not the intrinsic opposition between them insofar as they are contradictories. For the contradictory is thus by itself opposed, even if contradiction depends on those conditions. For we say: Grant that it is so, but the proposition is not contradictory unless those conditions are realized, and the contradictory is not opposed by itself unless it is contradictory; so the contradictory is thus not opposed by itself unless those conditions are realized. And the truth in this investigation is to say: If intrinsic opposition is an expression for that whose existence does not depend on anything at all, then there is no room for its existence between contradictories, and indeed intrinsic opposition between them depends on the opposition itself inevitably. And if it is an expression for that which by its existence necessitates that this proposition is opposed to that proposition without anything else having a role in its being opposed, then it would be necessary that it be realized between them.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the conditions necessary for logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) and a defense against the objection that these conditions undermine the intrinsic nature of contradiction. The author begins by stating that according to most philosophers, contradiction requires unity (ittiḥād) in the subject (maḥkūm ʻalayh), predicate (maḥkūm bihi), and time. Other forms of unity—such as condition, part, whole—fall under subject unity, while place, time, relation, potentiality, and actuality fall under predicate unity. Then the author explains that after establishing unity, difference (ikhtilāf) in quantity, quality, or modality suffices for contradiction, but only when these modalities are restricted (maḥṣūratān). For unrestricted modalities, difference in quality and modality is enough. The author illustrates with examples from possibility: universal propositions like 'Every existent is possible' and 'No existent is impossible' can both be false (i.e., contraries), while particular propositions like 'Some existent is possible' and 'Some existent is not possible' can both be true. This shows that indefinite propositions cannot contradict because they reduce to particulars. An objection arises: how can both particulars be true if their subject is one? The author replies that 'one' may mean numerically one (which would be impossible) or one by definition (which allows opposites like even/odd on the same number). The correct definition of contradictories involves quantifiers: 'Every S is P' vs. 'Some S is not P' or 'Some S is P' vs. 'No S is P'. The relation of universals is like contrariety (they cannot both be true but can both be false in some matter), while contradictories can neither both be true nor both be false. Particulars are subsumed under contrariety only because they are subsumed under universals. Next, the author discusses adverse (bi-l-ʻudūl) propositions, where the predicate is negated, giving formulas like 'Every S is P' and 'No S is non-P' as negatives. A further objection claims that if contradiction requires these conditions, it cannot be intrinsic opposition (munāfāt dhātīyya) because dependence on conditions is contrary to self-sufficiency. The author responds by distinguishing: the conditions are necessary for the propositions to be contradictory; the contradictory itself is intrinsically opposed only when those conditions hold. The truth (al-ḥaqq) is that if 'intrinsic opposition' means requiring no external condition, then it cannot exist between contradictories because they require at least themselves. If it means that given the opposition, nothing else has a role, then such opposition must have its conditions. Thus, the conditions for contradiction are not separate from the intrinsic opposition but are part of its realization.

Key Terms
التناقض
المنافاة الذاتية
الشرائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the final resolution regarding intrinsic opposition (المنافاة الذاتية) and the conditions for contradiction?
A) Intrinsic opposition requires absolutely no conditions, thus contradiction is impossible.
B) Intrinsic opposition is defined as that which has no external role in causing opposition, and conditions are part of the definition of the contradictory propositions.
C) Intrinsic opposition is identical to the conditions themselves, so conditions are the opposition.
D) The objector is correct, and contradiction is not intrinsic.

Key Takeaway
Intrinsic opposition is not undermined by conditions; rather, the conditions are part of what makes two propositions intrinsically opposed, when properly understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a jadal exchange between two scholars over the following: One says 'The world is temporally originated' and 'The world is eternal' are contradictories. The objector argues that they cannot be intrinsic opposites because they involve different temporal conditions. Write the response using the author's resolution.
?
Identify the two senses of 'intrinsic opposition' distinguished by the author. Then evaluate which sense is appropriate for the concept of contradiction in Islamic logic.

Conditions for Contradiction and Intrinsic Opposition
Author presents an objection against conditioned contradiction and responds with the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of intrinsic opposition (munāfāh dhāthiyyah) and explain why conditions for contradiction do not negate it.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires conditions, but intrinsic opposition does not depend on external causes.

This lesson addresses a key objection in Islamic logic: if contradiction requires specific conditions (unity of subject, predicate, time, etc.), how can it be considered intrinsic opposition? The author presents the objection and refines the doctrine, showing that while conditions are necessary for a proposition to be contradictory, the opposition itself is intrinsic and requires no external cause. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion about the nature of logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that if contradiction depends on conditions, then it cannot be intrinsic opposition because intrinsic opposition should depend on nothing external.
2
Identify the initial response
The author initially grants that conditions are conditions of contradiction, not of the opposition itself; the contradictory is opposing by itself.
3
Recognize the refinement
The author then pushes further: a proposition is not contradictory unless conditions are met, so the opposition is not independent of conditions in the sense that the propositions must fulfill the contradictory relation.
4
Grasp the resolution
The correct view distinguishes two definitions of intrinsic opposition: (a) something that depends on nothing at all – impossible for contradictories; (b) something that requires no external factor to cause the opposition – this holds for contradictories. Conditions are necessary but not external causes.

التضاد من حيث إنهما داخلتان تحت الكليتين لا من حيث إنهما يجتمعان، أو لا يجتمعان؛ إذ الاجتماع ممتنع في الكليتين دون الارتفاع، فالارتفاع ممتنع في الجزئيتين دون الاجتماع كما مر . ثمَّ المحمول إذا يدل تحصيله بالعدول كما يقال كل موجود ممكن، لا شيء / من الموجود بلا ممكن، فهما السالبتان من حيث إن كل واحد منهما يتلو تلو صاحبه ، والجزئيتان منهما فهما الداخلتان تحت التالي كما يقال بعض الموجود ممكن بعض الموجود ليس بلا ممكن. ثم لقائل أن يقول : لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان التناقض بين القضيتين مفتقرا في التحقق إلى الشرائط المذكورة نحو الاتحاد في البعض من الأمور، والاختلاف في البعض كذلك كما مر ؛ وإنه لا يمكن لما كان التناقض هو المنافاة الذاتية وجودًا وعدمًا. والافتقار إلى الشرائط مما ينافي في المنافاة الذاتية بالضرورة ضرورة افتقار كل واحدة منهما إلى وجود تلك الشرائط في كونها منافية؛ ولا يقال تلك الشرائط شرائط التناقض بينهما فالتناقض مما يفتقر إلى تلك الشرائط لا المنافاة الذاتية بينهما من حيث إنهما متناقضتان؛ فالمتناقضة إذن لذاتها ،منافية، وإن كان التناقض مفتقرًا إلى تلك الشرائط. فإنا نقول: هب أنه كذلك لكن القضية لا تكون متناقضة إلا وأن تكون تلك الشرائط متحققة، والمتناقضة لا تكون منافية لذاتها إلا وأن تكون متناقضة؛ فالمتناقضة إذن لا تكون منافية لذاتها إلا وأن تكون تلك الشرائط متحققة. والحق في هذا البحث أن يقال : المنافاة الذاتية إذا كانت عبارةً عما لا يفتقر وجودها إلى شيء ما أصلا فلا مجال لوجودها بين المتناقضتين، وقد كانت المنافاة الذاتية بينهما مفتقرة إلى نفس المنافاة لا محالة. وإذا كانت عبارة عما يجب بوجودها أن تكون هذه القضية منافية لتلك القضية من غير أن يكون لغيرها مدخل في كونها منافية . فقد كان من اللوازم أن تكون متحققة بينهما فإنه لا مدخل للغير في كونها منافية وإلا لما كانت منافية على الإطلاق، وقد كانت منافية ، هذا خلف.
The contrariety in terms of them both falling under the two universals, not in terms of whether they combine or do not combine; for combination is impossible in the two universals without elimination, so elimination is impossible in the two particulars without combination, as previously. Then the predicate, if its acquisition is indicated by modulation (al-ʻudūl), as in: 'Every existent is possible, no existent is without possibility'—so they are the two negative propositions in the sense that each one follows the other, and the two particular ones among them are those that fall under the consequent, as in: 'Some existent is possible, some existent is not without possibility.' Then someone may say: If it were as mentioned completely, then the contradiction between the two propositions would, in actualization, depend upon the conditions mentioned—such as unity in some respects and difference in others, as previously; and that is impossible, because contradiction is the intrinsic opposition in existence and non-existence. And the dependence on conditions contradicts intrinsic opposition necessarily, by the necessity of each one of them needing the existence of those conditions in its being opposing. And it cannot be said that those conditions are conditions of the contradiction between them, so contradiction depends upon those conditions, but not the intrinsic opposition between them insofar as they are contradictories; for the contradictory is thus opposing by itself, even if contradiction depends upon those conditions. For we say: Grant that it is so, but a proposition is not contradictory unless those conditions are realized, and the contradictory is not opposing by itself unless it is contradictory; so the contradictory is thus not opposing by itself unless those conditions are realized. And the truth in this investigation is to say: If intrinsic opposition is an expression for what does not depend in its existence on anything at all, then there is no room for its existence between contradictories, even though the intrinsic opposition between them inevitably depends on opposition itself. And if it is an expression for what must, by its existence, make this proposition opposing that proposition without anything else having a role in its being opposing, then it would be necessary that it be actualized between them, for nothing else has a role in its being opposing—otherwise it would not be opposing absolutely, and it has been opposing—this is a contradiction (khulf).
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about the nature of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and its dependence on conditions. The author first sets up an example of universal and particular negative propositions under the topic of contrariety (taḍādd), then presents an objection. The objection states that if contradiction requires conditions (such as unity of subject, predicate, time, etc.), then contradiction cannot be intrinsic opposition (munāfāh dhāthiyyah), because intrinsic opposition should not depend on anything external. The author's response refines this: the conditions are necessary for a proposition to be contradictory, but the opposition itself is intrinsic to the contradictory relation. The correct view distinguishes two definitions of 'intrinsic opposition': (1) something that depends on nothing at all—in which case it cannot exist between contradictories; (2) something that requires no external factor to cause the opposition—in which case it does exist between contradictories. The argument uses the example of 'Every existent is possible' and 'No existent is without possibility' to illustrate types of negative propositions. This lesson clarifies that conditions for contradiction do not negate the intrinsic opposition; they are necessary for the proposition to fulfill the contradictory relation.

The conditions for contradiction do not negate intrinsic opposition; they are necessary for the propositions to be in a contradictory relation, while the opposition itself requires no external cause.

Understanding Intrinsic Opposition in Contradictory Propositions
Author defines scope of intrinsic opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which pairs of statements or terms exhibit intrinsic opposition (munāfāh dhāthiyyah) and distinguish them from accidental or verbal opposites.

Core Idea
Intrinsic opposition exists only between contradictory propositions, not between simple terms or contraries.

This passage refines our understanding of logical opposition. The author explains that genuine, intrinsic opposition—the kind that makes two statements true contradictories—is found nowhere else. By mastering this distinction, you avoid common pitfalls like mistaking verbal opposites for logical contradictories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Locate where the author asserts that intrinsic opposition is only realized between two contradictory propositions. This is the central thesis.
The phrase 'لا تكون متحققة إلا بين القضيتين المتناقضتين' is the key.
2
Understand why simple terms are excluded
The author argues that contradiction between simple terms (mufradāt) is impossible. Why? Because contradiction requires a proposition with a truth value. Simple terms like 'existence' and 'non-existence' are only verbal opposites (naqīḍ lafẓī), not logical contradictories.
Refer to the example of الوجود واللاوجود.
3
Analyze the case of contraries (mutadāddatān)
Contraries (e.g., black and white) are opposites, but their opposition is accidental. One contrary opposes the other only because it opposes the other's necessary concomitant. This is not intrinsic opposition.
The author says 'منافاة في الغير' means the opposition is due to a third factor.
4
Apply the concept to an Islamic science example
Consider the kalam argument: 'The world is either eternal or originated.' These two propositions are contradictory if they meet the conditions of unity (subject, predicate, time, etc.). Check whether the opposition is intrinsic—i.e., they cannot both be true and cannot both be false.
If they are truly contradictory, the opposition is intrinsic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ المنافاة الذاتية لا تكون متحققة إلا بين القضيتين المتناقضتين، أما بين القضيتين فلاستحالة التناقض بين المفردين، وقد كان التناقض بين الشيئين بالنسبة إلى محل واحد في زمان واحد من جهة واحدة كما مر . نعم، قد يطلق لفظ النقيض على المفرد من حيث إنه يقابل المفرد الآخر لفظا نحو الوجود واللاوجود مثلًا لكنه لا يكون نقيضًا بالحقيقة. وأما بين المتناقضتين فلاستحالة المنافاة الذاتية في غيرهما، والمنافاة في الغير لا تكون إلا بعرضية شيء آخر كما في المتضادتين مثلا. فإن إحداهما لا تكون منافية للأخرى إلا بعرضية كونها منافية للازمها على ما عرف من قبل في المتقابلات.
Then, intrinsic opposition is only realized between two contradictory propositions. As for between two propositions, it is because contradiction between simple terms is impossible, and contradiction between two things is relative to a single locus at a single time from a single respect, as previously. Indeed, the term 'contradictory' may be applied to a simple term insofar as it verbally opposes another simple term, such as 'existence' and 'non-existence' for example, but it is not truly a contradictory. And as for between two contradictories, it is because intrinsic opposition in other than them is impossible, and opposition in other than them only occurs accidentally, as in the case of two contraries, for example. For one of them is not opposing the other except accidentally, due to its opposition to the other's concomitant, as was previously known in the section on correlatives.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of intrinsic opposition (munāfāh dhāthiyyah). The author asserts that this kind of opposition only exists between contradictory propositions (qadiyyatān mutanāqiḍatān). It cannot exist between simple terms (mufradāt) because contradiction requires a propositional structure with subject and predicate. The terms 'existence' and 'non-existence' are only verbal opposites (naqīḍ lafẓī), not logical contradictories. Similarly, intrinsic opposition is not found in other pairs like contraries (mutadāddatān); the opposition in contraries is accidental (bi-ʼaraḍiyyah), because one contrary opposes the other only indirectly through its opposition to the other's necessary concomitant. For example, the opposition between 'black' and 'white' is not intrinsic but due to each excluding the other's location in a subject. The lesson reinforces that true logical contradiction is a relation between propositions, not between terms.

Key Terms
منافاة ذاتية
نقيض
متضادّتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Simple terms like 'existence' and 'non-existence'.
B) Two contraries such as 'black' and 'white'.
C) Two propositions that meet the conditions of unity.
D) Two correlatives like 'father' and 'son'.

Key Takeaway
Intrinsic opposition is exclusive to contradictory propositions; simple terms and contraries do not qualify.

The Uniqueness of the Contradictory in Categorical Propositions
Author explains why each type of categorical proposition has a unique contradictory and the logician's need to identify them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary conditions for a contradictory proposition and explain why each categorical type has only one unique contradictory.

Core Idea
A proposition's contradictory is unique because time, subject, and predicate must be unified.

In this passage, the author explains that every categorical proposition has exactly one contradictory, not more. This is because negation of a predicate from a subject at a single time and in a single respect can only be done in one way. The logician must be able to pinpoint the contradictory for each type of proposition, which is often difficult due to the need to observe unity of time. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the uniqueness of contradiction and why it matters for logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of unity
The author states that a single predicate of a single subject at a single time and in a single respect cannot be negated more than once. Reflect on why this imposes a unique contradictory: if there were multiple contradictories, they would have to differ in some condition, but the conditions are fixed.
Consider the example: 'Zayd is standing' has only one contradictory: 'Zayd is not standing' at that same time.
2
Identify the role of time in absolute and modal propositions
Time is a crucial element of unity. In absolute propositions (without modality), the time is implicit; in modal propositions, time is part of the modal modifier. The author warns that observing time unity is difficult, sometimes impossible.
For absolute propositions, if no time is specified, the contradictory must refer to the same time context.
3
Recognize the logician's task
Because identifying the correct contradictory is hard, the logician is compelled to clarify the contradictory for each proposition type. The well-known view is that an absolute proposition's contradictory is another absolute proposition of the same type.
This sets the stage for enumerating contradictories in later sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في التناقض في الحمليات ] ولما كانت القضية منقسمة إلى الأقسام التي مرَّ ذكرها في الجهات ويتعين كل قسم من تلك الأقسام في الحمليّات والشرطيات فلا بد من أن يتعين نقيض كل قسم من الأقسام ويتبين بأنه نقيضه. والنقيض للواحد منها واحد ليس إلا ؛ إذ المحمول الواحد من موضوع واحد في زمان واحد بجهة واحدة لا يمكن أن يسلب عنه مرة بعد مرة بل مرة واحدة كما في الإيجاب. والزمان من جملة ما يجب رعاية اتحاده بالحقيقة في المطلقات والموجهات. والرعاية أمر صعب لا يمكن كل واحد في كل صورة بل لا يمكن أصلا في البعض من الصور، فالمنطقي يضطر إلى التنبيه على نقيض كل واحدة من القضايا المطلقة والموجهة . والمشهور في المطلقة أن لها نقيضا من جنسها لكنه
Section: [On Contradiction in Categorical Propositions]. And since the proposition is divided into the divisions previously mentioned in the section on modalities, and each division of those divisions is determined in categoricals and conditionals, it is necessary that the contradictory of each division be determined and established as its contradictory. And the contradictory of a single one is only one, because a single predicate of a single subject at a single time in a single respect cannot be negated from it time after time, but only once, as in affirmation. And time is among the things whose unity must be observed in the absolute (muṭlaqāt) and modal (muwajjahāt) propositions. And observing it is difficult; not everyone can do it in every case, and it is actually impossible in some cases. So the logician is compelled to indicate the contradictory of each absolute and modal proposition. And the well-known view regarding the absolute proposition is that it has a contradictory of the same type, but...
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section (faṣl) on contradiction specifically in categorical propositions (ḥamliyyāt). The author states that since propositions are divided into various types (according to quantity, quality, and modality), each type has a unique contradictory (naqīḍ). The argument is that a single predicate cannot be negated from a single subject at the same time and respect in more than one way—just as affirmation is singular, so too negation must be singular. Time unity is particularly important for absolute and modal propositions. However, identifying the exact contradictory is difficult, sometimes impossible in practice, so the logician must point out the contradictory for each type. The author mentions the well-known view (al-mashhūr) that the contradictory of an absolute proposition is of the same type (i.e., another absolute proposition). The passage sets the stage for enumerating the contradictories of each categorical proposition type.

Key Takeaway
Every categorical proposition has exactly one contradictory, and the logician must carefully determine it based on the conditions of unity (subject, predicate, time, respect).

Essential Contradiction: Between Propositions, Not Singulars
Author defines essential contradiction as exclusive to contradictory propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish true contradiction (tanāquḍ) from verbal opposition of singular terms.

Core Idea
True contradiction only exists between propositions, not between singular terms.

In this passage, the author clarifies a fundamental point: essential opposition (al-munāfāh al-dhātiyya) only occurs between two contradictory propositions. While we sometimes loosely call singular terms like 'existence' and 'non-existence' contradictory, this is not true contradiction. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise logical analysis in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about essential opposition
The author states that essential opposition is only between two contradictory propositions. Find the Arabic phrase that makes this claim.
2
Understand why singulars cannot be contradictory
Contradiction requires a single subject, time, and aspect; singular terms lack these. The author mentions these conditions in passing.
3
Recognise the loose use of 'contradictory' for singulars
The author acknowledges that words like 'existence' and 'non-existence' are called contradictories in a verbal sense, but this is not real contradiction.
4
Distinguish from other opposites like contraries
Opposition in contraries is accidental, not essential. The author contrasts essential opposition with accidental opposition found in contraries.
Refer to the earlier discussion on correlatives (mutāqābīlāt).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه لا مدخل للغير في كونها منافية وإلا لما كانت منافية على الإطلاق، وقد كانت منافية ، هذا خلف. ثمَّ المنافاة الذاتية لا تكون متحققة إلا بين القضيتين المتناقضتين، أما بين القضيتين فلاستحالة التناقض بين المفردين، وقد كان التناقض بين الشيئين بالنسبة إلى محل واحد في زمان واحد من جهة واحدة كما مر . نعم، قد يطلق لفظ النقيض على المفرد من حيث إنه يقابل المفرد الآخر لفظا نحو الوجود واللاوجود مثلًا لكنه لا يكون نقيضًا بالحقيقة. وأما بين المتناقضتين فلاستحالة المنافاة الذاتية في غيرهما، والمنافاة في الغير لا تكون إلا بعرضية شيء آخر كما في المتضادتين مثلا. فإن إحداهما لا تكون منافية للأخرى إلا بعرضية كونها منافية للازمها على ما عرف من قبل في المتقابلات.
For there is no entry for other [than the two contradictory propositions] in its being oppositional, otherwise it would not be oppositional absolutely, and it was oppositional; this is a contradiction. Then essential opposition is not realized except between two contradictory propositions. As for between two propositions, it is because contradiction between singulars is impossible, and contradiction between two things is relative to a single locus, in a single time, from a single aspect, as previously mentioned. Yes, the term 'contradictory' may be applied to a singular in that it verbally opposes another singular, like existence and non-existence, for example, but it is not a contradictory in reality. And as for between the two contradictories, it is because essential opposition in others is impossible, and opposition in others is only due to the accidentally of something else, as in the case of the two contraries, for example. For one of them is not opposed to the other except due to its accidentally opposing its necessary concomitant, as was known previously in the section on correlatives.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and clarifies its essential conditions. The author begins by asserting that opposition (munāfāh) cannot involve any third party; if it did, it would not be absolute opposition. Then he distinguishes essential opposition (al-munāfāh al-dhātiyya), which only occurs between two contradictory propositions (qadiyyatāni mutanāqiḍatāni). This is because contradiction between singular terms is impossible; contradiction requires a single subject, time, and aspect, as known from previous discussion. He acknowledges that the word 'contradictory' (naqīḍ) is sometimes applied to singulars like 'existence' and 'non-existence' based on verbal opposition, but this is not true contradiction. Then he explains that essential opposition cannot exist in other pairs (like contraries) because opposition there occurs accidentally through a relation to a necessary concomitant. For example, two contraries are not directly opposed except because one opposes the necessary concomitant of the other. This ties back to the earlier discussion on correlatives (mutāqābīlāt).

Key Takeaway
Essential contradiction (tanāquḍ) exists only between propositions, not between singular terms; verbal opposition of singulars is not real contradiction.

The Contradictory of Modal Propositions
Author explains the contradictory of modal propositions, addressing necessity and perpetuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory of any modal proposition by applying the rule of difference in quantity, quality, and modality.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a modal proposition must differ in quantity, quality, and modality – not just one.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle but crucial point: how to correctly negate a modal proposition like 'necessarily every A is B'. Many students mistakenly think the contradictory preserves the modality, but the key insight is that the contradictory must differ in modality as well. The author explains why the negation of necessity is not the same as the necessity of negation, and why perpetuity must be considered. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three elements of a modal proposition
Every modal proposition has a subject, a predicate, and a modality (jiha) such as necessity, perpetuity, or possibility. For example, 'necessarily every A is B' has modality 'necessity'.
Modal propositions are called qadaya muwajjaha.
2
State the rule for contradiction
For two propositions to be contradictories (naqid), they must differ in quantity (kamm: universal vs particular), quality (kayf: affirmative vs negative), and modality (jiha). The author emphasizes that simply differing in one or two is insufficient.
This rule applies to all categorical propositions, but modal adds the third dimension.
3
Apply the rule to 'necessarily every A is B'
The contradictory must be: particular (not universal), negative (not affirmative), and the modality must be the negation of necessity (general possibility). Thus the contradictory is 'some A is not B by general possibility' (al-imkan al-'amm).
General possibility means 'neither necessary nor impossible', i.e., not necessarily A is B.
4
Distinguish negation of modality from modality of negation
The contradictory is 'not necessarily every A is B', which is the negation of necessity (salb al-darura), not 'necessarily some A is not B' (darura al-salb). The author stresses they are not the same.
Negation of necessity ≠ necessity of negation.
5
Address the role of perpetuity
For absolute (non-modal) propositions, the contradictory must include a perpetual negation because only perpetual negation contradicts both perpetual and non-perpetual affirmation. The author uses this to illustrate that modality matters.
This example shows how modality enters even simple contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يكون كذلك. فإنا إذا قلنا كل «ب ج» مثلا ولا نتعرض لهذا الحمل أن يكون بالضرورة، أو لا بالضرورة، أو بالدوام، أو لا بالدوام البتة، فلا يناقض هذا القول قولنا بعض «ب» ليس «ب» «ج» على الإطلاق. فإن من المحتمل أن لا يكون دائمًا فبتقدير أن يكون كذلك لا يكون هذا منافيًا لذلك الاحتمال أن يكون هذا في وقت وذلك في وقت آخر بل لا بد من اعتبار قيد الدوام في النقيض؛ إذ السلب الدائم ينافي الإيجاب الدائم وغير الدائم.
[٢] ثم الدوام قد يكون ضروريا، وقد لا يكون لكنه لا يمكن أن يكون مقيدا بأحد هذين القيدين في هذه الصورة لصحة اجتماع المطلقة مع كل واحد منهما على الكذب عندما يكون الصادق القسم الآخر فيلزم أن يعتبر قيد الدوام عند الإطلاق لا على معنى أن هذه الدائمة نفس النقيض بل على معنى أنه لا يمكن الإشارة إلى النقيض إلا به؛ فيقال كل «أب» بالإطلاق، بعض ( أ ) ليس «ب» دائمًا. وكما أن المطلقة لا يناقضها من نوعها شيء البتة فكذلك الغير أي قضية كانت من القضايا الموجّهة فلا يناقضها من نوعها شيء لصحة اجتماعها على الكذب عندما يكون الصادق هو المخالف في الجهة بل الذي يناقضها ما يخالف في الكم والكيف والجهة جميعا؛ فنقيض قولنا بالضرورة كل «أب» ليس بالضرورة كل «أب»، وهذا هو سلب الضرورة التي مرَّ ذكرها. وسلب الضرورة غير ضرورة السلب. وكذلك سلب الدوام غير دوام السلب، وسلب الإمكان غير إمكان السلب؛ غير أنه وهو النقيض قد يمكن أن يعبر بعبارة لا يفهم منها إلا ما هو النقيض بعينه، وقد لا يمكن كما مرّ من قبل. فيعبر إذن بما يدل عليه من العبارات، وإن لم يدل عليه صريحًا فيقال نقيض الضرورة المطلقة، الممكنة العامة المخالفة كما وكيفا لما أن للشيء إما أن يكون ضروري الوجود، أو ضروري العدم، أو لا ضروري الوجود ولا ضروري العدم أيضًا. ولو كان كذلك فلا يرتفع إحدى الضرورتين إلا وأن تبقى الضرورة الأخرى، أو اللا ضرورة من الجانبين وهو الإمكان الخاص. والمشترك بينهما هو الإمكان العام فيقال بالضرورة كل «أب»؛ بعض «أ» ليس (ب) بالإمكان العام. ولا يقال إنه لا يمكن أن يكون مشتركا؛ وقد كان واحد من الأقسام الثلاثة هو الامتناع فإنه هو المشترك بينهما باعتبار ضرورة العدم. وضرورة العدم هي الامتناع لكنها مستلزمة لامتناع الوجود كما أن ضرورة الوجود مستلزمة لامتناع العدم. وعلى هذا في كل ما يكون من قبيل الضرورة المطلقة.
وإن قيل هب أنه كذلك لكن القضية الموجهة أي قضية كانت ضرورية أو لا ضرورية فهي مشتملة على نفس ثبوت الشيء للشيء، وكيفية ذلك الثبوت فتكون مقيدة إما بقيدين أو ثلاثة قيود مثلا؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون النقيض رفع قيد من تلك القيود لا على التعيين. وما ذكرتم لا يكون كذلك فيما ذكرتم وهو الضرورة المطلقة. فنقول قد مرّ من قبل أن النقيض قد يعبر بعبارة يدل عليه وإن لم يدل عليه صريحًا. فالذي مرَّ ذكره إما أن يدل عليه صريحًا؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا كلام فيه، وإما أن لا يدل عليه صريحًا؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يدل عليه وإن لم يدل عليه صريحًا وإلا لا يقوم مقام النقيض وهو رفع قيد من القيود التي ذكرتم، والذي مرَّ ذكره وهو الممكنة العامة المخالفة كما وكيفا فإنه يدل عليه. وذلك لأن الإمكان العام هو سلب الضرورة عن جانبها المخالف؛ وهو سلب ضرورة ثبوت الشيء للشيء فيما نحن فيه. وسلب ضرورة ثبوت الشيء للشيء قد يكون برفع الثبوت، وقد يكون برفع الضرورة فيكون دالا على الرفع الذي يلزم ذلك الرفع. وأما أنه لا يمكن أن يناقضها من نوعها شيء فذلك ظاهر لما أمكن اجتماعهما على الكذب حال ما يكون الصادق قولنا
It is not the case. For if we say 'every B is C' for example and we do not address this predication as being by necessity, or not by necessity, or by perpetuity, or not by perpetuity at all, then this statement does not contradict our statement 'some B is not C' absolutely. For it is possible that it is not perpetual; so assuming it is like that, this is not incompatible with that possibility that this is in one time and that in another time. Rather, the condition of perpetuity must be considered in the contradictory, since perpetual negation is incompatible with perpetual affirmation and non-perpetual affirmation. [2] Then perpetuity may be necessary and may not be necessary, but it cannot be restricted by either of these two conditions in this form because of the possibility of the absolute proposition being true together with each one of them in falsehood when the true is the other division. So it is necessary to consider the condition of perpetuity in the absolute sense, not in the sense that this perpetual proposition is the contradictory itself, but in the sense that the contradictory cannot be indicated except by it. So it is said: 'every A is B' absolutely, 'some A is not B' perpetually. And just as the absolute proposition is not contradicted by anything of its type at all, so also the other, i.e., any modal proposition whatever, is not contradicted by anything of its type because of the possibility of their being true together in falsehood when the true is the one that differs in modality. Rather, what contradicts it is what differs in quantity, quality, and modality all together. Thus the contradictory of our statement 'necessarily every A is B' is 'not necessarily every A is B', and this is the negation of necessity that was mentioned earlier. And the negation of necessity is not the necessity of negation. Likewise, the negation of perpetuity is not the perpetuity of negation, and the negation of possibility is not the possibility of negation. However, the contradictory may be expressed by an expression from which only the contradictory itself is understood, or it may not be possible, as was mentioned before. So one expresses accordingly with expressions that indicate it, even if they do not indicate it explicitly, so it is said: the contradictory of absolute necessity is the general possible that differs in quantity and quality, because a thing is either necessarily existent, or necessarily non-existent, or neither necessarily existent nor necessarily non-existent. And if it were like that, then neither of the two necessities is removed unless the other necessity remains, or the non-necessity from both sides, which is special possibility. And what is common between them is general possibility. So it is said: 'necessarily every A is B', 'some A is not B' by general possibility. And it is not said that it cannot be common; and one of the three divisions is impossibility, for it is common between them by virtue of necessity of non-existence. And necessity of non-existence is impossibility, but it necessitates the impossibility of existence just as necessity of existence necessitates the impossibility of non-existence. And on this basis in everything that is of the category of absolute necessity. And if it is said: suppose it is like that, but the modal proposition, whether necessary or non-necessary, contains the very affirmation of a thing for a thing and the modality of that affirmation, so it is restricted by either two or three conditions, for example. And then it would be necessary that the contradictory be the removal of one of those conditions indeterminately. And what you have mentioned is not like that in what you mentioned, namely absolute necessity. So we say: it has been mentioned before that the contradictory may be expressed by an expression that indicates it even if it does not indicate it explicitly. So what was mentioned either indicates it explicitly—and that is among matters that are not disputed—or it does not indicate it explicitly. And then it is necessary that it indicate it even if it does not indicate it explicitly, otherwise it does not stand in the place of the contradictory, which is the removal of one of the conditions that you mentioned. And what was mentioned, namely the general possible that differs in quantity and quality, does indicate it. That is because general possibility is the negation of necessity from its opposite side; it is the negation of the necessity of the affirmation of a thing for a thing in our case. And the negation of the necessity of the affirmation of a thing for a thing may be by removing the affirmation, or by removing the necessity, so it indicates the removal that necessitates that removal. As for the fact that nothing of its type can contradict it, that is apparent because of the possibility of their being true together in falsehood when the true is our statement.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on how to find the contradictory of modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha), specifically those involving necessity (darura) and perpetuity (dawam). The author begins by clarifying that a simple absolute proposition (mutlaqa) like 'every B is C' does not contradict another absolute proposition like 'some B is not C' because they can both be false when the true proposition is a modal one. For contradiction, one must consider the condition of perpetuity. He then explains that perpetuity can be necessary or not, but in the context of contradiction, the perpetual negation must be used. The key point is that the contradictory must differ in quantity (kamm), quality (kayf), and modality (jiha). For example, the contradictory of 'necessarily every A is B' is 'not necessarily every A is B', which is the negation of necessity (salb al-darura), not the necessity of negation. Similarly, the negation of perpetuity is not the perpetuity of negation. The author then addresses the expression of the contradictory. Sometimes a direct expression is possible, sometimes not. For absolute necessity, one uses the general possible (al-imkan al-'amm) that differs in quantity and quality. He explains that a thing is either necessarily existent, necessarily non-existent, or neither, which is special possibility. The common between them is general possibility. So the contradictory of a necessary universal affirmative is a particular negative with general possibility. He then refutes an objection: a modal proposition contains multiple constraints (affirmation and modality), so its contradictory should remove one of them indeterminately. The author responds that the general possible does indicate the removal of the relevant constraint because it negates the necessity from the opposite side, thereby implying the negation of either the affirmation or the necessity. Finally, he reiterates that no modal proposition of the same type can contradict another because they can both be false when the truth is a different modality.

Key Terms
الموجّهة
النقيض
الضرورة
الدوام
الإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Necessarily some A is not B
B) Not necessarily every A is B
C) Possibly some A is not B
D) Every A is not B necessarily

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a modal proposition must differ in quantity, quality, and modality; for necessity, the modality becomes general possibility (negation of necessity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Contradictories of Modal Propositions: Necessary and Non-Necessary
Author enumerates the contradictory opposites of each type of necessary and non-necessary modal proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any necessary or non-necessary modal proposition as classified by the author.

Core Idea
Every contradictory of a modal proposition flips quantity and quality, and replaces necessity with possibility or permanence.

In previous lessons, you learned about contradictory pairs for non-modal categorical propositions. Now, the author extends this to modal propositions, which include temporal conditions, permanence, and distribution. Understanding these rules is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with theological and jurisprudential statements that involve necessity and possibility.

Guided Path
1
Classify the modal proposition
Identify the type: mashruta (conditioned), waqtiyya (temporal), muntashira (distributed), or wujudiyya la daruriyya (non-necessary existential); note whether it is general or special.
The passage distinguishes between general and special for each type.
2
Determine quantity and quality
Note if the proposition is universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative.
The contradictory always differs in both quantity and quality.
3
Apply the contradictory rule
The contradictory must differ in quantity (universal↔particular) and quality (affirmative↔negative). Additionally, the modality flips: necessity is replaced with general possibility or permanence; non-necessity is replaced with permanence or necessity.
Refer to the author's pattern for each specific type.
4
Verify with the author's example
For each type, the author gives a concrete example using variables A and B. Check that your derived contradictory matches the one stated.
The examples mirror the earlier treatment for the subject side.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٦] وفي المشروطة الخاصة من جانب المحمول فذلك هو الممكنة العامة المخافلة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفا فيقال بالضرورة كل «أب» لا دائما بل ما دام (ب)، بعض (أ) ليس (ب) حين هو «ب» بالإمكان العام أو بعضه «ب» دائمًا على مثال ما مر من قبل في جانب الموضوع.
[۷] وأما في الوقتية العامة فذلك هو الممكنة العامة الوقتية المخالفة كما وكيفا فيقال بالضرورة كل «أب» وقت كذا، بعض «أ» ليس «ب» وقت كذا بالإمكان العام.
[۸] وفي الوقتية الخاصة هو الممكنة العامة الوقتية المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الوقتية الموافقة كيفا.
[۹] وفي المنتشرة العامة هو الممكنة العامة المخالفة كما وكيفا فيقال بالضرة كل «أب» في وقت من الأوقات، بعض «أ» ليس ب أصلا بالإمكان العام.
[١٠] وفي المنتشرة الخاصة هو الممكنة العامة المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفًا فيقال بالضرورة كل «أب» لا دائما بل في وقت من الأوقات، بعض «أ» ليس «ب» أصلا بالإمكان العام أو بعضه «أ» دائمًا.
[١١] وأما في الوجودية اللا ضرورية فذلك هو الدائمة المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الضرورة الموافقة كيفًا فيقال كل «أب» لا بالضرورة، بعض «أ» ليس «ب» دائمًا أو بعضه «ب» بالضرورة. هذا هو الكلام في الضرورة واللا ضرورة من الجهات.
[6] And concerning the special conditioned (al-mashruta al-khassa) from the side of the predicate, that is the general possible (al-mumkina al-‘amma) differing in quantity and quality, or the permanent (al-da’ima) agreeing in quality. So it is said: necessarily every A is B, not always but as long as B; some A is not B when it is B by general possibility, or some of it is B always, following the example given previously for the subject side. [7] As for the general temporal (al-waqtiyya al-‘amma), that is the general temporal possible differing in quantity and quality, so it is said: necessarily every A is B at such a time; some A is not B at such a time by general possibility. [8] And concerning the special temporal (al-waqtiyya al-khassa), it is the general temporal possible differing in quantity and quality, or the permanent temporal agreeing in quality. [9] And concerning the general distributed (al-muntashira al-‘amma), it is the general possible differing in quantity and quality, so it is said: necessarily every A is B at some time; some A is not B at all by general possibility. [10] And concerning the special distributed (al-muntashira al-khassa), it is the general possible differing in quantity and quality, or the permanent agreeing in quality, so it is said: necessarily every A is B, not always but at some time; some A is not B at all by general possibility, or some of it is A always. [11] And as for the non-necessary existential (al-wujudiyya al-la daruriyya), that is the permanent differing in quantity and quality, or the necessary agreeing in quality, so it is said: every A is B, not necessarily; some A is not B always, or some of it is B necessarily. This is the discussion concerning necessity and non-necessity among the modalities.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory opposites for various kinds of modal propositions within the category of necessity and non-necessity. The author systematically treats each type: the special conditioned from the predicate side, the general and special temporal, the general and special distributed, and the non-necessary existential. For each, he states what its contradictory is in terms of quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative), and the modal qualifier (necessary, possible, permanent, etc.). The pattern is that the contradictory of a universal necessary affirmative is a particular possible negative, or sometimes a permanent proposition. Conversely, for non-necessary existential propositions, the contradictory involves permanence or necessity. The author notes that this parallels the earlier treatment for the subject side. The aim is to show how to correctly oppose any modal claim in disputation.

Modal contradictories always involve a change in quantity, quality, and modal status, with necessity giving way to possibility or permanence.

Finding Contradictories of Temporal Propositions
Enumerates contradictory opposites for temporal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any temporal proposition from the categories of permanent, conventional, temporal, or distributed using the rules of quantitative and qualitative opposition along with temporal specifications.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a universal temporal proposition is a particular negative with the same temporal condition removed.

This passage details how to negate propositions that involve temporal conditions like 'always', 'as long as', 'at a certain time', or 'at some unspecified time'. Understanding this is crucial for constructing valid arguments in logic, especially in kalām where temporal arguments about the world's temporality are common. The pattern is consistent: the contradictory of a universal affirmative temporal proposition is a particular negative, with the temporal condition either removed or replaced by a general absolute temporal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the proposition is permanent, conventional (general or special, from subject or predicate side), temporal (general or special), or distributed (general or special). The passage enumerates each type with its own contradictory.
Conventional propositions lack necessity, while conditioned ones have it.
2
Extract the temporal qualifier
Find the temporal condition: 'always', 'as long as A', 'as long as B', 'at such a time (specified or unspecified)'. This qualifier will be modified or removed in the contradictory.
3
Apply the quantitative and qualitative change
The contradictory of a universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B') is a particular negative ('Some A is not B'). This is the standard immediate inference.
Quality and quantity both change.
4
Adjust the temporal condition
Remove the temporal restriction from the original and replace it with the appropriate 'general absolute' or 'general absolute temporal' as indicated in the passage. For permanent propositions, use the general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma). For conventional, use the general absolute temporal (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma al-hiniyya).
For special conventional, there is an option of the permanent agreeing in quality.
5
Verify with the passage examples
Check your result against the explicit examples given in the text: e.g., 'Every A is B always' contradicts with 'Some A is not B by general absoluteness'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of contradictory opposites, now for the category of permanence (al-dawam) and non-permanence. The author starts with the absolute permanent (al-da’ima al-mutlaqa) and explains that its contradictory is the general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma) differing in quantity and quality: from 'Every A is B always' to 'Some A is not B absolutely.' Then he treats the conventional propositions (al-‘urfiyya) from both subject and predicate sides, both general and special. For these, the contradictory is either the general absolute temporal (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma al-hiniyya) or the permanent. He notes that the only difference between the conditioned (mashruta) and conventional is necessity: the former implies necessity, the latter does not. Then he handles temporal and distributed propositions, showing that their contradictory is also the general absolute, with appropriate time qualifiers. The pattern is consistent: the contradictory of a universal permanent affirmative is a particular absolute negative, with variations for temporal restrictions.

Types of Antecedents in Conditional Propositions
Classification of antecedents into two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an antecedent by itself and an antecedent due to something else in a conditional proposition.

Core Idea
The antecedent is not always temporally prior; it can be prior in causation or implication.

In conditional propositions, the antecedent is the 'if' part, but its logical priority can vary. This lesson introduces a key distinction: whether the antecedent is prior by its own nature or due to its relation to the consequent. Understanding this distinction helps you analyze the direction of implication in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent
In a conditional like 'If A then B', locate the part after 'if'. That is the antecedent (muqaddam).
2
Check if the antecedent is prior by itself
Ask: Does the antecedent naturally precede the consequent in a causal or temporal order? If yes, it is an antecedent by itself (muqaddam li dhatihi). Example: 'If the sun has risen, then it is daytime.' The sunrise causes daytime, so antecedent is prior by itself.
3
Check if the antecedent is prior due to something else
If the antecedent's priority depends on the consequent or something related, it is an antecedent due to something else (muqaddam li ghayrihi). Example: 'If it is daytime, then the sun has risen.' Here, daytime depends on sunrise, so the antecedent is prior due to its relation to the consequent.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take a conditional from fiqh: 'If the prayer is valid, then the ablution was performed.' The validity of prayer depends on ablution, so the antecedent is prior due to the consequent. This is an antecedent due to something else.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في المقدم والتالي ] واعلم أن المقدم في القضايا الشرطية المتّصلة قد يكون مقدما لذاته وقد يكون مقدما لغيره؛ وذلك الغير قد يكون من جملة ما يتعلق بالمقدم، وقد يكون
Section: [On the antecedent and consequent]. And know that the antecedent in conditional propositions may be an antecedent by itself or an antecedent due to something else; and that something else may be among the things related to the antecedent, or may be...
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This short passage introduces a classification of antecedents (muqaddam) in conditional propositions (shartiyya muttasila). There are two types: the antecedent that is prior by itself (muqaddam li dhatihi) and the antecedent that is prior due to something else (muqaddam li ghayrihi). The 'something else' can be part of what pertains to the antecedent. For example, in the conditional 'If it is light, then the sun has risen', the antecedent 'it is light' is prior by itself. In 'If the sun has risen, then it is light', the antecedent 'the sun has risen' might be considered prior due to its consequence. This distinction is important for understanding the logical structure of conditionals and the direction of implication.

Key Terms
مقدم
تالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Neither
D) Both

Key Takeaway
The antecedent in a conditional can be either essential (by itself) or accidental (due to something else), depending on whether its logical priority is intrinsic or derived from its relation to the consequent.

Contradictories of Necessity-Modal Disjunctives
Rules for contradictories of necessity-modal disjunctives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contradictory of any necessity-modal disjunctive proposition by applying the patterns given in the text.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a necessity-modal disjunctive is either a general possibility or a permanent that agrees in quality.

Modal disjunctive propositions are common in Islamic logic, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh. This lesson focuses on those qualifying by necessity (الضرورة). The text specifies three types: the permanent (الدائمة), the general temporal (الوقتية العامة), and the specific temporal (الوقتية الخاصة). Each has a unique contradictory that alters the quantity or quality. Mastering these will sharpen your ability to evaluate arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the necessity-modal type
Determine whether the proposition is permanent (دائمة) or temporal (وقتية). If temporal, decide if it is general or specific.
2
Permanent agreeing in quality
For this type, the contradictory is either 'Not every A is either B or C by general possibility' or 'Some A is either B or C always.'
There are two possible forms.
3
General temporal (الوقتية العامة)
The contradictory is a general possible temporal proposition opposite in both quantity and quality. Example: 'Necessarily, every A is either B or C at time t' contradicts 'Not every A is either B or C at time t by general possibility.'
4
Specific temporal (الوقتية الخاصة)
The contradictory is either the general possible temporal opposite or the permanent agreeing in quality.
Two options again.
5
Apply the pattern
Use the formulas with variables A, B, C to derive the contradictory for any necessity-modal disjunctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» لا دائما بل ما دام «أ»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» حين هو «أ» بالإمكان العام، أو بعضه إما «ب» وإما (ج) دائما . [١٨] وأما في الوقتية العامة فهو الممكنة العامة الوقتية المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» وقت كذا، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» وقت كذا بالإمكان العام. [١٩] وفي الوقتية الخاصة هو الممكنة العامة الوقتية المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» لا دائما بل وقت كذا، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما (ج) وقت كذا بالإمكان العام، أو بعضه إما «ب» وإما «ج» دائما. [٢٠] وعلى هذا في الغير من القضايا الموجهة بجهة الضرورة. [٢١] وأما في الموجهة بجهة الدوام ففي الدائمة المطلقة هو المطلقة / العامة المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال دائما كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» بالإطلاق العام. [٢٢] وفي العرفية العامة هو المطلقة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» ما دام «أ»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» حين هو «أ» بالإطلاق العام. [٢٣] وفي العرفية الخاصة هو المطلقة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال كل «أ» إما (ب) وإما (ج) حين هو «أ» بالإطلاق العام ، أو بعضه إما «ب» وإما «ج» دائما . [٢٤] وعلى هذا في الغير من القضايا الباقية فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
The permanent (al-da'ima) agreeing in quality, as it is said: 'Necessarily, every A is either B or C, not always but as long as A; it is not that every A is either B or C when it is A by general possibility, or some of it is either B or C always.' [18] As for the general temporal (al-waqtiyya al-'amma), it is the general possible temporal contrary in quantity and quality, as it is said: 'Necessarily, every A is either B or C at such a time; it is not that every A is either B or C at such a time by general possibility.' [19] As for the specific temporal (al-waqtiyya al-khassa), it is the general possible temporal contrary in quantity and quality, or the permanent agreeing in quality, as it is said: 'Necessarily, every A is either B or C, not always but at such a time; it is not that every A is either B or C at such a time by general possibility, or some of it is either B or C always.' [20] And similarly for other propositions modally qualified by necessity. [21] As for those qualified by permanence (al-dawam), in the absolute permanent (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa) it is the absolute general contrary in quantity and quality, as it is said: 'Always, every A is either B or C; it is not that every A is either B or C by absolute generality.' [22] In the general customary (al-'urfiyya al-'amma), it is the absolute general temporal contrary in quantity and quality, as it is said: 'Every A is either B or C as long as A; it is not that every A is either B or C when it is A by absolute generality.' [23] In the specific customary (al-'urfiyya al-khassa), it is the absolute general temporal contrary in quantity and quality, or the permanent agreeing in quality, as it is said: 'Every A is either B or C when it is A by absolute generality, or some of it is either B or C always.' [24] And similarly for the remaining propositions, so consider what you have learned, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the contradictories (نقيض) of modal disjunctive propositions (القضايا المنفصلة الموجهة). The author walks through each type of modality and specifies the contradictory in terms of quantity (كيفا) and quality (كما). He begins with the 'permanent agreeing in quality' (الدائمة الموافقة كيفا), which is a type of necessity proposition that is not always but as long as the subject exists; its contradictory is expressed in terms of general possibility (الإمكان العام). Then he covers general temporal (الوقتية العامة) and specific temporal (الوقتية الخاصة), giving the contradictories accordingly. Next, he transitions to propositions qualified by permanence (جهة الدوام) and examines the absolute permanent (الدائمة المطلقة) and the customary (العرفية) general and specific types. In each case, he provides a formula using the variables A, B, C to illustrate the logical structure. The passage concludes by stating that similar reasoning applies to all remaining modal propositions, urging the student to analogize and seek divine assistance. The enumeration follows a consistent pattern: for each modal type, the contradictory is either contrary in quantity and quality or a permanent agreeing in quality, depending on the case.
