Contradictories of Permanence-Modal Disjunctives
Rules for contradictories of permanence-modal disjunctives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contradictory of any permanence-modal disjunctive proposition (absolute permanent, general customary, specific customary) by applying the patterns given in the text.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a permanence-modal disjunctive is either an absolute general opposite or a temporal absolute, except for specific customary which also allows a permanent agreeing in quality.

This lesson continues the exploration of modal disjunctive contradictories, now focusing on propositions qualified by permanence (الدوام). The text distinguishes three types: the absolute permanent (الدائمة المطلقة), the general customary (العرفية العامة), and the specific customary (العرفية الخاصة). Each has a specific contradictory formula. These patterns are crucial for logical analysis in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the permanence-modal type
Determine whether the proposition is absolute permanent (دائمة مطلقة), general customary (عرفية عامة), or specific customary (عرفية خاصة).
2
Absolute permanent (الدائمة المطلقة)
The contradictory is an absolute general proposition opposite in quantity and quality. Example: 'Always, every A is either B or C' contradicts 'Not every A is either B or C by absolute generality.'
3
General customary (العرفية العامة)
The contradictory is an absolute general temporal proposition opposite in quantity and quality. Example: 'Every A is either B or C as long as A' contradicts 'Not every A is either B or C when it is A by absolute generality.'
4
Specific customary (العرفية الخاصة)
The contradictory is either the absolute general temporal opposite or the permanent agreeing in quality.
Two options.
5
Apply the pattern
Use the formulas with variables A, B, C to derive the contradictory for any permanence-modal disjunctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» لا دائما بل ما دام «أ»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» حين هو «أ» بالإمكان العام، أو بعضه إما «ب» وإما (ج) دائما . [١٨] وأما في الوقتية العامة فهو الممكنة العامة الوقتية المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» وقت كذا، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» وقت كذا بالإمكان العام. [١٩] وفي الوقتية الخاصة هو الممكنة العامة الوقتية المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال بالضرورة كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» لا دائما بل وقت كذا، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما (ج) وقت كذا بالإمكان العام، أو بعضه إما «ب» وإما «ج» دائما. [٢٠] وعلى هذا في الغير من القضايا الموجهة بجهة الضرورة. [٢١] وأما في الموجهة بجهة الدوام ففي الدائمة المطلقة هو المطلقة / العامة المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال دائما كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» بالإطلاق العام. [٢٢] وفي العرفية العامة هو المطلقة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا كما يقال كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» ما دام «أ»، ليس كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» حين هو «أ» بالإطلاق العام. [٢٣] وفي العرفية الخاصة هو المطلقة العامة الحينية المخالفة كما وكيفا أو الدائمة الموافقة كيفا كما يقال كل «أ» إما (ب) وإما (ج) حين هو «أ» بالإطلاق العام ، أو بعضه إما «ب» وإما «ج» دائما . [٢٤] وعلى هذا في الغير من القضايا الباقية فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
The permanent (al-da'ima) agreeing in quality, as it is said: 'Necessarily, every A is either B or C, not always but as long as A; it is not that every A is either B or C when it is A by general possibility, or some of it is either B or C always.' [18] As for the general temporal (al-waqtiyya al-'amma), it is the general possible temporal contrary in quantity and quality, as it is said: 'Necessarily, every A is either B or C at such a time; it is not that every A is either B or C at such a time by general possibility.' [19] As for the specific temporal (al-waqtiyya al-khassa), it is the general possible temporal contrary in quantity and quality, or the permanent agreeing in quality, as it is said: 'Necessarily, every A is either B or C, not always but at such a time; it is not that every A is either B or C at such a time by general possibility, or some of it is either B or C always.' [20] And similarly for other propositions modally qualified by necessity. [21] As for those qualified by permanence (al-dawam), in the absolute permanent (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa) it is the absolute general contrary in quantity and quality, as it is said: 'Always, every A is either B or C; it is not that every A is either B or C by absolute generality.' [22] In the general customary (al-'urfiyya al-'amma), it is the absolute general temporal contrary in quantity and quality, as it is said: 'Every A is either B or C as long as A; it is not that every A is either B or C when it is A by absolute generality.' [23] In the specific customary (al-'urfiyya al-khassa), it is the absolute general temporal contrary in quantity and quality, or the permanent agreeing in quality, as it is said: 'Every A is either B or C when it is A by absolute generality, or some of it is either B or C always.' [24] And similarly for the remaining propositions, so consider what you have learned, and with Allah is success.

Types of Antecedent in Conditional Propositions
Defines antecedent types and modal classification of conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an antecedent by itself and an antecedent for something else in a conditional proposition, and classify the conditional as absolute necessary or conditioned.

Core Idea
The antecedent may be intrinsic or extrinsic, determining whether the conditional is absolute necessary or conditioned.

In this passage, the author explains that the antecedent (المقدم) in a conjunctive conditional is not always the same. It can be antecedent by itself, meaning the implication follows from its essence, or antecedent for something else, where the implication depends on an external condition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly evaluating conditional arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent in the conditional
Locate the part that precedes 'if' in meaning. For example, in 'If it is day, then the sun has risen', the antecedent is 'it is day'.
The antecedent may be a simple proposition or a compound one, but focus on its role as the condition.
2
Determine if the antecedent is antecedent by itself or for something else
Ask: Does the implication follow from the very meaning of the antecedent, or does it require an added condition? If the implication is intrinsic (e.g., 'If a person is human, then they are rational'), it is by itself. If external conditions are needed (e.g., 'If a body is wet, then it has been rained upon' – maybe the wetness is from a flood), it is for something else.
The 'something else' can be related to the antecedent, consequent, or neither, as the text states.
3
Classify the conditional as absolute necessary or conditioned
If the implication follows from the essence of the antecedent (by itself), the conditional is absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة). Otherwise, it is conditioned (مشروطة بشرط).
The absolute necessary implies that whenever the antecedent holds, the consequent holds necessarily, without extra conditions.
4
Test with an example from Kalam
Consider the argument: 'If the world is temporally originated (حادث), then it requires a cause (مؤثر).' The antecedent 'world is temporally originated' is antecedent by itself because the need for a cause follows directly from its temporality. Hence this conditional is absolute necessary.
This example is used in proofs for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في المقدم والتالي ] واعلم أن المقدم في القضايا الشرطية المتّصلة قد يكون مقدما لذاته وقد يكون مقدما لغيره؛ وذلك الغير قد يكون من جملة ما يتعلق بالمقدم، وقد يكون من جملة ما يتعلق بالتالي، وقد يكون من جملة ما لا يتعلق بالمقدم أصلا ولا بالتالي كذلك. ولو كان كذلك لكانت الشرطية إما أن تكون ضرورية مطلقة يكون اللزوم فيها بحسب ذات المقدم، وإما أن لا تكون بل تكون مشروطة بشرط. وذلك الشرط قد يكون من جانب المقدم؛ وفيه من التعدد ما فيه منها ما تقدم ذكره؛ وذلك على قياس الحمليات لما أن الحملية في قوة الشرطية كما مر. ومنها ما لا يكون على ذلك القياس كما يقال [١] في الضرورية المطلقة بالضرورة كلَّما كان كل «أب» فــ «ج د»؛ أو يقال بالضرورة كلَّما كان كل «أ ب فكل ج د [٢] وفي المشروطة العامة يقال بالضرورة كلما كان «أب»، بالضرورة كان (ج) د ما دام «أب بالضرورة أو يقال بالضرورة كلَّما كان كل «أب بالضرورة كان (ج) د ما دام كل أب بالضرورة. [٣] وفي المشروطة الخاصة يقال بالضرورة كلَّما كان «أب»، بالضرورة كان «ج د» لا دائما بل ما دام كل أب بالضرورة أو يقال بالضرورة كلما كان كل «أب»، بالضرورة كان (ج) د لا دائما بل ما دام كل «أب» بالضرورة، وعلى هذا فإنه يتعدد بحسب تعدد وصف المقدم بالضرورة أو وصفه بالدوام وغير ذلك في كل هذا صورة من الصور المذكورة في الشرطيات المتلازمة. وكما أنه متعدد من الجانب وهو جانب المقدم فكذلك من جانب التالي. ثمَّ الشرطية وإن كانت مختلفة باختلاف المقدم والتالي فلا يمكن أن يختلف النقيض في الجهة بل النقيض أو ما يدل عليه. فذلك هو الذي مرَّ ذكره في الكل من العامة والخاصة وغيرهما، ولنقتصر على هذا القدر فإن فيه كفايته لأهل التحصيل فلا حاجة إلى التطويل.
Section: [On the Antecedent and Consequent] Know that the antecedent in conjunctive conditional propositions (al-shartiyya al-muttasila) may be an antecedent by itself (li-dhatihi) or an antecedent for something else (li-ghayrihi). That something else may be part of what pertains to the antecedent, part of what pertains to the consequent, or part of what pertains neither to the antecedent nor to the consequent. If it is like that, then the conditional is either an absolute necessary (daruriyya mutlaqa), where the implication (luzum) is according to the essence of the antecedent, or it is not such but is conditional on a condition. That condition may be on the side of the antecedent; it has a multiplicity which includes what was previously mentioned; and that is analogous to categorical (hamliyya) propositions, because the categorical is equivalent to a conditional as previously mentioned. And among them there is what is not on that analogy, as is said [1] in the absolute necessary: 'Necessarily, whenever A-B, then C-D'; or it is said 'Necessarily, whenever A-B, then C-D'. [2] In the general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma), it is said: 'Necessarily, whenever A-B, necessarily C-D as long as A-B necessarily'; or it is said 'Necessarily, whenever A-B necessarily, C-D as long as A-B necessarily'. [3] In the specific conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa), it is said: 'Necessarily, whenever A-B, necessarily C-D not always but as long as A-B necessarily'; or it is said 'Necessarily, whenever A-B necessarily, C-D not always but as long as A-B necessarily'. And on this, it is multiplied according to the multiplicity of the description of the antecedent by necessity or by permanence, etc., in each of these forms mentioned in the correlative conditionals (al-shartiyyat al-mutalazima). And just as it is multiple on one side, namely the side of the antecedent, so also on the side of the consequent. Then, although the conditional differs according to the difference of antecedent and consequent, the contradictory cannot differ in modality; rather the contradictory or what indicates it. That is what was previously mentioned in all of the general, specific, and others. Let us suffice with this amount, for it is sufficient for those seeking accomplishment, so there is no need for lengthening.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the antecedent (المقدم) and consequent (التالي) in conjunctive conditional propositions (القضايا الشرطية المتصلة). The author first distinguishes between an antecedent that is antecedent by itself (مقداما لذاته) and one that is antecedent for something else (مقداما لغيره). The 'something else' can be related to the antecedent, the consequent, or neither. Based on this, the conditional's implication (اللزوم) is either absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة) if the implication follows from the essence of the antecedent, or it is conditioned (مشروطة بشرط). The condition may be on the antecedent side, and there is multiplicity as previously discussed in categorical propositions (الحمليات), because a categorical is equivalent to a conditional. The author then provides examples with variables A, B, C, D. For the absolute necessary conditional, it is simply 'Necessarily whenever A-B, then C-D'. For the general conditional (المشروطة العامة), the implication holds as long as A-B is necessary. For the specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة), the implication holds not always but as long as A-B is necessary. The passage explains that these forms multiply based on how the antecedent is described (by necessity, permanence, etc.) and similarly for the consequent. Importantly, the contradictory of the conditional does not differ in modality; it remains consistent. The author concludes that this is sufficient for the diligent student.

الشرطية المتصلة

Modal Varieties of Conditional Propositions
Describes three modal types with variable notation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three modal types of conditional propositions (absolute necessary, general conditional, specific conditional) and represent them using logical notation with Arabic variables.

Core Idea
Conditionals vary by modality: absolute necessary, general conditional, and specific conditional each have distinct notation.

After establishing the two main categories of conditionals, the author provides detailed examples using variables أ،ب،ج،د to illustrate the modal types. These include the absolute necessary conditional, where the implication holds without any restriction; the general conditional, where it holds as long as the antecedent is necessary; and the specific conditional, where the implication holds only when the antecedent is necessary but not always. Mastering these notations is essential for formalizing logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional from the pattern
The passage gives explicit patterns: for absolute necessary, 'Necessarily, whenever A-B, then C-D'. For general conditional, it adds 'as long as A-B necessarily'. For specific conditional, it adds 'not always but as long as A-B necessarily'.
Pay attention to the presence or absence of 'as long as' and 'not always'.
2
Note the role of necessity and duration
In the general conditional, the implication is necessary as long as the antecedent is necessary. In specific, the implication is necessary only during the duration that the antecedent is necessary, but not always (i.e., not permanently).
This distinction is subtle but crucial for modal logic.
3
Apply the notation to an Islamic example
Take the categorical proposition 'body is created' as أب and 'contingent requires a cause' as جد. The absolute necessary conditional: 'Necessarily, whenever a body is created, then it is contingent and requires a cause.' This is intrinsic.
Here the implication is always true without extra conditions.
4
Understand that the contradictory does not change modality
The passage emphasizes that the contradictory of the conditional does not differ in modality. That is, if the conditional is necessary, its negation is not a different modal type.
This is consistent with the rules of modal logic in the tradition.

The author first distinguishes between an antecedent that is antecedent by itself (مقداما لذاته) and one that is antecedent for something else (مقداما لغيره). The 'something else' can be related to the antecedent, the consequent, or neither. Based on this, the conditional's implication (اللزوم) is either absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة) if the implication follows from the essence of the antecedent, or it is conditioned (مشروطة بشرط). The condition may be on the antecedent side, and there is multiplicity as previously discussed in categorical propositions (الحمليات), because a categorical is equivalent to a conditional. The author then provides examples with variables A, B, C, D. For the absolute necessary conditional, it is simply 'Necessarily whenever A-B, then C-D'. For the general conditional (المشروطة العامة), the implication holds as long as A-B is necessary. For the specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة), the implication holds not always but as long as A-B is necessary. The passage explains that these forms multiply based on how the antecedent is described (by necessity, permanence, etc.) and similarly for the consequent. Importantly, the contradictory of the conditional does not differ in modality; it remains consistent. The author concludes that this is sufficient for the diligent student.

Modalities of Conditional Propositions
Author classifies modal conditionals and their multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the three types of modal conditional propositions (absolute necessary, general conditioned, special conditioned) and distinguish their modalities based on the antecedent's role.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions have modally distinct types based on whether implication holds essentially or under a condition.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions (shartiyyat) are central for reasoning about causal and conditional relationships, such as in usul al-fiqh where legal rulings depend on conditions. This lesson dissects the modalities of conditionals, differentiating between absolute necessity (where implication holds by the essence of the antecedent) and conditioned necessity (where implication holds under a condition on the antecedent or consequent). You will learn to parse and classify these modalities using the examples provided in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent in the examples
The author uses variables: 'A is B' as the antecedent, and 'C is D' as the consequent. In the absolute necessary conditional, the implication holds whenever the antecedent holds, without further condition.
An antecedent is the 'if' part; consequent is the 'then' part.
2
Distinguish absolute necessary from conditioned conditionals
An absolute necessary conditional (daruriyya mutlaqa) has the implication based purely on the essence of the antecedent. Conditioned conditionals (mashruta) add a condition: the implication holds only under that condition.
The condition is often expressed by phrases like 'ma dama' (as long as).
3
Locate modal markers in the Arabic
Look for words like 'bidarura' (necessarily), 'ma dama' (as long as), and 'la da'iman' (not always). These indicate the modality.
The general conditioned uses 'ma dama' without 'la da'iman'; the special conditioned adds 'la da'iman'.
4
Compare general conditioned vs special conditioned
General conditioned: implication holds necessarily as long as the antecedent is necessary. Special conditioned: implication holds necessarily but not always, only as long as the antecedent is necessary (i.e., the necessity is not permanent).
The special conditioned is weaker: it does not claim the implication holds at all times, only during the antecedent's necessity.
5
Understand the multiplication of modalities
The multiplicity arises from describing the antecedent with necessity or permanence, and similar variation on the consequent side. The author indicates that the contradictory does not change in modality; the modality of the contradictory is the same as the original.
This multiplication is analogous to the treatment of categorical propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من جملة ما يتعلق بالتالي، وقد يكون من جملة ما لا يتعلق بالمقدم أصلا ولا بالتالي كذلك. ولو كان كذلك لكانت الشرطية إما أن تكون ضرورية مطلقة يكون اللزوم فيها بحسب ذات المقدم، وإما أن لا تكون بل تكون مشروطة بشرط. وذلك الشرط قد يكون من جانب المقدم؛ وفيه من التعدد ما فيه منها ما تقدم ذكره؛ وذلك على قياس الحمليات لما أن الحملية في قوة الشرطية كما مر. ومنها ما لا يكون على ذلك القياس كما يقال [١] في الضرورية المطلقة بالضرورة كلَّما كان كل «أب» فــ «ج د»؛ أو يقال بالضرورة كلَّما كان كل «أ ب فكل ج د [۲] وفي المشروطة العامة يقال بالضرورة كلما كان «أب»، بالضرورة كان (ج) د ما دام «أب بالضرورة أو يقال بالضرورة كلَّما كان كل «أب بالضرورة كان (ج) د ما دام كل أب بالضرورة. Some of what pertains to the consequent, and sometimes it pertains to what is not related to the antecedent at all nor to the consequent likewise. And if it were so, the conditional would either be an absolute necessary [conditional] where the implication is according to the essence of the antecedent, or it would not be, but would be conditioned by a condition. That condition may be on the side of the antecedent; and there is multiplicity in it as there is in what was previously mentioned; and that is analogous to the categoricals, since the categorical is in the force of a conditional as has passed. And among them there is what is not analogous to that, as is said [1] in the absolute necessary: necessarily, whenever every A is B, then C is D; or it is said: necessarily, whenever every A is B, then every C is D. [2] And in the general conditioned: it is said: necessarily, whenever A is B, necessarily C is D as long as A is B necessarily; or it is said: necessarily, whenever every A is necessarily B, C is D necessarily as long as every A is necessarily B. [3] And in the special conditioned: it is said: necessarily, whenever A is B, necessarily C is D, not always, but as long as every A is necessarily B; or it is said: necessarily, whenever every A is B, necessarily C is D, not always, but as long as every A is necessarily B. And upon this, it multiplies according to the multiplicity of the description of the antecedent by necessity or its description by permanence and other things in every one of these images among the images mentioned in the co-implicative conditionals. And just as it is multiple on one side — that is the side of the antecedent — so too on the side of the consequent. Then, although the conditional differs with the difference of the antecedent and consequent, the contradictory cannot differ in modality; rather, the contradictory or what indicates it is the same. That is what has been mentioned before regarding the universal, the particular, and others. And we will limit ourselves to this extent, for it is sufficient for those seeking accomplishment, so there is no need for elongation.

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional propositions (shartiyyat) according to their modality. It begins by noting that the conditional relation (luzum) can be either absolute (daruriyya mutlaqa), where the implication is based on the essence of the antecedent, or conditioned (mashruta) by an additional condition on the antecedent or consequent. The author then provides examples using logical variables: for absolute necessity, the implication holds unconditionally; for general conditioned (mashruta 'amma), the implication holds necessarily as long as the antecedent exists necessarily; for special conditioned (mashruta khassa), the implication holds necessarily only as long as the antecedent exists necessarily, but not always (i.e., not permanently). The multiplicity arises from describing the antecedent with necessity or permanence. The same applies to the consequent side. Importantly, the contradictory of a conditional does not vary in modality; the modality of the contradictory is the same as that of the original. The author concludes that this classification suffices for serious students.

Classifying Propositions: The 36 Types and the Real vs. External Modality
Classification of propositions for contraposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition according to its subject and predicate modality (haqiqi/khariji) and their form (affirmative/privative/negative), and identify which of the 36 types it belongs to.

Core Idea
Thirty-six proposition types arise from combining three subject forms with three predicate forms and four modality combinations.

This passage introduces a systematic classification of propositions used in immediate inferences like contraposition. The author explains that propositions can be considered according to 'real' essence (haqiqiyya) or 'external' existence (kharijiyya), and their subject and predicate can each be affirmative, privative, or negative. By multiplying these dimensions, we get 36 conceptually distinct proposition types. Understanding this grid is essential for correctly applying contraposition and other immediate inference operations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two foundational modalities
The author distinguishes between 'absolute real' (haqiqiyya mutlaqa) and 'absolute external' (kharijiyya mutlaqa). In the first, both subject and predicate are taken according to their essences; in the second, both are taken according to actual external existence.
These modalities affect the truth conditions and the behavior under contraposition.
2
Recognize the three forms of a term
The subject of a universal affirmative can be affirmative (محصل), privative (معدول), or negative (سلب). The same applies to the predicate. These are formal properties of the term itself, not truth values.
Examples: 'every human' (affirmative), 'every non-human' (privative), 'every nothing' (negative).
3
Combine subject and predicate forms
With 3 possibilities for the subject and 3 for the predicate, we get 3 × 3 = 9 types of propositions based solely on the form of the terms.
These 9 types are independent of modality.
4
Introduce the four modality combinations
The author then lists four ways the terms can be taken: both real, both external, subject real and predicate external, or the opposite. This gives 4 modality combinations for each of the 9 form types.
Thus 9 × 4 = 36 proposition types, all differing in concept.
5
Understand the relationships between types
The author notes that these 36 types are conceptually distinct, but some entail each other and some have partial generality relationships (عموم وخصوص).
This grid is a tool for analyzing logical relations in immediate inferences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فالكلام في عكس النقيض وما يتعلق به مختلف؛ والجمهور من السلف والخلف لما اطلعوا على أقسام القضايا بحسب الحصر والإهمال والعدول والتحصيل لم يتعرضوا لبيانها على سبيل التفصيل في عكس النقيض، وهذا القوم من المتقدمين تعرضوا لذلك لا على تبيين الاستقامة فقالوا : إذا كان موضوع القضية بحسب الحقيقة ومحمولها كذلك سميت بالحقيقية المطلقة، وإذا كان موضوعها بحسب الوجود في الخارج ومحمولها كذلك سميت بالخارجية المطلقة. وقسَّموا من بعد على حسب ذلك مع اعتبار العدول والتحصيل والسلب في الطرفين لاختلاف الحكم بحسب اختلاف هذه الاعتبارات في العكوس فقالوا إن موضوع الموجبة الكلية لا تخلو من أن يكون محصلا أو معدولاً أو سلبًا وعلى التقادير كلها فالمحمول على أحد الأنحاء الثلاثة. فتحصل إذن تسعة أنواع من القضايا، وكل واحد منها على أربعة أقسام : فإن الطرفين إما أن يكون بحسب الحقيقة، أو بحسب الوجود الخارجي أو الموضوع بحسب الحقيقة والمحمول بحسب الوجود الخارجي أو بالعكس فيحصل ستة وثلاثون نوعا من القضايا متغايرة المفهوم وإن كانت يتلازم بعضها ويتخالف بعضها بالعموم والخصوص.
And in summary, the discussion on contraposition and what pertains to it is disputed; the majority of early and later scholars, when they became aware of the divisions of propositions according to quantification, negation, privation, and affirmation, did not undertake to explain them in detail concerning contraposition. But this group of earlier scholars did undertake that, not to demonstrate correctness, and they said: If the subject of a proposition is according to reality and its predicate likewise, it is called the absolute real proposition; and if its subject is according to existence in the external world and its predicate likewise, it is called the absolute external proposition. Then they classified accordingly, taking into account privation, affirmation, and negation in the two terms, because of the difference in judgment according to the difference of these considerations in the conversions, and they said: The subject of a universal affirmative is not free from being affirmative, privative, or negative, and in all cases the predicate is one of the three modes. So nine types of propositions result, and each of them has four divisions: for the two terms are either according to reality, or according to external existence, or the subject according to reality and the predicate according to external existence, or the opposite, so thirty-six types of propositions result, differing in concept, even if some entail each other and some differ by generality and particularity.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is a classification of propositions in the context of contraposition (عكس النقيض). The author begins by noting that early and late scholars generally did not detail the types of propositions in contraposition, but a certain group of earlier scholars did. They introduced two fundamental modalities: 'real' (حقيقية) where subject and predicate are taken according to their essence, and 'external' (خارجية) where they are taken according to actual existence in the external world. Then they consider the subject of a universal affirmative proposition, which can be affirmative (محصل), privative (معدول), or negative (سلب). The predicate also takes one of these three. This yields 3 × 3 = 9 types. Then each of these 9 types is further divided by four combinations of modality for subject and predicate: both real, both external, subject real and predicate external, or the opposite. This gives 9 × 4 = 36 distinct propositional concepts. The author notes that while these 36 types differ in meaning, some entail each other and some have partial generality relationships. This classification is foundational for understanding logical relations in immediate inferences.

حقيقية مطلقة
خارجية مطلقة

The Four Types of Affirmative Propositions with Positive Terms
Author details four types of universal affirmative propositions with affirmative subject and predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a universal affirmative proposition into one of the four types (absolute external, external-subject, real-subject, absolute real) and explain the logical relations among them.

Core Idea
Modality of subject and predicate creates four distinct proposition types with subtle entailments.

In Islamic logic, universal affirmative propositions can be nuanced based on whether their terms refer to external existence or essential reality. The author presents four types using the variables ج and ب, each with different logical force. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for valid syllogistic reasoning in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the variable terms
The passage uses ج as subject and ب as predicate. Note that both are affirmative (محصل) terms. Recognize that the same letters appear in all four types, but the modality of each term changes.
Variables are placeholders; in real arguments, they are replaced by actual concepts.
2
Distinguish the four types by modality
Each type is defined by whether the subject and predicate are taken as external (existing in the world) or real (essential, regardless of existence). List the four: (1) both external, (2) external subject + real predicate, (3) real subject + external predicate, (4) both real.
Remember the Arabic names: الخارجية المطلقة, الخارجية الموضوع, الحقيقية الموضوع, الحقيقية المطلقة.
3
Analyze logical relations between types
The passage claims that type 3 (real-subject) is more specific than types 1 and 2, and type 4 (absolute real) is more general than type 3. Check the entailments: does type 3 entail type 1? Does type 4 entail type 3? Understand the directions.
The author also notes potential criticisms, such as temporal mismatch between essential entailment and actual existence.
4
Apply the critique
The author argues that the claimed entailments (e.g., between type 1 and type 2) only hold if the entailment is universal or if existence is constant, which may not be known. Reflect on how this critique affects the reliability of inferences in Islamic sciences.
This critique is important when using these propositions in proofs for theological doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأربعة منها هي أقسام محصلة الطرفين وهما «ج» و «ب» في قولنا كل ج ب، أحدها أن يكون الموضوع والمحمول خارجين وهي الخارجية المطلقة، الثاني أن يكون الموضوع خارجيا والمحمول حقيقياً وهي المسماة بالخارجية الموضوع، وإنه يغاير الأول لما أن المحمول في الأول نفس الباء وفي الثاني الحيثية؛ وهي كونه بحيث لو دخل في الوجود كان «ب» لكنهما وإن كانا متغايرين فهما متلازمان لأنه إذا صدق أن كل (ج) موجود في الخارج ملزوم للباء صدق أن كله «ب» في الخارج، وبالعكس لأنه إذا كان كل «ج» موجود في الخارج صدق أن كل واحد منها بحيث لو دخل في الوجود كان «ب» لكنه إنما يلزم إذا كان استلزام كل واحد من أفراد (ج) لوصف الباء استلزاما كليا أو كان موجودًا في الخارج دائما ولا علم بواحد منهما بل يحتمل أن يكون كذا، وذلك لا يكفي . الثالث أن يكون الموضوع حقيقيا والمحمول خارجيا أي كل ما هو ملزوم للجيم فهو «ب» في الخارج، وهذه هي المسماة بالحقيقية الموضوع. وهذا هو الأخص من الأول والثاني لما أن مفهومه مستلزم لمفهوم كل واحد منهما من غير عكس وفيه ما في الثاني من القدح الذي مر ذكره لجواز أن يكون زمان استلزام كل واحد منهما أو أحدهما للجيم غير زمان وجوده في الخارج واتصافه بالباء، وحينئذ لا يلزم أن يتصف كل واحد منهما بالجيم في الخارج. وأما أنه أخص من الأول والثاني ففيه من الكلام أيضًا. الرابع أن يكون الموضوع والمحمول حقيقيتين أي كل ما هو ملزوم للجيم فهو ملزوم للباء وإنها هي المسماة بالحقيقية المطلقة؛ وهذ هو الأعم من الثالث، وليس بينه وبين الأول والثاني عموم وخصوص.
Four of them are the divisions of the two terms being affirmative, namely 'ج' and 'ب' in our statement 'Every ج is ب'. One of them is that the subject and predicate are both external, which is the absolute external. The second is that the subject is external and the predicate is real, called the external-subject [proposition], and it differs from the first because the predicate in the first is the very 'ب', whereas in the second it is the state of being such that if it entered into existence it would be 'ب'. But although they are different, they are necessarily concomitant because if it is true that every 'ج' exists externally and is a necessary condition for 'ب', then it is true that every 'ج' is 'ب' in the external world, and conversely because if every 'ج' exists externally, then it is true that each one is such that if it entered into existence it would be 'ب'. But this only follows if the entailment of each individual of 'ج' to the description 'ب' is universal, or if it always exists externally, and we have no knowledge of either, rather it is possible that it is so, and that is not sufficient. The third is that the subject is real and the predicate external, i.e., everything that is a necessary condition for 'ج' is 'ب' in the external world, and this is called the real-subject [proposition]. And this is more specific than the first and second because its concept entails the concept of each of them without converse, and it contains the same criticism as the second mentioned above due to the possibility that the time of the entailment of each or one of them to 'ج' is not the time of its existence in the external world and its being described by 'ب', and then it does not follow that each one of them is described by 'ج' in the external world. As for it being more specific than the first and second, there is also discussion about it. The fourth is that the subject and predicate are both real, i.e., everything that is a necessary condition for 'ج' is a necessary condition for 'ب', and it is called the absolute real [proposition]; and this is more general than the third, and between it and the first and second there is no generality and particularity.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage dissects the four subtypes of universal affirmative propositions where both subject and predicate are affirmative (محصل). The author uses the variables 'ج' for subject and 'ب' for predicate. The four types are: (1) absolute external (خارجية مطلقة) where both terms are external, meaning every existing ج is an existing ب. (2) external-subject (خارجية الموضوع), where the subject is external but the predicate is real, meaning every existing ج is such that by its essence it would be ب if it existed. The author notes a subtle difference: in the first type the predicate is the actual ب, while in the second it is the dispositional property of being ب. They are logically equivalent only under certain conditions, which are critiqued. (3) real-subject (حقيقية الموضوع), where the subject is real and the predicate external: everything that is essentially related to ج is an existing ب. This is more specific than the first two. (4) absolute real (حقيقية مطلقة), where both terms are real: everything essentially related to ج is essentially related to ب. This is more general than the third. The author also points out criticisms, such as temporal mismatch between the essential entailment and external existence, which weakens the claimed entailments. This detailed analysis prepares the ground for understanding contraposition in these modal propositions.

Key Terms
محصلة الطرفين
خارجية الموضوع
حقيقية الموضوع
خارجية مطلقة
حقيقية مطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Universal affirmatives vary in logical strength depending on whether their terms are taken as external or real; the real-subject type is the most specific.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition: 'Every act that leads to harm is forbidden.' Classify it into the four types by considering whether 'act that leads to harm' refers to external existent acts or essential acts, and whether 'forbidden' is an external property or an essential ruling. Which type would a usuli typically adopt?

The Four Types of Negated-Subject Propositions
Author applies fourfold division to negated-subject propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four types of negated-subject propositions and explain why the real-subject type is always false.

Core Idea
Negated-subject propositions form a separate system with no direct relation to affirmative-subject propositions.

In the previous lesson, we learned four types of affirmative-subject propositions. Now the author applies the same division to propositions where the subject is a negated term (e.g., 'non-ج'). This classification is crucial for understanding contraposition and conversion in Islamic logic. Pay close attention to the distinction between 'external' and 'real' interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general form
Recognize the notation 'لا ج ب' which means 'Non-ج is ب'. The subject is a privative term (معدول الموضوع).
This is different from a negative proposition where the copula is negated.
2
Understand 'external' vs 'real'
The 'external' interpretation refers to existence in the external world; the 'real' interpretation refers to essential entailment (لزوم).
3
List the four types
From the passage: (1) absolute external, (2) external-subject, (3) real-subject, (4) absolute real. Note the precise Arabic phrasing for each.
4
Spot the always-false type
The third type (real-subject) is said to be 'always false'. Try to understand why: if something essentially requires being non-ج, it cannot simultaneously exist as ب in the external world without contradiction.
The author does not elaborate, but this is a known result.
5
Relate to affirmative-subject types
The four negated-subject types have analogous relations among themselves, but they are mutually exclusive with the affirmative-subject types – no entailment between the two groups.
This is important for contraposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأربعة منها هي أقسام معدولة الموضوع كقولنا لا «ج ب»، أحدها الخارجية المطلقة؛ أي كل لا «ج» في الخارج فهو «ب» في الخارج. والثاني خارجية الموضوع؛ أي كل لا «ج» في الخارج ملزوم للباء. الثالث حقيقية الموضوع؛ أي كل ما هو ملزوم لسلب الجيم فهو «ب» في الخارج، وهذه القضية كاذبة أبدًا. والرابع الحقيقية المطلقة؛ أي كل ما هو ملزوم لسلب الجيم ملزوم للباء وحكم هذه الأربعة فنسبة بعضها إلى البعض حكم الأربعة التي تقدم ذكرها ؛ ولا ملازمة بين هذه وتلك بل بينهما مباينة.
And four of them are the divisions of the negated subject, like our statement 'لا ج ب' (non-ج is ب). One of them is the absolute external: i.e., every non-ج in the external world is ب in the external world. The second is external-subject: i.e., every non-ج in the external world is a necessary condition for ب. The third is real-subject: i.e., everything that is a necessary condition for the negation of ج is ب in the external world, and this proposition is always false. The fourth is absolute real: i.e., everything that is a necessary condition for the negation of ج is a necessary condition for ب. And the ruling of these four, relating some to others, is the same as the ruling of the four mentioned earlier; and there is no entailment between these and those, rather there is mutual exclusion.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage parallels the previous one but for propositions with a negated subject, i.e., where the subject term is privative (معدول) like 'non-ج'. The author again lists four types: (1) absolute external: every existing non-ج is an existing ب. (2) external-subject: every existing non-ج is essentially such that it would be ب. (3) real-subject: everything that essentially necessitates the negation of ج is an existing ب. The author notes that this third type is always false (كاذبة أبدًا) because if something essentially requires being non-ج, it cannot simultaneously be existent and ب in the external world without contradiction? The author does not elaborate why. (4) absolute real: everything that essentially requires being non-ج essentially requires being ب. The author then says that the relationships among these four are analogous to the relationships among the four affirmative types earlier (the first is more general etc.), and importantly, there is no entailment between these negated-subject types and the affirmative-subject types; they are mutually exclusive. This is crucial for contraposition because converting a proposition with a negated subject does not directly relate to the original affirmative form.

Parsing the 24 Types of Universal Categorical Propositions
Author enumerates 24 types of categorical propositions by subject/predicate status and negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical form of any given universal categorical proposition and classify it into one of the 24 types based on subject/predicate status and negation pattern.

Core Idea
Every universal categorical proposition takes one of 24 forms defined by how subject and predicate relate to existence and by negation type.

In classical Islamic logic, universal categorical propositions are not all alike. They are classified according to whether the subject and predicate are considered as existing externally (khariji) or as real concepts (haqiqi), and according to six patterns of negation (affirmative, negated subject, negative subject, negated predicate, negative predicate, negated both). This classification yields 24 distinct propositional forms. Understanding them is crucial for evaluating syllogisms in Islamic sciences, where the truth of a premise often depends on how it is interpreted. In this lesson, we will work through the taxonomy step by step, learning to parse each type and recognize its logical behavior.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three dimensions of classification
The classification uses two dimensions: (1) subject/predicate status: both external (khariji), subject external & predicate external-subject (a mixed form), subject haqiqi & predicate external, both haqiqi; (2) negation pattern: affirmative, negated subject (ma‘dūlat al-mawḍū‘), negative subject (sālibat al-mawḍū‘), negated predicate, negative predicate, and negated both. Each combination gives a unique type.
There are 4 subject/predicate statuses × 6 negation patterns = 24 types.
2
Identify the four subject/predicate statuses
Affirmative types are presented first. The text mentions the third type (haqīqiyya al-mawḍū‘: subject real, predicate external) and fourth type (haqīqiyya muṭlaqa: both real). The first two (absolute external and external-subject) are missing from the beginning but are implied. For each status, the proposition has a specific logical form: e.g., 'everything that is an implicans of J is B in the external world' is haqīqiyya al-mawḍū‘.
The variables J and B stand for any two terms. The 'implicans' (malzūm) refers to the concept that implies the term.
3
Learn the six negation patterns
The author then lists six sets of four types each, corresponding to different negation patterns: [B] ma‘dūlat al-mawḍū‘ (e.g., 'no J is B'), [T] sālibat al-mawḍū‘ (e.g., 'everything that is not J is B'), [Th] ma‘dūlat al-maḥmūl (e.g., 'every J is not B' with negated predicate), [J] sālibat al-maḥmūl (e.g., 'every J is not B' with negative predicate), [H] ma‘dūlat al-mawḍū‘ wa al-maḥmūl (e.g., 'everything that is not J is not B'). For each pattern, the four subject/predicate statuses are applied.
The difference between 'ma‘dūla' and 'sāliba' is subtle: in ma‘dūla, the negation is attached to the term; in sāliba, the negation is on the copula.
4
Parse a specific type from the Arabic
Take the example from [T]: 'absolute external' of the negative-subject type: 'kullu mā laysa J fī al-khārij B fī al-khārij' ('everything that is not J in the external world is B in the external world'). The subject is 'kullu mā laysa J fī al-khārij' (everything that is not J externally), predicate 'B fī al-khārij'. The type is 'khārijiyya muṭlaqa' but with negative subject. The author notes this is always false because it includes all non-existents.
Always check if the subject is qualified by 'fī al-khārij' (external) or is a real concept (malzūm).
5
Practice identifying relations of generality
The author often compares types in terms of 'a‘amm' (more general) and 'akhass' (more specific). For instance, haqīqiyya muṭlaqa is more general than haqīqiyya al-mawḍū‘. Also, some types are 'kādhiba abadan' (always false) because their subject includes impossible things. Recognize these relations to understand logical implications.
These relations help in choosing the correct premise for a syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملزوم للباء صدق أن كله «ب» في الخارج، وبالعكس لأنه إذا كان كل «ج» موجود في الخارج صدق أن كل واحد منها بحيث لو دخل في الوجود كان «ب» لكنه إنما يلزم إذا كان استلزام كل واحد من أفراد (ج) لوصف الباء استلزاما كليا أو كان موجودًا في الخارج دائما ولا علم بواحد منهما بل يحتمل أن يكون كذا، وذلك لا يكفي . الثالث أن يكون الموضوع حقيقيا والمحمول خارجيا أي كل ما هو ملزوم للجيم فهو «ب» في الخارج، وهذه هي المسماة بالحقيقية الموضوع. وهذا هو الأخص من الأول والثاني لما أن مفهومه مستلزم لمفهوم كل واحد منهما من غير عكس وفيه ما في الثاني من القدح الذي مر ذكره لجواز أن يكون زمان استلزام كل واحد منهما أو أحدهما للجيم غير زمان وجوده في الخارج واتصافه بالباء، وحينئذ لا يلزم أن يتصف كل واحد منهما بالجيم في الخارج. وأما أنه أخص من الأول والثاني ففيه من الكلام أيضًا. الرابع أن يكون الموضوع والمحمول حقيقيتين أي كل ما هو ملزوم للجيم فهو ملزوم للباء وإنها هي المسماة بالحقيقية المطلقة؛ وهذ هو الأعم من الثالث، وليس بينه وبين الأول والثاني عموم وخصوص. [ب] وأربعة منها هي أقسام معدولة الموضوع كقولنا لا «ج ب»، أحدها الخارجية المطلقة؛ أي كل لا «ج» في الخارج فهو «ب» في الخارج. والثاني خارجية الموضوع؛ أي كل لا «ج» في الخارج ملزوم للباء. الثالث حقيقية الموضوع؛ أي كل ما هو ملزوم لسلب الجيم فهو «ب» في الخارج، وهذه القضية كاذبة أبدًا. والرابع الحقيقية المطلقة؛ أي كل ما هو ملزوم لسلب الجيم ملزوم للباء وحكم هذه الأربعة فنسبة بعضها إلى البعض حكم الأربعة التي تقدم ذكرها ؛ ولا ملازمة بين هذه وتلك بل بينهما مباينة. [ت] وأربعة منها هي أقسام سالبة الموضوع كقولنا كل ما ليس «ج ب»، أحدها خارجية مطلقة كقولنا كل ما ليس (ج) في الخارج (ب) في الخارج، وهذه أخص من نظيرة من معدولة الموضوع؛ وهذه القضية كاذبة أبدا لاندراج جميع المعدومات في موضوعها. والثاني خارجية الموضوع كقولنا كل ما ليس «ج» في الخارج ملزوم للباء، وإنه أعم من الأول ولا يمتنع صدقه لعدم توقف الحيثية على الوجود الخارجي الثالث حقيقية الموضوع كقولنا كل ما ليس بملزوم للجيم «ب» في الخارج وهذه أعم من الأول لأن ما لم يكن ملزوما للجيم لم يكن «ج» في الخارج وإلا لكان بحيث إذا وجد كان «ج» لمطلق الاتصال ولا معنى للحيثية إلا ذلك؛ ولا ينعكس لجواز أن لا يكون (ج) في الخارج مع الحيثية، وهذه القضية مما يباين الثانية. والرابع الحقيقية المطلقة أي كل ما ليس بملزوم للجيم فهو ملزوم للباء، وهذه أعم من الثالثة بحسب المحمول لأن محمولها أعم من محمول الثالثة، ومن الثانية بحسب الموضوع، ومن الأولى بحسب الموضوع والمحمول لأن موضوعها أخص ومحمولها أعم. [ث] وأربعة منها هي أقسام معدولة المحمول وهي: الخارجية المطلقة كقولنا كل «ج» في الخارج لا «ب» في الخارج، والخارجية الموضوع والحقيقية الموضوع، والحقيقية المطلقة. [ج] وأربعة منها هي أقسام السالبة المحمول وهي: الخارجية المطلقة كقولنا كل «ج» في الخارج فهو ليس «ب» في الخارج، والخارجية الموضوع، والحقيقية الموضوع، والحقيقية المطلقة. [ح] وأربعة [منها] هي أقسام معدولة الموضوع والمحمول وهي: الخارجية المطلقة كقولنا كل لا «ج» في الخارج فهو لا «ب» في الخارج والخارجية الموضوع، والحقيقية الموضوع، والحقيقية المطلقة.
Necessarily implying the predicate (B) is true that all of it is B in the external world, and vice versa because if every J exists in the external world, then it is true that every one of them is such that if it entered existence it would be B, but that follows only if the implication of each individual of J for the description B is universal or it exists in the external world always, and there is no knowledge of either, rather it is possible that it is so, and that is not sufficient. Third: that the subject be real and the predicate external, i.e., ‘everything that is an implicans of J is B in the external world’, and this is called the real-subject proposition. This is more specific than the first and the second because its concept implies the concept of each of them without the converse, and it contains the objection mentioned in the second regarding the possibility that the time of implication of each of them or one of them for J is not the time of its existence in the external world and its being described by B, and then it does not follow that each of them is described by J in the external world. As for its being more specific than the first and the second, there is discussion also. Fourth: that the subject and predicate both be real, i.e., ‘everything that is an implicans of J is an implicans of B’, and this is called the absolute real proposition; this is more general than the third, and there is no generality and specificity between it and the first and the second. [B] And four of them are the divisions of the negated-subject proposition, like our saying ‘no J is B’: first, the absolute external, i.e., ‘everything that is not J in the external world is B in the external world’. Second, the external-subject, i.e., ‘everything that is not J in the external world is an implicans of B’. Third, the real-subject, i.e., ‘everything that is an implicans of the negation of J is B in the external world’, and this proposition is always false. Fourth, the absolute real, i.e., ‘everything that is an implicans of the negation of J is an implicans of B’, and the rule of these four is that the relation of some to others is like the rule of the four mentioned earlier; there is no implication between these and those, rather they are mutually exclusive. [T] And four of them are the divisions of the negative-subject proposition, like our saying ‘everything that is not J is B’: first, the absolute external, like our saying ‘everything that is not J in the external world is B in the external world’, and this is more specific than its counterpart in the negated-subject; this proposition is always false because all non-existents are included in its subject. Second, the external-subject, like our saying ‘everything that is not J in the external world is an implicans of B’, and it is more general than the first and its truth is not impossible because the aspect does not depend on external existence. Third, the real-subject, like our saying ‘everything that is not an implicans of J is B in the external world’, and this is more general than the first because what is not an implicans of J is not J in the external world, otherwise it would be such that if it existed it would be J because of absolute connection, and there is no meaning of aspect except that; and it does not convert because it is possible that it is not J in the external world together with the aspect, and this proposition is mutually exclusive with the second. Fourth, the absolute real, i.e., ‘everything that is not an implicans of J is an implicans of B’, and this is more general than the third in respect of the predicate because its predicate is more general than the predicate of the third, and more general than the second in respect of the subject, and more general than the first in respect of subject and predicate because its subject is more specific and its predicate is more general. [Th] And four of them are the divisions of the negated-predicate proposition, namely: the absolute external, like our saying ‘every J in the external world is not B in the external world’, and the external-subject, and the real-subject, and the absolute real. [J] And four of them are the divisions of the negative-predicate proposition, namely: the absolute external, like our saying ‘every J in the external world is not B in the external world’, and the external-subject, and the real-subject, and the absolute real. [H] And four of them are the divisions of the negated-subject-and-predicate proposition, namely: the absolute external, like our saying ‘everything that is not J in the external world is not B in the external world’, and the external-subject, and the real-subject, and the absolute real.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of universal categorical propositions. The author first discusses four types of affirmative propositions based on whether the subject and predicate are considered as existing externally (khariji) or as real concepts (haqiqi). The text begins mid-argument, critiquing a condition about implication. Then it presents the third type: subject real, predicate external (haqiqiyya al-mawdu‘), which is more specific than the first two. The fourth type, both real (haqiqiyya mutlaqa), is more general than the third. Next, the author introduces six sets of four propositions each, distinguished by negation types. The first set [ب] covers propositions with a negated subject (ma‘dulat al-mawdu‘), such as 'no J is B'. These are further divided into absolute external, external-subject, real-subject, and absolute real. The real-subject type is always false. The second set [ت] covers propositions with a negative subject (salibat al-mawdu‘), like 'everything that is not J is B'. The absolute external type here is always false because it includes all non-existents. The author compares each type to earlier ones. The third [ث], fourth [ج], and fifth [ح] sets deal with negated predicate, negative predicate, and both negated subject and predicate respectively, each with four subcategories. Throughout, the author emphasizes the relations of generality and specificity among these types. This taxonomy is essential for understanding the logical behavior of propositions in syllogisms.

Propositions

10 min
The 36-Fold Classification of Categorical Propositions
Enumeration of the 36 proposition types with examples and logical relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition into its specific type among the 36, identifying the subject-predicate status and modality.

Core Idea
36 proposition types emerge from six negation/privation forms crossed with four modalities.

In post-Avicennan logic, categorical propositions are classified into 36 types based on two dimensions: the status of the subject and predicate (affirmative, privative, or negative) and the modality (external absolute, external subject, real subject, real absolute). This elaborate system is essential for accurate logical operations like conversion, which are foundational in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the two classification axes
The 36 types arise from two factors: (1) the status of the subject and predicate as affirmative (muhassal), privative (ma‘dūl), or negative (sālib), and (2) the modality: absolute external (khārijiyya muṭlaqa), external subject (khārijiyya al-mawḍū‘), real subject (ḥaqīqiyya al-mawḍū‘), and absolute real (ḥaqīqiyya muṭlaqa).
Modalities differ in how they consider the existence and essence of the subject.
2
List the subject-predicate forms mentioned
The passage enumerates several groups: negated subject (sālibat al-mawḍū‘), privative predicate (ma‘dūlat al-maḥmūl), negated predicate (sālibat al-maḥmūl), privative both (ma‘dūlat al-mawḍū‘ wa al-maḥmūl), negated both sides (sālibat al-ṭarafayn), privative subject with negated predicate, and negated subject with privative predicate. Each group represents a distinct combination of negation/privation on subject and predicate.
The basic affirmative (muhassal subject and predicate) is not listed here but is implied as a separate group.
3
Examine the first group (sālibat al-mawḍū‘) in detail
The four sub-types under sālibat al-mawḍū‘ are: absolute external (always false because subject includes all non-existents), external subject (can be true, conditional on external existence), real subject (more general, based on essence), and absolute real (most general). Note the logical relationships of generality and specificity among them.
Example: 'Everything that is not J in the external world is B in the external world' is absolute external and always false.
4
Generalize the pattern to other groups
Each of the other subject-predicate forms (e.g., ma‘dūlat al-maḥmūl) also has the same four modalities, yielding 36 types (6 forms × 4 modalities = 24, plus the basic affirmative with its 4 modalities makes 36). Practice by picking another form and generating its four sub-types.
The author uses the same four sub-types for each group: absolute external, external subject, real subject, absolute real.
5
Connect to conversion ('aks al-naqīḍ)
The purpose of this classification is to understand 'aks al-naqīḍ (conversion of the contradictory). Knowing the exact type of a proposition is necessary to determine which conversions are valid. For example, the absolute external sālibat al-mawḍū‘ cannot be simply converted because it is false, while the real subject version may allow conversion.
This logic is used in juristic debates to ensure correct inference from one ruling to another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] وأربعة منها هي أقسام سالبة الموضوع كقولنا كل ما ليس «ج ب»، أحدها خارجية مطلقة كقولنا كل ما ليس (ج) في الخارج (ب) في الخارج، وهذه أخص من نظيرة من معدولة الموضوع؛ وهذه القضية كاذبة أبدا لاندراج جميع المعدومات في موضوعها. والثاني خارجية الموضوع كقولنا كل ما ليس «ج» في الخارج ملزوم للباء، وإنه أعم من الأول ولا يمتنع صدقه لعدم توقف الحيثية على الوجود الخارجي الثالث حقيقية الموضوع كقولنا كل ما ليس بملزوم للجيم «ب» في الخارج وهذه أعم من الأول لأن ما لم يكن ملزوما للجيم لم يكن «ج» في الخارج وإلا لكان بحيث إذا وجد كان «ج» لمطلق الاتصال ولا معنى للحيثية إلا ذلك؛ ولا ينعكس لجواز أن لا يكون (ج) في الخارج مع الحيثية، وهذه القضية مما يباين الثانية. والرابع الحقيقية المطلقة أي كل ما ليس بملزوم / للجيم فهو ملزوم للباء، وهذه أعم من الثالثة بحسب المحمول [١٣٩ظ] لأن محمولها أعم من محمول الثالثة، ومن الثانية بحسب الموضوع، ومن الأولى بحسب الموضوع والمحمول لأن موضوعها أخص ومحمولها أعم. [ث] وأربعة منها هي أقسام معدولة المحمول وهي: الخارجية المطلقة كقولنا كل «ج» في الخارج لا «ب» في الخارج، والخارجية الموضوع والحقيقية الموضوع، والحقيقية المطلقة. [ج] وأربعة منها هي أقسام السالبة المحمول وهي: الخارجية المطلقة كقولنا كل «ج» في الخارج فهو ليس «ب» في الخارج، والخارجية الموضوع، والحقيقية الموضوع، والحقيقية المطلقة. [ح] وأربعة [منها] هي أقسام معدولة الموضوع والمحمول وهي: الخارجية المطلقة كقولنا كل لا «ج» في الخارج فهو لا «ب» في الخارج والخارجية الموضوع، والحقيقية الموضوع، والحقيقية المطلقة. [وأربعة هي أقسام سالبة الطرفين وهما الموضوع والمحمول وهي: الخارجية المطلقة كقولنا كل ما ليس «ج» في الخارج فهو ليس «ب» في الخارج، والخارجية الموضوع، والحقيقية الموضوع، والحقيقية المطلقة كقولنا كل ما ليس بملزوم للجيم ليس بملزوم للباء. [د] وأربعة هي أقسام معدولة الموضوع سالبة المحمول؛ [ذ] وأربعة هي أقسام سالبة الموضوع معدولة المحمول بالتفصيل الذي عرفته. فكان الكل ستة وثلاثين فهو خلاصة ما قالوه في بيان هذه الأقسام على اعتقاد أن الاطلاع على حقائق هذه الأقسام، وسبيله إلى معرفة ما يكون من اللازم معرفته في عكس النقيض. فإن من اطلع عليها فقد اطلع على المشاركات بينها؛ والمتباينات كذلك. وكذلك على العمومية والخصوصية وغير ذلك.
[T] And four of them are the divisions of the negated subject, like our saying 'Everything that is not J is B'. The first is the absolute external, like our saying 'Everything that is not J in the external world is B in the external world', and this is more specific than its counterpart from the privative subject; and this proposition is always false because all non-existents are included in its subject. The second is the external subject, like our saying 'Everything that is not J in the external world is necessarily B', and it is more general than the first and its truth is not impossible because the conditional aspect does not depend on external existence. The third is the real subject, like our saying 'Everything that is not necessarily J is B in the external world', and this is more general than the first because whatever is not necessarily J is not J in the external world, otherwise it would be such that if it existed it would be J due to absolute connection, and the conditional aspect means nothing else; and it does not convert because it is possible that something is not J in the external world while having the conditional aspect. And this proposition is distinct from the second. The fourth is the absolute real, i.e., 'Everything that is not necessarily J is necessarily B', and this is more general than the third with respect to the predicate because its predicate is more general than the predicate of the third, and more general than the second with respect to the subject, and more general than the first with respect to both subject and predicate because its subject is more specific and its predicate is more general. [Th] And four of them are the divisions of the privative predicate: the absolute external like our saying 'Every J in the external world is not B in the external world', and the external subject, and the real subject, and the absolute real. [J] And four of them are the divisions of the negated predicate: the absolute external like our saying 'Every J in the external world is such that it is not B in the external world', and the external subject, and the real subject, and the absolute real. [H] And four of them are the divisions of the privative subject and predicate: the absolute external like our saying 'Every not J in the external world is not B in the external world', and the external subject, and the real subject, and the absolute real. [And four are the divisions of the negated both sides, i.e., the subject and predicate: the absolute external like our saying 'Everything that is not J in the external world is such that it is not B in the external world', and the external subject, and the real subject, and the absolute real like our saying 'Everything that is not necessarily J is not necessarily B'. [D] And four are the divisions of the privative subject and negated predicate; [Dh] And four are the divisions of the negated subject and privative predicate, according to the detail you have learned. So the total is thirty-six. This is the essence of what they said in explaining these divisions, believing that understanding the realities of these divisions and the way to it is to know what is necessary to know about conversion of the contradictory. For whoever understands them has understood the commonalities between them and the differences as well, and also the generality and specificity and so on.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of types of categorical propositions based on the status of the subject and predicate as affirmative (muhassal), privative (ma'dul), or negative (salb), combined with four 'modalities' relating to the reality of the terms. The author lists six groups, each containing four sub-types, for a total of 36. The six groups are: (ت) negated subject, (ث) privative predicate, (ج) negated predicate, (ح) privative both subject and predicate, (?) negated both sides (subject and predicate), (د) privative subject and negated predicate, (ذ) negated subject and privative predicate. Within each group, the four sub-types are: absolute external (mutlaqa kharijiyya), external subject (kharijiyya al-mawdu'), real subject (haqiqiyya al-mawdu'), and absolute real (haqiqiyya mutlaqa). The author gives examples using placeholder letters and explains the relationships of generality and specificity among the sub-types. For instance, in the first group (salibat al-mawdu'), the absolute external is always false because it includes all non-existents; the external subject is more general and can be true; the real subject is more general still; and the absolute real is the most general. The purpose of this enumeration, the author says, is to aid in understanding the conversion of the contradictory ('aks al-naqid), as knowing the commonalities and differences among these propositions is essential for logical reasoning.

Key Terms
سالبة الموضوع
معدولة المحمول
خارجية مطلقة
حقيقية الموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The 36 proposition types are generated by combining six subject-predicate negation/privation patterns with four modalities, and this classification is essential for understanding logical relations like conversion.

Critique of Dividing the Subject into Muhassal, Ma'dul, and Salb
Author critiques the validity of dividing the subject into muhassal, ma'dul, and salb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two arguments used by the majority of logicians to reject the tripartite division of the subject in a proposition, and apply this critique to determine whether a given term is muhassal, ma'dul, or reducible.

Core Idea
Negation (salb) is not a third type of term—only muhassal and ma'dul are genuine categories.

In the previous lesson, we encountered an elaborate division of propositions based on whether the subject is muhassal (affirmative), ma'dul (privative), or salb (negation). The author now reports that the majority of logicians (al-jumhur) reject this tripartite division. This passage presents their two main arguments. Understanding this critique is essential for correctly classifying terms in propositions and avoiding a common error in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Reconstruct the tripartite division
Identify the three categories the author is criticizing: muhassal (affirmative term), ma'dul (privative term), and salb (negation). These were used to classify the subject of a proposition.
This division is part of the previous enumeration of 36 proposition types.
2
Analyze the first argument against salb
The author argues that salb (negation) as such cannot be a subject or predicate because it is not a concept but a relation of denial. When we say 'negation is general', the subject is actually a muhassal concept (the idea of negation), not negation itself.
The first argument is that negation is not a standalone concept that can occupy term positions.
3
Analyze the second argument: the negative particle
In expressions like 'every not-J', the negative particle 'not' (or its equivalent in Arabic) is either included in the subject (making it ma'dul) or excluded (making it muhassal). There is no third option, so 'salb' as a separate category is impossible.
This shows that the tripartite division collapses into a binary one.
4
Conclude the correct classification
The majority view holds that only muhassal and ma'dul are genuine term types. Any term that appears as negation must be reinterpreted as either muhassal or ma'dul.
This conclusion undermines the six groups of propositions that assumed a salb category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أن هذه القسمة لا تكون صحيحة عند الجمهور منهم لما أنه لا يصح أن يقال موضوع القضية إما أن يكون محصلا أو معدولاً أو سلبًا: أما أولا فلأن السلب من حيث هو السلب لا يصح لكونه موضوعا ولا لكونه محمولاً كذلك. وإن كان موضوعًا أو محمولاً بمعنى كذا فذلك المعنى لا يكون سلبا بل هو معنى يمكن أن يعبر عنه بعبارة يفهم منه ذلك المعنى من غير تأويل. ولا يقال كيف وقد صح قولهم إن السلب عام يعم سلب هذا وذلك وذلك، فإن الموضوع في مثل هذا القول لا يكون سلبا بل هو مفهوم محصل يكون السلب عبارة عن ذلك المفهوم. وأما ثانيًا فلان حرف السلب في قولهم كل ما ليس (ج)» إما أن يكون داخلا في الموضوع، وحينئذ يكون الموضوع معدولا، وإما أن لا يكون داخلا فيه، وحينئذ يكون الموضوع محصلا؛ إذ لا واسطة بين أن يكون داخلا وبين أن لا يكون. وكذلك بين أن يكون معدولا
However, this division is not correct according to the majority of them, because it is not valid to say that the subject of a proposition is either muhassal, or ma'dul, or salb. First, because negation (salb) as such is not valid to be a subject nor a predicate. And if it is a subject or predicate in a certain sense, then that sense is not negation, but rather a concept that can be expressed by an expression from which that concept is understood without interpretation. And it cannot be said: How is it that their saying 'negation is general, encompassing the negation of this, that, and so on' is valid? For the subject in such a statement is not negation, but rather a muhassal concept, and negation is a description of that concept. Second, because the particle of negation in their saying 'Everything that is not J' is either included in the subject, in which case the subject is ma'dul, or it is not included, in which case the subject is muhassal; for there is no intermediary between being included and not being included. And likewise between being ma'dul and being muhassal.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the previous enumeration of 36 proposition types. The author states that the majority of logicians (al-jumhur) do not accept the division of the subject into three categories: muhassal (affirmative term), ma'dul (privative term), and salb (negation). Two main arguments are given. First, negation (salb) itself cannot serve as a subject or predicate because it is a relation of denial, not a concept. When we say 'every not-J', the subject is actually a concept (the privative 'non-J'), not pure negation. The phrase 'non-J' expresses a concept that is understood without the need for interpretation. Even statements like 'negation is general' have a muhassal subject; the word 'negation' refers to a concept. Second, the negative particle in expressions like 'every not-J' is either part of the subject (making it ma'dul) or not part of it (making it muhassal). There is no third option; hence 'salb' as a category is invalid. Thus the six groups that assume a 'salb' category are not accepted by the majority, undermining the 36-fold division.

Key Terms
الجمهور (al-jumhūr): The majority of logicians, i.e., those who reject the division of subject into muhassal, ma'dul, and salb. [Note: In different contexts, al-jumhur may refer to the majority of scholars in a particular field.]
محصل (muḥaṣṣal): An affirmative term that denotes a positive concept, e.g., 'human' or 'J'. [Note: Stable term across the tradition, contrasted with ma'dul.]
معدول (ma‘dūl): A privative term that includes a negation within the concept itself, e.g., 'non-human' or 'not-J'. [Note: In some texts, ma'dul is considered a type of muhassal in a broad sense, but here it is distinct.]
سلب (salb): Negation, as a logical operation; here it is argued that it cannot be a concept occupying the subject or predicate position without being interpreted as a muhassal. [Note: In other contexts, salb is sometimes used interchangeably with 'adam (privation), but here it is distinguished from 'adl.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority critique, why is the division of the subject into muhassal, ma'dul, and salb invalid?
A) Because salb cannot be a subject or predicate; it is not a concept.
B) Because there are only two types: muhassal and ma'dul.
C) Both A and B are correct.
D) Because the majority did not understand the division.

Key Takeaway
The tripartite division of the subject into muhassal, ma'dul, and salb is invalid because salb cannot be a subject/predicate, and the negative particle is either part of the subject (making it ma'dul) or not (making it muhassal), leaving no third category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition from fiqh: 'Every non-muslim is ritually impure.' Identify whether the subject term is muhassal, ma'dul, or salb, and justify using the two arguments from the passage.
?
Test the tripartite division on the phrase 'negation of adultery is required.' Can 'negation of adultery' function as a subject? Explain using the first argument and determine its correct classification (muhassal or ma'dul).

Why Negation Cannot Be a Subject Type
Author critiques the inclusion of 'negative' as a subject type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negation cannot serve as a subject or predicate in categorical propositions and show how this reduces possible subject types from three to two.

Core Idea
Negation is an operator, not a term – it cannot be a subject.

The proponents of the 36-fold division assume that the subject of a proposition can be actual, modified, or negative. The author immediately rejects the third category. In this lesson, you will understand his reasoning and see how the binary nature of inclusion versus non-inclusion forces a simpler classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three claimed subject types
The proponents claim subjects can be actual (محصل), modified (معدول), or negative (سلب).
2
Understand the first objection: negation as such
The author argues that negation insofar as it is negation (السلب من حيث هو سلب) cannot be a subject because it is a logical operator, not a concept that can be quantified. If it is considered a concept, then it is no longer negation but a positive notion.
The subject must be a term with content.
3
Analyze the second objection: the binary of inclusion
In a proposition like 'كل ما ليس ج ب', the negation particle 'ليس' is either part of the subject (making it modified) or not (making it actual). There is no third option; hence only two subject types exist.
This eliminates the 'negative' subject category.
4
Conclude the reduction to 16 types
With 2 subject types and 2 predicate types (actual/modified), and each having 4 divisions (external/real, uniform/converse), the total becomes 2×2×4=16 types.
This matches the view of the majority of logicians.
