اللوازم معرفته والمستعمل بطريق الحقيقة لا يفتقر في الإفادة إلى التأويل
And four are the divisions of negative both sides, which are the subject and the predicate: they are the absolute external, like our saying 'Everything that is not J in external existence is not B in external existence'; and the external subject and the real subject and the absolute real, like our saying 'Everything that is not a necessary concomitant of Jim is not a necessary concomitant of Ba.' And four are the divisions of modified subject with negative predicate; and four are the divisions of negative subject with modified predicate in the detail you have known. So the total is thirty-six. That is the summary of what they said in explaining these divisions, believing that examining the realities of these divisions and its path leads to knowledge of what is necessary to know in contraposition. For whoever examines them has examined the commonalities between them and the differences as well, and likewise universality and particularity and other things. However, this division is not valid according to the majority of them because it is not correct to say that the subject of a proposition is either actual, modified, or negative: As for first, because negation insofar as it is negation is not valid as a subject nor as a predicate as well. And if it is a subject or a predicate in such-and-such sense, that sense is not negation but rather a meaning that can be expressed by an expression from which that meaning is understood without interpretation. And it is not said how, since their statement that negation is general, encompassing the negation of this, that, and that, is valid. For the subject in such a statement is not negation but rather an actual concept of which negation is an expression of that concept. And as for second, because the particle of negation in their statement 'Everything that is not J' is either included in the subject, in which case the subject is modified, or is not included in it, in which case the subject is actual; for there is no intermediate between being included and not being included. And likewise between being modified and not being modified as the majority of the earlier and later scholars held. And if it were so, the result would be four types of propositions, each of which is divided into four divisions according to what is in reality and what is in external existence, uniformly or conversely as has passed, so the total would be sixteen. That is the result of multiplying four by four, and this is in accordance with what the majority encountered in the division of propositions contrary to that. And as for third, because their statement that the predicate here is the 'aspect' (haythiyya) is also in the position of rejection because the aspect is not suitable as a predicate insofar as it is the aspect, but rather it is said that what is intended by it is such-and-such; and that is necessarily other than the aspect, otherwise it would be in the realm of prevention. So the judgment upon it of being a predicate, even if interpretation is valid, does not escape corruption. And as for fourth, because they interpreted our saying 'Every J is B' when its subject is according to reality and its predicate likewise by their saying 'Everything that, if it came into existence, would be J, is such that, if it came into existence, would be B'; and its meaning was 'Everything that is a necessary concomitant of Jim is a necessary concomitant of Ba.' In this interpretation there is discussion: for a necessary concomitant of a thing must be other than that thing. So the subject and predicate in our saying 'Every J is B' are either J and B, in which case the jim and ba are related to each other, or they are the necessary concomitant of Jim and the necessary concomitant of Ba, in which case the mention of Jim and Ba is a pointless redundancy devoid of benefit. And it is known that it is not so; rather the subject in our saying 'Every J is B' is nothing but J, and the predicate is nothing but B. Therefore our saying 'Every J is B' and 'Every B is A' yields our saying 'Every J is A'. And if it were as they mentioned, it would yield nothing at all, neither this nor other, because the middle term would not be unified; and this is evident. And as for fifth, because using words in the sciences in a literal way specifically in this matter; for the purpose is to define what is necessary to know, and what is used in a literal way does not require interpretation for conveying meaning.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a proposed division of categorical propositions into 36 types. The author first summarizes the proponents' division: they divide propositions based on subject and predicate being actual, modified, or negative, and also based on external and real existence, leading to 36 types. The author then launches five objections (أولا, ثانيا, ثالثا, رابعا, خامسا) to demonstrate the invalidity of this division.

First objection: Negation as such cannot serve as a subject or predicate because negation is a logical operator, not a concept that can be quantified. If one interprets it as a concept, then it is no longer negation but a positive notion.

Second objection: In a proposition like 'كل ما ليس ج ب' , the negation particle is either part of the subject (making it a modified subject) or not (making it an actual subject). There is no third option. Thus only two types of subjects (actual and modified) are possible, not three. Consequently, the total number of proposition types reduces from 36 to 16 (4 types of subjects × 4 types of predicates, considering reality and external existence). This is what the majority of scholars accept.

Third objection: The proponents' use of 'حيثية' (aspect) as a predicate is invalid because 'aspect' itself is not a real predicate; it must be interpreted as something else, which introduces corruption.

Fourth objection: The proponents interpret 'كل ج ب' as 'كل ما لو دخل في الوجود كان ج فهو بحيث لو دخل في الوجود كان ب', meaning 'everything that is a necessary concomitant of J is a necessary concomitant of B'. This interpretation fails because the necessary concomitant of a thing must be other than the thing. Thus either the subject and predicate are J and B themselves, or they are their concomitants. In the latter case, mentioning J and B becomes pointless. Moreover, this interpretation undermines syllogistic validity: the standard syllogism 'كل ج ب, كل ب أ => كل ج أ' would not work because the middle term (B) would not be unified (since the interpretation introduces concomitant relations).

Fifth objection: Using terms like 'حيثية' in a non-literal sense requires interpretation, but the sciences aim to use words literally to avoid ambiguity. The proponents' reliance on interpretation defeats the purpose.

Throughout, the author uses logical variables ج, ب, أ as placeholders for terms, following the standard mantiq practice. The critique is aimed at logicians who overcomplicate propositional divisions.

Key Takeaway
Negation cannot be a term; it only modifies terms, so the subject of a proposition can only be actual or modified, not negatively bare.

The 'Aspect' Predicate and the Syllogistic Breakdown
Author critiques 'حيثية' predicate and concomitant interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'حيثية' (aspect) is not a valid predicate and why the proponents' interpretation of universal propositions as relations between concomitants undermines syllogistic validity.

Core Idea
Literal terms are essential; introducing aspects or concomitants breaks the syllogistic machinery.

The proponents attempt to use 'حيثية' (aspect) as a predicate and reinterpret 'كل ج ب' in terms of necessary concomitants. The author shows both moves are logically flawed. This lesson dissects these errors and their consequences for the validity of syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proponents' predicate 'حيثية'
They claim the predicate in propositions like 'كل ج ب' is 'حيثية' (insofar as it is). This means the predicate is not a straightforward term but an abstraction.
2
Author's third objection: 'حيثية' is not a genuine predicate
The author argues that 'حيثية' as such is not a real predicate; it requires interpretation into something else. That something else is not the same as 'حيثية', so the predicate is corrupted.
Literal usage is crucial in scientific discourse.
3
Examine the proponents' reinterpretation of 'كل ج ب'
They interpret 'كل ج ب' as 'كل ما لو دخل في الوجود كان ج فهو بحيث لو دخل في الوجود كان ب', meaning 'Everything that is a necessary concomitant of J is a necessary concomitant of B'.
This changes the subject and predicate to concomitants, not the terms themselves.
4
Identify the syllogistic consequence
If the subject and predicate are concomitants, then in a syllogism like 'كل ج ب, كل ب أ => كل ج أ', the middle term 'ب' in the first premise is a concomitant of J, while in the second premise it is a concomitant of something else, so the middle term is not unified. The syllogism fails.
This shows the interpretation is not merely odd but logically destructive.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a proposed division of categorical propositions into 36 types. The author first summarizes the proponents' division: they divide propositions based on subject and predicate being actual, modified, or negative, and also based on external and real existence, leading to 36 types. The author then launches five objections (أولا, ثانيا, ثالثا, رابعا, خامسا) to demonstrate the invalidity of this division.

First objection: Negation as such cannot serve as a subject or predicate because negation is a logical operator, not a concept that can be quantified. If one interprets it as a concept, then it is no longer negation but a positive notion.

Second objection: In a proposition like 'كل ما ليس ج ب' , the negation particle is either part of the subject (making it a modified subject) or not (making it an actual subject). There is no third option. Thus only two types of subjects (actual and modified) are possible, not three. Consequently, the total number of proposition types reduces from 36 to 16 (4 types of subjects × 4 types of predicates, considering reality and external existence). This is what the majority of scholars accept.

Third objection: The proponents' use of 'حيثية' (aspect) as a predicate is invalid because 'aspect' itself is not a real predicate; it must be interpreted as something else, which introduces corruption.

Fourth objection: The proponents interpret 'كل ج ب' as 'كل ما لو دخل في الوجود كان ج فهو بحيث لو دخل في الوجود كان ب', meaning 'everything that is a necessary concomitant of J is a necessary concomitant of B'. This interpretation fails because the necessary concomitant of a thing must be other than the thing. Thus either the subject and predicate are J and B themselves, or they are their concomitants. In the latter case, mentioning J and B becomes pointless. Moreover, this interpretation undermines syllogistic validity: the standard syllogism 'كل ج ب, كل ب أ => كل ج أ' would not work because the middle term (B) would not be unified (since the interpretation introduces concomitant relations).

Fifth objection: Using terms like 'حيثية' in a non-literal sense requires interpretation, but the sciences aim to use words literally to avoid ambiguity. The proponents' reliance on interpretation defeats the purpose.

Throughout, the author uses logical variables ج, ب, أ as placeholders for terms, following the standard mantiq practice. The critique is aimed at logicians who overcomplicate propositional divisions.

Distinguishing Implication from Predication
Author responds to four objections about possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between predication (haml) and logical implication (luzum) and identify when they are conflated in a logical argument.

Core Idea
Predication does not entail necessary coexistence.

This passage addresses a common confusion in Islamic logic: the difference between simply predicating one thing of another and the stronger relation of necessary implication. The author responds to four objections concerning the definition of 'possible by general possibility' and concludes by drawing a sharp line between haml and luzum. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating arguments in fiqh, kalam, and usul al-fiqh where implication is often assumed from predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four objections
The passage lists four objections (طُعُون) against the earlier proofs regarding possibility. Read the Arabic carefully and locate each one: 1) prevention of the antecedent (منع الملزوم), 2) the claim that 'general' possibility is too broad, 3) an example about an impossible predicate, 4) the definition of possibility as 'not impossible'.
The author responds to each in order.
2
Analyze the author's responses
For each objection, note how the author refutes it. For the first, he says it was already established. For the second, he says 'general' means it is one of the divisions. For the third, he argues the example's predicate cannot exist externally. For the fourth, he upholds the definition of possibility as 'not impossible'.
3
Extract the core distinction
The passage concludes with a clear separation: from the perspective of implication (luzum), Imam al-Kasi's view is correct—if A implies B, then non-existence of B implies non-existence of A. But from the perspective of predication (haml), simply predicating B of A does not mean B is a necessary concomitant of A.
This is the key logical point.
4
Apply the distinction to a new example
Consider the proposition 'The world is temporally originated' (العالم حادث). Does 'temporally originated' imply that the world cannot exist without being temporally originated? Actually, it is a predication of a property, but the world could exist without being temporally originated? No, because if the world is temporally originated, it is a necessary concomitant? This is debatable. But the lesson is that predication alone does not automatically entail necessary implication.
Use this to test your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يصدق معه بعض ما ليس بضروري الوجود ليس بالإمكان العام؛ وقد كان المعنى من الممكن بالإمكان العام ما ليس بضروري مطلقا : فهذه خلاصة طعنهم في الحجج التي مرَّ ذكرها . وقد يقال في الطعن الأول وهو منع الملزوم إن ذلك من جملة ما تقدم ذكره وتقرر ؛ وفي الثاني أنه مدفوع بالعناية كما إذا قيل يعنى بكونه أعم أنه من جملة ما ينقسم إليه وإلى غيره ؛ وفي الثالث وهو المثال المذكور أن الكلام فيما يمكن وجوده في الخارج، والمحمول فيما ذكرتم وهو أحدهما لا على التعيين فلا يمكن أن يوجد في الخارج أصلا، ولو أمكن وجوده لكان الكلام فيه كما في الغير؛ فلا فائدة في التعرُّض لذلك المثال؛ وفي الرابع أن الممكن بالإمكان العام عبارة عما لا يكون ممتنعا عند الجمهور من المتقدمين والمتأخرين، ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون كما ذكرتم. وبالجملة فما ذهب إليه الإمام الكسي فهو حق باعتبار لزوم الشيء للشيء في كل صورة من هذه الصور؛ والشيء لا يكون من لوازم الغير إلا وأن يكون عدم الغير من لوازمه. فأما باعتبار حمل الشيء على الشيء ففيه من الكلام؛ وقد مرّ من قبل أنه لا يلزم من حمل الشيء على الشيء أنه من لوازم ذلك الشيء على معنى أنه لا يمكن وجوده دون وجود ذلك، ولا عدم ذلك دون عدمه.
With it, some of what is not necessary of existence is not true by general possibility; and the meaning of possible by general possibility was what is not absolutely necessary: this is the summary of their objection to the proofs that were mentioned. And it may be said regarding the first objection, which is prevention of the antecedent, that it is part of what was previously mentioned and established; and regarding the second, that it is refuted by consideration, as when it is said 'by being more general it is part of what is divided into it and other than it'; and regarding the third, which is the mentioned example, that the discussion concerns what can exist externally, and the predicate in what you mentioned, which is one of them without specification, cannot exist externally at all, and if its existence were possible, the discussion about it would be as with the other; so there is no benefit in addressing that example; and regarding the fourth, that the possible by general possibility is an expression for what is not impossible according to the majority of the earlier and later scholars, and if it were as such, it cannot be as you mentioned. In summary, what Imam al-Kasi adhered to is true from the perspective of the implication of a thing for a thing in each of these forms; and a thing is not among the necessary concomitants of another unless the non-existence of the other is among its necessary concomitants. As for from the perspective of predicating a thing of a thing, there is discussion in it; and it has passed before that from predicating a thing of a thing it does not follow that it is among the necessary concomitants of that thing in the sense that its existence cannot be without the existence of that, nor its non-existence without its non-existence.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage refines the concept of logical implication (luzum) in relation to possibility. The author begins by summarizing an objection concerning the definition of 'possible by general possibility' (al-mumkin bil-imkan al-amm). The objectors had argued that some things that are not necessary of existence are not possible in the general sense. The author then responds to four specific objections (al-tu'un). First, the objection of 'prevention of the antecedent' (man' al-malzum) is dismissed as already established. Second, the objection that possibility is 'general' is refuted by noting that 'general' here means it is one of the divisions. Third, an example involving something that cannot exist externally is rejected because the predicate cannot be specified. Fourth, the definition of possibility as 'what is not impossible' is upheld, so the objection fails. The author concludes that Imam al-Kasi's view is correct regarding implication (luzum) but not regarding predication (haml). The final statement distinguishes predication from implication: predicating one thing of another does not mean the predicate is a necessary concomitant of the subject, such that they cannot exist apart. This is a key distinction in Islamic logic, often misunderstood. The examples used are abstract, but the point is clear: logical necessity is not the same as ontological dependence.

اللزوم
الحمل
منع الملزوم

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view regarding the relationship between predication (haml) and implication (luzum)?
A) Predication always implies necessary coexistence.
B) Implication is identical to predication.
C) Predication does not entail that the predicate is a necessary concomitant of the subject.
D) The third objection was about the impossibility of external existence for the predicate.

Key Takeaway
Predication (haml) should not be conflated with necessary implication (luzum); the latter requires that the non-existence of one entails the non-existence of the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'The world is contingent (mumkin)'. Determine whether 'contingent' is a necessary concomitant of the world (i.e., does the world's non-existence imply non-contingency?) or is it merely a predication? Justify your answer using the distinction from the passage.
?
Find another example from Islamic sciences (e.g., 'All prophets are infallible') and test whether predication entails implication. Write a short analysis.

Propositions

7 min
Reductio Proofs for Universal Propositions Across Modal Types
Author presents reductio proofs for universal propositions in mutlaqa, kharijiyya, and haqiqiyya forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof for universal affirmative propositions in al-mutlaqa al-amma, al-kharijiyya, and al-haqiqiyya forms.

Core Idea
Every universal proposition, regardless of modality, can be proven by reductio ad absurdum with appropriate conditioning.

In this passage, the author demonstrates how the same essential proof structure — a reductio ad absurdum — applies to universal propositions across different modal types: the general absolute (mutlaqa amma), the external (kharijiyya), and the real (haqiqiyya). The key difference lies in how the subject is conditioned to match the modality. Understanding this unity and variation is crucial for mastering syllogistic theory in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof structure
Notice that the author uses a reductio: assume the contradictory of the universal, combine it with the original universal, and derive an impossible consequence (a contradiction).
2
Trace the reductio for mutlaqa amma
Assume 'something is not B always but is C always'. With 'every C is B', we infer 'some non-B is B' — a direct contradiction.
This shows the original universal must be true.
3
Observe the modality adjustment
For other modal types (e.g., necessity, possibility), the proof is identical except that the subject condition is modified to reflect the modality, such as conditioning by necessity for a possibility proposition.
The author stresses: 'the demonstration does not differ except in the condition of the subject'.
4
Analyze the haqiqiyya case
For the absolute real proposition, the reductio assumes something that is not always implied by B but is B in the external world, leading to the impossibility of both being and not being implied by B.
5
Apply to kharijiyya and haqiqiyya with negated subject
The same method works for propositions with negated subjects (e.g., 'every non-B is C'), yielding universal permanent propositions. The proof again uses a reductio with appropriate subject conditioning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شيء مما هو ليس ب دائما في الخارج بل ج دائما في الخارج وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض ما ليس ب دائما في الخارج ج في الخارج بالإطلاق العام؛ وقد كان كل ج في الخارج فهو ب في الخارج. فينتج بعض ما ليس ب دائما في الخارج ب بالإطلاق العام، هذا خلف. ثم إنه في المطلقة العامة ولا يختلف البرهان فيما عداها إلا في قيد الموضوع فإنه يجب أن يقيد في كل قضية بما يناقض جهتها نحو الضرورة في الممكنة العامة؛ وبالعكس. وإذا كانت حقيقية مطلقة يلزمها كلية دائمة حقيقية الموضوع لكونها أعم من الأولى ولتمام البرهان. فإن نقيضها مع الأصل ينتج أن بعض ما ليس بملزوم للباء دائما فهو ب في الخارج، وذلك محال؛ لأنه إذا كان ب في الخارج كان موجودا في الخارج موصوفا به فيكون ملزوما للباء بالإطلاق وإنه ليس بملزوم له دائما، هذا خلف. ولأنه إذا صدق الأصل صدق أن كل ج ملزوم للباء ويلزمه هذه القضية من غير هذا التكلُّف.
وأما من أقسام معدولة الموضوع فالخارجية يلزمها كلية دائمة خارجية مطلقة لما أن الأصل مع النقيض ينتج صدق قولنا بعض ما هو لا ب دائمًا مما وجد في الخارج فهو ب بالإطلاق العام، وذلك محال. وللحقيقية كلية دائمة حقيقية الموضوع لما أن الأصل مع النقيض ينتج صدق قولنا بعض ما هو ملزوم لسلب الباء دائما فهو ب في الخارج، وإنه محال أيضًا لأنه يجب وجوده في الخارج حتى يثبت له الباء. وإنه ينبغي عنه دائما، هذا خلف.
A thing that is not B always in the external world but is C always in the external world; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is: some things that are not B always in the external world are C in the external world with absolute universality; and indeed every C in the external world is B in the external world. So it follows that some things that are not B always in the external world are B with absolute universality; this is a contradiction. Then concerning the general absolute proposition, the demonstration does not differ except in the condition of the subject, for it must be conditioned in every proposition by what contradicts its modality, like necessity in the general possible, and vice versa. And if it is an absolute real proposition, it entails a universal permanent real subject because it is more general than the first and for the completeness of the demonstration. For its contradictory together with the original yields that some things that are not always implied by B are B in the external world, and that is impossible; because if it is B in the external world, then it exists in the external world described by it, so it is implied by B absolutely, while it is not implied by it always; this is a contradiction. And because if the original is true, then 'every C is implied by B' is true, and this proposition follows from it without this difficulty. As for the types with a negated subject, the external proposition entails a universal permanent external absolute, because the original together with the contradictory yields the truth of our statement: 'some things that are not B always among those existing in the external world are B with absolute universality', and that is impossible. And for the real proposition, a universal permanent real subject, because the original together with the contradictory yields the truth of our statement: 'some things that are implied by the negation of B always are B in the external world', and it is also impossible because it must exist in the external world in order for B to be predicated of it. And it should always be [negated?] about it, this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic demonstration analyzing the logical properties of universal affirmative propositions across different modal types. The author begins with a reductio ad absurdum proof for the 'general absolute' (mutlaqa amma) proposition. He assumes a thing that is not always B but is always C, then derives a contradiction to show that the original universal must hold. He then notes that the proof structure is similar for other modal types, but requires adjusting the subject condition according to the modality (e.g., necessity for possibility). Next, he treats the 'absolute real' (haqiqiyya mutlaqa) proposition, showing that it entails a universal permanent proposition with a real subject. The proof again uses contradiction: assuming the original and its contradictory leads to an impossible state where something is both B and not B in a certain way. Finally, he discusses propositions with negated subjects: the 'external' (kharijiyya) and 'real' (haqiqiyya) types, each entailing a universal permanent proposition. For each, the original together with the contradictory yields an impossible conclusion, thus establishing the necessity of the initial universal. The author carefully distinguishes the conditions for each type, emphasizing that the demonstrations differ only in the subject's modality.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة (al-mutlaqa al-amma): A general absolute proposition, i.e., a universal affirmative without any modal condition (e.g., 'Every C is B' simpliciter). [Note: Stable term in post-Avicennan logic; sometimes contrasted with 'daima' (permanent).]
خارجية (kharijiyya): An 'external' proposition whose subject refers to actual existents in the external world (e.g., 'Every existing thing is B'). [Note: Distinct from 'haqiqiyya'; in earlier texts, may blend with 'haqiqiyya'.]
حقيقية (haqiqiyya): A 'real' proposition whose subject considers the essence regardless of existence (e.g., 'Every possible C is B'). [Note: Stable; sometimes called 'mahmula' in other schools.]
ملزوم (malzum): The subject of a predicate; literally 'that which is followed by' (here, the thing having attribute B). [Note: In usul al-fiqh, malzum means the antecedent in an implication; here it is used for the subject of a categorical proposition.]
عكس النقيض (aks al-naqid): Contraposition: converting a proposition by negating both subject and predicate, and swapping them (e.g., 'Every C is B' becomes 'Every non-B is non-C'). [Note: Standard term in Aristotelian logic; some later logicians restrict it to categoricals only.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the reductio proof for the general absolute proposition 'Every human is mortal', what contradictory assumption would lead to a contradiction?
A) Assume there is something that is not mortal always but is human always, combined with 'Every human is mortal'.
B) Assume there is something that is not human always but is mortal always, combined with 'Every human is mortal'.
C) Assume there is something that is both human and mortal always, combined with 'Every human is mortal'.
D) Assume there is something that is neither human nor mortal always, combined with 'Every human is mortal'.

Key Takeaway
The reductio proof for universal propositions adapts to modal types by conditioning the subject, but the core logical structure remains constant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio proof for the universal affirmative proposition 'Every wine is intoxicating' (as a mutlaqa amma) using the method shown. Then adjust the proof for a haqiqiyya version (e.g., 'Every potential wine is intoxicating') by appropriately conditioning the subject.
